Talaq — Divorce
beginner
8 min
Oaths Conditioned on Birth: Stillbirth and Intercourse
Rulings on oaths about birth and intercourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce and manumission triggered by birth or intercourse, and distinguish the effect of stillbirth.

Core Idea
The occurrence of the event triggers the oath, even if the child is stillborn.

Oaths often hinge on future events like childbirth or intercourse. This lesson examines the Hanafi position: the mere happening of the condition, not its perfection, suffices. We break down the key cases to see how the ruling is applied uniformly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
Read each statement and identify the precise condition: e.g., 'when you give birth' or 'if I have intercourse'.
2
Identify the Consequence
What is the promised result? Divorce, freedom, or something else?
3
Check if Condition is Fully Met
Did the event occur as stated? For birth, note that stillbirth is still birth.
4
State the Hanafi Rule
The consequence applies upon occurrence, regardless of additional qualities.
This is consistent across all birth-related oaths.
5
Distinguish from Potential Exceptions
Are there any exceptions? The text shows none for these cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في العتق والطلاق
بیوی سے کہا جب تو بچہ جنے تجھے طلاق اس نے مرا ہوا بچہ جنا طلاق ہو جائے گی
کسی نے کہا اپنی باندی سے کہ تو جب بچہ جنے تو وہ آزاد ہے، اس نے بچہ جنا آزاد ہو جائے گا
پہلا وہ غلام جسے میں خریدوں وہ آزاد ہے، غلام خریدا آزاد ہو جائے گا آخری غلام جسے میں خریدوں وہ آزاد ہے، یہ کہہ کر مولی مرگیا ، اب کیا ہوگا ؟
وہ آخری عورت جس سے میں نکاح کروں اسے تین طلاق نہیں ، اس کا حکم
آقا نے کہا کہ ہر وہ غلام جو مجھے فلاں بیوی کے ہاں ولادت کی خوشخبری دے وہ آزاد ہے، تین غلاموں نے اسے علیحدہ علیحدہ خوشخبری دی ، پہلے والا آزاد ہو جائیگا
اگر کسی نے کہا ان اشتریث فلانا فهو حر ، پھر کفارہ قسم کی طرف سے خریدا، یہ کفارہ درست نہیں ہے
اپنی ام ولد کو کفارہ کی نیت سے خریدا تو یہ کفارہ درست نہیں
کسی نے کہا اگر جاریہ سے ہمبستری کروں تو وہ آزاد ہے، پھر ہمبستری کی ، وہ آزاد ہو جائے گی
کسی نے کہا كل مملوك لي حق تو اس میں مکاتب بغیر نیت کے شامل نہ ہوگا
اپنی بیویوں کو کہا کہ اس کو طلاق ہے یا اس کو اور اس کو طلاق ہے ، آخری والی کو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی
Chapter on oaths regarding manumission and divorce. He said to his wife, 'When you give birth to a child, you are divorced.' She gave birth to a dead child: divorce occurs. Someone said to his female slave, 'When you give birth to a child, you are free.' She gave birth: she becomes free. 'The first slave I buy is free.' He bought a slave: he becomes free. 'The last slave I buy is free.' He said this and then the master died: what happens now? 'That last woman I marry, I divorce her three times' – its ruling. The master said, 'Every slave who gives me the good news of the birth from so-and-so wife is free.' Three slaves gave him the news separately: the first one becomes free. If someone said, 'If I buy so-and-so, he is free,' then he bought him for the kaffarah of an oath: this kaffarah is not valid. He bought his own umm walad with the intention of kaffarah: this kaffarah is not valid. Someone said, 'If I have intercourse with a slave girl, she is free.' Then he had intercourse: she becomes free. Someone said, 'Every slave that I own is free' – the mukatib is not included without intention. He said to his wives, 'So-and-so is divorced, or so-and-so is divorced,' the last one suffers divorce.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This chapter covers oaths involving manumission (freeing slaves) and divorce. The first ruling: if a husband says to his wife, 'When you give birth, you are divorced,' and she gives birth to a stillborn child, divorce still occurs because the birth happened, regardless of whether the child was alive. Similarly, if a master says to his female slave, 'When you give birth, you are free,' birth triggers freedom even if the child is dead. The second set: if a man says 'the first slave I buy is free,' and he buys a slave, that slave becomes free immediately upon purchase. If he says 'the last slave I buy is free' and then dies before buying another, the statement is considered a general oath; since he died, the condition cannot be fulfilled, so the oath is invalid? Actually the text asks 'what happens?' – the implied answer is that the oath is considered canceled because the condition cannot be met after death; but the text leaves it open. The ruling on 'the last woman I marry is divorced three times' – that is a conditional divorce that takes effect when he marries that woman. The passage then addresses a case where a master promises freedom to any slave who brings news of a birth; if multiple slaves bring the same news separately, only the first one is freed because the condition was fulfilled once. Important: if someone buys a slave for kaffarah of an oath (i.e., to free as expiation), and he had previously said 'if I buy so-and-so, he is free,' the same slave cannot be used for kaffarah because the slave becomes free by the previous oath at the moment of purchase, so the purchase itself is not valid as kaffarah. Similarly, buying one's own umm walad (a female slave with a child from the master) for kaffarah is invalid because she is already in a special status. The last part: if one says 'every slave I own is free,' the mukatib (a slave who has a contract of manumission) is not included unless explicitly intended, because he is already on the path to freedom. And if a man says to his wives 'you are divorced, or you are divorced,' pointing at them one by one, only the last one indicated is divorced because the word 'or' implies choice, and the last chosen takes effect.

Key Terms
عتق (ʿitq): Manumission; freeing a slave.
مکاتب (mukātib): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) to buy his freedom.
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, acquiring special status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says to his female slave, 'When you give birth, you are free.' She gives birth to a dead child. What is the ruling?
A) She becomes free because birth occurred.
B) She does not become free because the child was dead.
C) The master's intention matters.
D) The oath is void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, conditional oaths are fulfilled by the mere occurrence of the condition, without requiring perfection.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Oaths on Purchase and Kaffarah
Rulings on conditional oaths tied to buying slaves and their effect on kaffarah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules when a conditional oath on purchase intersects with kaffarah (expiation).

Core Idea
A slave already freed by a prior conditional oath cannot be used for kaffarah.

When a man makes an oath about buying slaves—like 'the first slave I buy is free'—and later buys a slave with the intention of using him for kaffarah, complications arise. This lesson explains why such a purchase is invalid for kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conditional Statement
Read the phrase: 'The first slave I buy is free.' Understand that it is a conditional oath linking purchase to freedom.
2
Determine the Trigger Event
The purchase itself triggers the freedom immediately upon buying.
3
Examine the Kaffarah Scenario
If someone later buys that same slave with the intention of freeing as expiation, the slave is already free by the earlier oath.
Thus the purchase cannot count as kaffarah.
4
Apply the Rule to Umm Walad
Buying one's own umm walad for kaffarah is also invalid because she has a special status.
5
Conclude the Ruling
The purchase is not valid for kaffarah; the person must find another slave or alternative expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في العتق والطلاق
بیوی سے کہا جب تو بچہ جنے تجھے طلاق اس نے مرا ہوا بچہ جنا طلاق ہو جائے گی
کسی نے کہا اپنی باندی سے کہ تو جب بچہ جنے تو وہ آزاد ہے، اس نے بچہ جنا آزاد ہو جائے گا

ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, acquiring special status.

Checkpoint
A man says, 'If I buy this slave, he is free.' He later buys the slave intending to free him as kaffarah. What is the ruling?
A) The slave is free, but kaffarah is not valid.
B) The slave is not free because the intention was for kaffarah.
C) The kaffarah is valid because the intention overrides.
D) The purchase is void.

Key Takeaway
A conditional oath on purchase takes effect immediately upon the trigger, and it cannot be repurposed for kaffarah.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Oaths on Good News, General Statements, and 'Or' Divorce
Rulings on multiple informants, general statements, and disjunctive oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for multiple informants, general ownership statements, and disjunctive oaths involving 'or'.

Core Idea
Only the first fulfiller of a condition gets the reward; general ownership excludes mukatib; 'or' picks the last mentioned.

Oaths can involve multiple people fulfilling the same condition. This lesson covers who gets freed when several slaves bring the same news, what happens when a master says 'every slave I own is free', and how to interpret 'or' in divorce oaths.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the Good News Case
The master offers freedom to the first slave who brings news. Three slaves bring the same news separately: only the first is freed.
The condition is 'the first to give news'.
2
Examine General Ownership
'Every slave I own is free.' Does it include the mukatib? Not without intention.
Mukatib is on the path to freedom.
3
Interpret the 'Or' Divorce
The husband says 'You are divorced, or you are divorced' to his wives. The last one indicated is divorced.
'Or' indicates choice; the final name takes effect.
4
Apply the Rulings
Understand the logic: specificity and chronology matter in conditional oaths.
5
Note Exceptions
No exceptions mentioned, but intention can broaden or narrow the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في العتق والطلاق

Checkpoint
A master says, 'Every slave who gives me good news of my son's birth is free.' Three slaves bring the news separately. How many are freed?
A) All three
B) Only the first
C) Only the last
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, conditional oaths apply to the first fulfillment of the condition; general ownership statements exclude slaves with partial freedom; and 'or' in divorce oaths designates the last named.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Mahr Reduction in Ambiguous Divorce: Imam Muhammad vs. the Sahibayn
Comparing ambiguous divorce to ambiguous manumission regarding mahr reduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad's position on mahr reduction in ambiguous divorce before bayan and distinguish it from the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
In ambiguous divorce before clarification, mahr reduction differs per wife according to Imam Muhammad, while the Sahibayn hold equal reduction.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the reduction of mahr (dower) when a husband ambiguously divorces one of three wives and dies before specifying which one. The ruling is derived by analogy to a case of ambiguous manumission of slaves. Imam Muhammad proposes different fractions for each wife, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf advocate a uniform reduction. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies how Hanafi jurists handle ambiguity and the importance of bayan (clarification).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Three wives, all non-consummated. Husband says 'one of you is divorced' ambiguously, then dies before clarifying which wife he intended.
The mahr is not yet fully fixed because consummation did not occur; it reduces upon divorce.
2
State Imam Muhammad's position
According to Imam Muhammad, the mahr reduction differs per wife: the wife who was 'to go out' (the one intended for divorce) loses 1/4, the wife 'who stays' (the middle) loses 3/8, and the wife 'who enters' (the one not intended) loses 1/8.
These fractions mirror the manumission analogy where the freed slave pays a different portion.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that each wife loses exactly one quarter of her mahr, making the reduction equal for all three.
They see no basis for differentiation; the ambiguity affects all wives equally.
4
Understand the basis of the difference
Imam Muhammad analogizes to the manumission case where three slaves are partially freed and each pays a different ransom. The Sahibayn reject this analogy and treat the divorces as equally probable, hence equal reduction.
The school's internal method of qiyas leads to divergent results.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The amount each wife actually receives from the estate differs. Under Muhammad's view, the wife who was 'to enter' retains 7/8 of her mahr, while under the Sahibayn each retains 3/4.
This affects estate distribution and the rights of the wives.

وَلَوْ كَانَ هَذَا فِي الطَّلَاقِ وَهُنَّ غَيْرُ مَدْ خُوْلَاتٍ وَمَاتَ الزَّوْجُ قَبْلَ الْبَيَانِ سَقَطَ مِنْ مَهْرِ الْخَارِجَةِ رُبْعُهُ وَمِنْ مَهْرِ النَّابِيَّةِ خَلَقَةُ الْمَائِهِ وَمِنْ مَهْرِ الدَّاخِلَةِ ثُمُنْهُ قِيلَ هَذَا قَوْلُ مُحَمَّدٍ خَاصَّةٌ وَعِنْدَ هُمَا يَسْقُطُ رُبْعُهُ وَقِيلَ هُوَ قَوْلُهُمَا أَيْضًا وَقَدْ ذَكَرْنَا الْفَرْقَ وَتَمَامُ تَفْرِيْعَا تِهَا فِي الزِيَادَاتِ
And if this were in the case of divorce, and they are non-consummated, and the husband dies before clarification, then from the mahr of the one who was to go out, one quarter drops; from the mahr of the one who stays, three eighths; and from the mahr of the one who enters, one eighth. It is said: This is specifically the view of Muhammad. And according to them (the two), one quarter drops. And it is said: It is also their view. And we have mentioned the difference and the complete exposition of its subdivisions in al-Ziyadat.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the reduction of mahr when a husband ambiguously divorces one of three wives and dies before clarifying. The analogy is drawn from the preceding discussion on manumission of three slaves. Imam Muhammad holds that the reduction differs per wife: the one who was to leave (like the absent slave) loses a quarter, the one who stays (like the present slave) loses three eighths, and the one who enters loses an eighth. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two) hold that only a quarter drops from each wife's mahr. The author refers to al-Ziyadat for the full exposition.

Key Terms
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce; the dissolution of marriage.
المهر (al-mahr): Dower; the payment given by the husband to the wife upon marriage.
البيان (al-bayan): Clarification; the husband's statement specifying which wife is divorced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad in this passage, how much of her mahr does the wife who was to 'stay' (النابية) lose?
A) 1/4
B) 3/8
C) 1/8
D) Depends on the situation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has internal difference on mahr reduction in ambiguous divorce: Imam Muhammad's view differentiates per wife, while the Sahibayn hold equal reduction.

Oath of Manumission: Pregnant Slave and Male Child
Rule on oath of manumission: the status of a male child born to a pregnant slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of the Hanafi school when an oath of manumission includes 'every male slave' and the owner's pregnant slave gives birth to a male child.

Core Idea
A male child born to a pregnant slave is not freed by an oath covering 'every male slave' because it is not an independent mamlūk at the time of oath.

This lesson examines a specific case in oaths of manumission. The key issue is whether a fetus or newborn child qualifies as a 'male slave' under an oath made before its birth. The Hanafi ruling is that it does not, based on the definition of mamlūk as a complete independent person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Subject
The act is an oath of manumission: the owner declares 'Every male slave of mine is free.' The subject is the male child born to his pregnant female slave. Determine whether the child falls within the scope of the oath.
The oath uses 'kullu mamlūkin lī dhakarin' — every male slave of mine.
2
Define 'Mamlūk Dhakar'
In Hanafi law, a 'mamlūk' is an independent person owned as property. A fetus is not a separate legal entity; it is part of the mother. Thus, the unborn child is not a 'male slave' at the time of the oath.
The definition excludes body parts and dependents like fetuses.
3
Apply the Definition to the Scenario
Even after birth, the child was not an independent mamlūk when the oath was made. The oath only covers existing independent male slaves. Since the child did not exist as a separate person at that moment, he is not freed.
This holds regardless of whether conception occurred before or after the oath.
4
State the Ruling
The child is not freed. He remains a slave following the mother's status.
The owner must make a separate oath or explicit declaration to free the child.
5
Note the Rationale and Precedent
The ruling is based on linguistic precision and legal principle. The same logic applies in sales: selling the unborn child alone is invalid.
This ruling is recorded in Al-Hidayah and is the standard Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ: كُلُّ مَمْلُوكٍ لِي ذَكَرٍ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ، وَلَهُ جَارِيَةٌ حَامِلٌ فَوَلَدَتْ ذَكَرًا لَمْ يَعْتِقْ.
And whoever says: 'Every male slave of mine is free,' and he has a pregnant female slave, and she gives birth to a male, he is not freed.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn of Al-Hidayah concerning oaths of manumission. The ruling states that if a person makes an oath that every male slave he owns is free, and he owns a pregnant female slave who later gives birth to a male child, that child is not included in the oath and therefore not freed. The reason is that the phrase 'every male slave' refers to an existing, independent male slave. A fetus in the womb is not a fully independent male slave at the time of the oath. Even if the birth occurs within six months (proving conception before the oath) or after more than six months (making conception uncertain), the child is not freed. The child is legally considered an extension of the mother (tabaʿan), not an independent mamlūk. This is why selling the unborn child alone is not permissible. The author emphasizes that the word 'mamlūk' includes only a complete person, not a limb or dependent being.

Key Terms
مَمْلُوك (mamlūk): A slave owned as property; a complete independent person in legal ownership.
حَمْل (ḥaml): Fetus or unborn child in the womb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says 'Every male slave of mine is free' and his pregnant female slave gives birth to a male child one month later, is the child freed?
A) Yes, because the child is male and owned.
B) Yes, because the oath includes all future males.
C) No, because the child is not an independent mamlūk at the time of oath.
D) No, because the child was not born yet.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath of manumission referring to 'every male slave' does not include a male child born to a pregnant female slave, because the child is not an independent mamlūk at the time of the oath.

Future-Oriented Oaths: Tadbir and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Rulings on manumission oaths tied to future events and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings for 'after tomorrow' and 'after my death' manumission oaths, state the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) to determine which slaves are freed.

Core Idea
An oath tied to death carries both manumission and bequest, including future slaves; 'after tomorrow' is only present-tense.

This passage examines two scenarios of manumission oaths linked to future events. The first, 'after tomorrow,' restricts manumission to slaves owned at the time of the oath because the present tense ('amlikuhu') applies only to current property. The second, 'after my death,' triggers a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) on one side and Imam Abu Yusuf on the other: does it also cover slaves acquired later? Understanding the dual nature of the death-linked oath is key to grasping the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two temporal scenarios
Identify the difference between an oath tied to a specific future time ('after tomorrow') and one tied to the occurrence of death ('after my death'). The latter introduces a bequest element.
The 'after tomorrow' case is unambiguous; the dispute only arises in the death-linked case.
2
State the rule for 'after tomorrow'
From the passage: only slaves owned at the time of the oath are freed when tomorrow arrives. Newly acquired slaves are not included because 'amlikuhu' is a reality for the present.
There is no ikhtilaf on this point.
3
Present the intra-Hanafi dispute for 'after my death'
Abu Yusuf (in al-Nawadir) argues that only the present slave becomes mudabbar and freed upon death; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that both present and future slaves are freed from one-third.
The key is whether the statement is purely manumission or also a bequest.
4
Analyze the reasoning of each side
Abu Yusuf relies on the present-tense reality of the phrase. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad emphasize the dual nature: immediate tadbir (applying to present slave) and a bequest (which can cover future property).
The bequest aspect is derived from the condition 'when I die' (mawt).
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah). In practice, if the owner dies, both slaves (owned at the time of the oath and later acquired) are freed from the one-third of the estate.
This affects estate planning and the rights of other heirs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ: كُلُّ مَمْلُوكٍ أَمْلِكُهُ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ بَعْدَ غَدٍ، وَقَالَ: كُلُّ مَمْلُوكٍ لِي فَهُوَ حُرٌّ بَعْدَ غَدٍ، وَلَهُ مَمْلُوكٌ فَاشْتَرَى آخَرَ، ثُمَّ جَاءَ بَعْدَ غَدٍ، عَتَقَ الَّذِي فِي مِلْكِهِ يَوْمَ حَلَفَ؛ لِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ أَمْلِكُهُ لِلْحَالِ حَقِيقَةٌ، فَلَا يَتَنَاوَلُ مَا يَشْتَرِيهِ بَعْدَ الْيَمِينِ. وَلَوْ قَالَ: كُلُّ مَمْلُوكٍ أَمْلِكُهُ أَوْ قَالَ: كُلُّ مَمْلُوكٍ لِي حُرٌّ بَعْدَ مَوْتِي، وَلَهُ مَمْلُوكٌ فَاشْتَرَى مَمْلُوكًا آخَرَ، فَالَّذِي كَانَ عِنْدَهُ وَقْتَ الْيَمِينِ مُدَبَّرٌ، وَالْآخَرُ لَيْسَ بِمُدَبَّرٍ، وَإِنْ مَاتَ عَتَقَا مِنَ الثُّلُثِ. وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ فِي النَّوَادِرِ: يَعْتِقُ مَا كَانَ فِي مِلْكِهِ يَوْمَ حَلَفَ وَلَا يَعْتِقُ مَا اسْتَفَادَ بَعْدَ يَمِينِهِ. وَعَلَى هَذَا إِذَا قَالَ: كُلُّ مَمْلُوكٍ لِي إِذَا مِتُّ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ؛ لَهُ أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ حَقِيقَةٌ لِلْحَالِ فَلَا يَعْتِقُ بِهِ مَا سَيَمْلِكُهُ. وَلَهُمَا (أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ) أَنَّ هَذَا إِيجَابُ عِتْقٍ وَإِيصَاءٍ حَتَّى اعْتُبِرَ مِنَ الثُّلُثِ، وَفِي الْوَصَايَا تُعْتَبَرُ الْحَالَةُ الْمُنْتَظَرَةُ وَالْحَالَةُ الرَّاهِنَةُ. فَمِنْ حَيْثُ أَنَّهُ إِيجَابُ الْعِتْقِ يَتَنَاوَلُ الْعَبْدَ الْمَمْلُوكَ اعْتِبَارًا لِلْحَالَةِ الرَّاهِنَةِ فَيَصِيرُ مُدَبَّرًا، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ أَنَّهُ إِيصَاءٌ يَتَنَاوَلُ الَّذِي يَشْتَرِيهِ اعْتِبَارًا لِلْحَالَةِ الْمُتَرَبِّصَةِ وَهِيَ حَالَةُ الْمَوْتِ، فَإِذَا مَاتَ عَتَقَا مِنَ الثُّلُثِ.
And if he said: 'Every slave that I own is free after tomorrow,' and he said: 'Every slave of mine is free after tomorrow,' and he had one slave, then he bought another, then tomorrow came: the slave in his ownership on the day of the oath is freed; because his statement 'amlikuhu' (I own) is a reality for the present, so it does not include what he buys after the oath. And if he said: 'Every slave that I own' or he said: 'Every slave of mine is free after my death,' and he had one slave, then he bought another slave: the one with him at the time of the oath becomes mudabbar, and the other is not mudabbar; and if he dies, both are freed from one-third. And Abu Yusuf said in al-Nawadir: He frees what was in his ownership on the day he swore, and he does not free what he acquired after his oath. And the same if he said: 'Every slave of mine, when I die, is free'; his proof is that the word is a reality for the present, so what he will own is not freed by it. And the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) argue that this is an enforcement of manumission and a bequest, such that it is considered from one-third; and in bequests, the anticipated state and the present state are considered. So from the aspect that it is an enforcement of manumission, it includes the owned slave considering the present state, so he becomes mudabbar; and from the aspect that it is a bequest, it includes the one he will buy considering the anticipated state, which is the state of death; so when he dies, both are freed from one-third.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage addresses two related scenarios. First, if someone says 'Every slave I own is free after tomorrow' or 'Every slave of mine is free after tomorrow', and he buys another slave after the oath, only the slave owned at the time of the oath is freed when tomorrow arrives. The second slave is not included because the word 'amlikuhu' (I own) refers to present ownership; it does not extend to future acquisitions without a context indicating future. Second, if the oath is tied to death ('after my death'), there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) on one side and Abu Yusuf on the other. According to Abu Yusuf (recorded in al-Nawadir), the same rule applies: only the present slave becomes mudabbar (a tadbir arrangement) and the future slave is not included. However, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that such an oath has a dual nature: it is both an immediate manumission commitment (ijāb al-‘itq) and a bequest (īṣā’). As a manumission commitment, it applies to the present slave, making him a mudabbar (which prevents his sale). As a bequest, it applies to future acquisitions as well, because a will can include property acquired after the will is made. Therefore, on death, both slaves are freed, but limited to one-third of the estate. This reasoning distinguishes it from the 'after tomorrow' case, which is purely a single action (manumission) without a bequest element. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is more comprehensive.

Key Terms
مُدَبَّر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the owner's death through a tadbir declaration.
إِيصَاء (īṣā’): A bequest or will (wasiyyah) regarding manumission after death.
أَمْلِكُهُ (amlikuhu): I own him (present tense indicating current possession).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling for a person who says 'Every slave I own is free after my death' and later acquires a new slave?
A) Only the slave owned at the time of the declaration becomes mudabbar and is freed.
B) Both the slave owned at the time and the later acquired slave are freed from one-third upon death.
C) The later acquired slave is freed immediately, not at death.
D) Neither slave is freed because the declaration is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A manumission oath tied to death (tadbir) includes slaves acquired after the oath due to its bequest element, unlike a time-specific oath like 'after tomorrow'.

Denial of Lineage for Umm al-Walad's Child: External vs Religious Obligation
Explains effect of denial and different transmissions within Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the external ruling (qada'an) and religious obligation (diyanatan) when a master denies paternity of his Umm al-Walad's child, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the effectiveness of denial.

Core Idea
A master can deny paternity of his Umm al-Walad's child externally, but religiously he must acknowledge if intercourse was without withdrawal.

This passage addresses the denial of lineage for a child born to an Umm al-Walad (slave-concubine who bore her master's child). It distinguishes between the external judicial ruling (hukm) and the religious conscientiousness (diyanatan). Furthermore, it notes internal differences within the Hanafi school, with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad having other narrations on the matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two legal domains
The act is the master's denial of paternity. The text distinguishes between the external ruling (hukm) and religious duty (diyanatan).
External ruling is enforceable in court; diyanatan is the obligation before Allah.
2
Understand the basis for the external ruling
The firash (legal bed) of Umm al-Walad is weak, so mere verbal denial suffices to repudiate the child. This is unlike a wife, whose strong firash requires li'an for denial.
Weak firash means the master can even marry her to another man.
3
Analyze the diyanatan obligation
If the master had intercourse without withdrawal and kept her protected, the outward presumption is that the child is his, so he must acknowledge. If he withdrew or did not protect her, denial is permissible due to counter-presumptions.
The text notes two cases: protected without withdrawal vs. unprotected or withdrawal.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text states that 'thus it is narrated from Abu Hanifa.' It also mentions two other narrations from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, referenced in Kifayat al-Muntaha.
The exact content of those narrations is not given here, but they represent alternative positions.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical effect: In court, denial is effective and the child's lineage is not established. Religiously, the master may be sinful if he denies without valid reason (i.e., without withdrawal or protection issues).
The mufta bihi appears to be Abu Hanifa's view as the primary transmission.

مولی ام ولد کے بچہ کی نفی کر دے تو کیا حکم ہوگا
إِلَّا أَنَّهُ إِذَا نَفَاهُ يَنْتَفِى بِقَوْلِهِ لِأَنَّ فِرَاشَهَا ضَعِيفٌ حَتَّى يَمْلِكُ نَقْلَهُ بِالتَّزْوِيجِ بِخِلَافِ الْمَنْكُوحَةِ حَيْثُ لَا يَنْتَفِي الْوَلَدُ بِنَفْيِهِ إِلَّا بِاللَّعَانِ لِتَأَكُّدِ الْفِرَاشِ حَتَّى لَا يَمْلِكُ إِبْطَالَهُ بِالتَّزْوِيجِ وَهَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ حُكْمٌ فَأَمَّا الدِّيَانَةُ فَإِنْ كَانَ وَطِيَهَا وَحَصَّنَهَا وَلَمْ يَعْزِلْ عَنْهَا يَلْزَمُهُ أَنْ يَعْتَرِفَ بِهِ وَيَدَّعِى لِأَنَّ الظَّاهِرَ أَنَّ الْوَلَدَ مِنْهُ وَإِنْ عَزَلَ عَنْهَا أَوَلَمْ يُحَصِّنْهَا جَازَلَهُ أَنْ يَنْقِيَهُ لِأَنَّ هَذَا الظَّاهِرَ يُقَابِلُهُ ظَاهِرَاخَرُ هَكَذَا رُوِيَ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَفِيهِ رِوَايَتَانِ أَخْرَيَانِ عَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَعَنْ مُحَمَّدٍ ذَكَرْنَا هُمَا فِي كِفَايَةِ الْمُنْتَهِي ترجمہ البتہ منکوحہ کے بچہ اور ام ولد کے بچہ میں یہ فرق ہوتا ہے کہ اگر مولیٰ نے ام ولد کے دوسرے بچہ کی نفی کر دی تو صرف زبانی انکار سے ہی نفی ہو جاتی ہے۔ کیونکہ ام ولد کا فراش ہونا کمزور ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ مولی کو یہ اختیار ہوتا ہے کہ اپنے پاس سے علیحدہ کر کے کسی اجنبی مرد سے اس کا نکاح کر دے۔ بخلاف منکوحہ کے کہ اس کے بچہ کے نسب کی اس کے شوہر سے نفی نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ البتہ اگر نفی کرنا ہو تو اس کے لئے لعان کرنا ہو گا۔ کیونکہ بیوی ہونے اور بستر کے اعتبار سے اس کا حق مضبوط ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ شوہر کو یہ اختیار نہیں ہوتا ہے کہ اپنی بیوی کا دوسرے مرد سے نکاح کر کے خود سے اس کو دور کر دے۔ پھر یہاں تک ہم نے جو کچھ ذکر کیا ہے وہ ظاہری حکم ہے۔ کیونکہ دیانت داری کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ اگر اپنی باندی سے وطی کر لی اور باندی کو محفوظ رکھا۔ اور انزال کے بغیر اس سے جدا نہیں ہوتا رہا تو مولی پر یہ واجب ہوتا ہے کہ اس کے بچہ اور اس کے نسب کا اعتراف کرے۔ کیونکہ ظاہر بات یہی ہے کہ بچہ اسی کا ہے۔ البتہ اگر بغیر انزال اس سے جدا ہوتا رہا یا اس کو محفوظ نہیں رکھا تو بھی اس بچہ کے نسب سے انکار کرنا جائز ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس ظاہر کے مقابلہ میں دوسرا ظاہر بھی موجود ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ سے ایسی ہی روایت پائی گئی ہے۔ اور اس مسئلہ میں امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد سے دو اور روایتیں ہیں جن کا ذکر ہم نے کفایہ المنتہی میں کیا ہے۔
But if he denies it, it is repudiated by his statement, because her firash (bed) is weak, to the extent that he can transfer her by marriage, unlike a married woman where the child is not repudiated by his denial except by li'an, due to the strength of the firash, so that he cannot nullify it by marriage. This which we have mentioned is the external ruling (hukm). As for religious conscientiousness (diyanatan), if he had intercourse with her and protected her and did not withdraw from her, it is necessary for him to acknowledge and claim him, because the apparent fact is that the child is from him. If he withdrew from her or did not protect her, it is permissible for him to deny him, because this apparent fact is countered by another apparent fact. Thus it is narrated from Abu Hanifa. And there are two other narrations from Abu Yusuf and from Muhammad which we have mentioned in Kifayat al-Muntaha. Translation: However, there is a difference between the child of a married woman and the child of Umm al-Walad: if the master denies the second child of the Umm al-Walad, it is repudiated by mere verbal denial, because the firash of Umm al-Walad is weak, to the extent that the master has the right to separate her and marry her to another man. Unlike a married woman, where the lineage of her child is not repudiated by the husband's denial; rather, if he wants to deny, he must perform li'an. Because the wife's right based on marriage and bed is strong, to the extent that the husband does not have the right to marry her to another man and remove her from himself. Thus what we have mentioned is the external ruling. As for religious conscientiousness: if he had intercourse with his slave-girl and protected her and did not separate from her without ejaculation, then it is wajib upon him to acknowledge the child and his lineage, because the apparent fact is that the child is from him. If he separated from her without ejaculation or did not protect her, then it is permissible for him to deny the child's lineage, because this apparent fact is countered by another apparent fact. This is the narration from Abu Hanifa. And on this issue, there are two other narrations from Abu Yusuf and from Muhammad which we have mentioned in Kifayat al-Muntaha.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage addresses the denial of lineage for a child of Umm al-Walad and includes both a comparison with married women and an intra-Hanafi dispute. The text first distinguishes between Umm al-Walad and a married woman: for Umm al-Walad, the master can deny the child by mere verbal denial because her 'firash' (legal bed) is weak—he can even marry her to another man. In contrast, with a married woman, denial requires li'an (oath of condemnation) because her firash is strong. Then the text discusses the diyanatan (religious conscientiousness) aspect: if the master had intercourse, protected her (i.e., without withdrawal), the outward presumption is that the child is his, so he must acknowledge. But if he withdrew or didn't protect her, denial is permissible because there is another presumption. This is the transmission from Abu Hanifa. However, there are two other narrations from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad mentioned in Kifayat al-Muntaha (not detailed here). This shows the Hanafi school's internal ijtihad on the issue, with different opinions on whether denial is effective.

Key Terms
لعان (li'an): Mutual oath of condemnation between spouses when husband denies paternity of the child; not applicable to Umm al-Walad.
ديانة (diyanatan): Religious conscientiousness; what is morally and religiously obligatory before Allah, as opposed to the external judicial ruling (qada'an).
فراش (firash): Legal bed; the marital or concubinage relationship that establishes paternity presumptions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for a married woman's husband to deny paternity of her child?
A) Mere verbal statement
B) Li'an (mutual oath of condemnation)
C) Nothing; his denial is always effective
D) Divorce

Key Takeaway
A master's verbal denial of his Umm al-Walad's child is effective externally, but religiously he must acknowledge if there was unprotected intercourse without withdrawal.

Expiation Before Breaking the Oath
Inter-madhhab dispute on validity of premature kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving kaffarah before breaking the oath and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Kaffarah requires a crime (hanth); the oath is a preventer, not a cause, so premature kaffarah is invalid.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses whether one can give expiation (kaffarah) for an oath before actually breaking it. The Hanafi school holds it invalid because kaffarah is intended to conceal the crime of oath-breaking, which has not yet occurred. The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing the oath itself is the cause. We'll explore the proof and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: giving kaffarah before breaking the oath is not valid (la yujzihi).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that it is valid after the oath, which he considers the cause.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof is an analogy (qiyas): just as kaffarah after a wound is valid even before death, kaffarah after an oath is valid before breaking it.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes: the oath is a preventer (mani') not a conduit (mufdi), unlike a wound which necessarily leads to death.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi says premature kaffarah becomes sadaqah (charity); Shafi'i counts it as valid kaffarah.
This affects whether the obligation is considered fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ لِسَتْرِ الْجَنَايَةِ وَلَا جِنَايَةَ هَهُنَا وَ الْيَمِينُ لَيْسَتْ بِسَبَبٍ لِأَنَّهُ مَانِعٌ غَيْرُ مُفْضِ بِخِلَافِ الْجَرْحِ لِأَنَّهُ مُفْضِ ثُمَّ لَا يُسْتَرَدُّ کفارہ کا سبب یعنی قسم پائے جانے کے بعد کفارہ ادا کیا ہے۔ اس لئے حکم ایسا ہوا جیسے کسی کو زخمی کرنے کے بعد اس کے مرنے سے پہلے ہی کفارہ کا غلام آزاد کر دیا ۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ جرم چھپانے کے لئے کفارہ ہوتا ہے۔ اور یہاں قسم توڑنے سے پہلے کوئی جرم نہیں ہے۔ اور تم کفارہ کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ قسم تو رو کنے والی ہوتی ہے پہنچانے والی نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ یعنی قسم اس لئے ہوتی ہے کہ آدمی اسے پورا کرنے اور اس لئے نہیں ہوتی ہے کہ اسے تو ڑ کر کفارہ ادا کرے۔ اس بناء پر قسم کفارہ تک پہنچانے کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ بخلاف زخم کے کہ وہ تو موت تک پہنچانے والا ہوتا ہے۔ پھر دینے والا اس مال کو واپس نہیں لے سکتا ہے کیونکہ وہ صدقہ ہو گیا۔ (اور صدقہ کا واپس لینا جائز نہیں ہے)۔ تشریح وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ لِسَتْرِ الْجَنَايَةِ وَلَا جِبَايَةَ هَهُنَا وَالْيَمِينُ لَيْسَتْ بِسَبَبٍ لَا نَّهُ مَانِعٌ غَيْرُ مُفْضِ الخ اگر تم توڑنے سے پہلے ہی اس کا کفارہ ادا کر دیا جائے تو احناف کے نزدیک جائز نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن شوافع کے نزدیک مال سے کفارہ دینا جائز ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کفارہ کا سبب جو تم ہے وہ تو کفارہ دینے سے پہلے ہی پایا گیا قسم کے مسئلہ سے پہلے تو کوئی جرم نہیں پایا گیا ہے بلکہ صرف قم پائی گئ ہے جبکہ تم کفارہ واجب ہونے کا سب نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ قسم کی چیز کو بدلنے والی ہوتی ہے۔ کفارہ تک پہنچانے والی نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ یعنی قسم اس لئے ہوتی ہے کہ آدمی اسے پوری کرے۔ اور اس لئے نہیں ہوتی ہے کہ اسے خواہ مخواہ ہی تو ڑ کر کفارہ ادا کرے۔ بخلاف زخم کے کہ اس کا آخر انجام موت ہی ہے۔ یعنی زخمی ہو کر آخر کار مرجاتا ہے۔ فائدہ یعنی زخمی کرنے میں مجروح کی موت سے پہلے کفارہ دینے کو اس لئے جائز کہا گیا ہے کہ کاری اور مہلک زخم کا انجام موت ہی ہے۔ بخلاف قسم کے کہ اس کا انجام کفارہ ہی نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ کوئی شخص قسم کھا کر زندگی بھر اپنی قسم پر ( جائز وناجائز ) باقی رہ جائے تو اس پر کفارہ لازم نہیں ہو گا۔ اور اگر کوئی حانث ہونے سے پہلے کفارہ ادا کر دے تو وہ کفارہ میں شمار نہ ہو گا۔ البتہ صدقہ ہو جائے گا۔
And if he gives the expiation before breaking the oath, it does not suffice him. Al-Shafi'i said: It suffices him with wealth because he performed it after the cause, which is the oath, so it is similar to expiation after a wound. Our proof is that expiation is to conceal the crime, and there is no crime here. And the oath is not a cause because it is a preventer, not a conduit, unlike the wound because it is a conduit. Then it cannot be taken back from the poor person because it has occurred as charity. Translation: And if the one who breaks the oath gives the expiation before breaking it, it is not valid according to the Hanafis. Imam Shafi'i said that giving expiation in wealth is valid because the cause of expiation, the oath, already exists before giving expiation. So the ruling is like freeing a slave for expiation after wounding someone but before their death; that is permissible by consensus. Our proof is that expiation is meant to conceal the crime, and in this case there is no crime before breaking the oath. The oath is not a cause because it is a preventer, not a conduit. Unlike a wound, which eventually leads to death. Then the one who gave it cannot take back the wealth because it has become charity. Explanation: If one gives expiation before breaking the oath, according to the Hanafis it is not valid. According to the Shafi'is, giving expiation in wealth is valid because the cause, the oath, has already occurred. Their analogy is that if one wounds someone and then frees a slave for expiation before the wounded dies, it is valid by consensus. Our proof is that expiation is to conceal a crime, and here there is no crime before breaking the oath. The oath is not a cause because it is meant to prevent one from doing something, not to lead to expiation. Unlike a wound, which eventually leads to death. Therefore, it cannot be taken back from the poor person because it has become charity. Benefit: The reason it is permissible to give expiation before death in case of a wound is that a deadly wound's end is death. Unlike an oath, whose end is not expiation, because one may keep the oath for life and never break it, so expiation is not obligatory. If one gives expiation before breaking the oath, it does not count as expiation but becomes charity.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of giving kaffarah before actually breaking the oath (hanth). The Hanafi position is that it is not valid (la yujzihi) because kaffarah is meant to cover the crime (jinaayah) of breaking the oath, and before breaking, there is no crime. The oath itself is not a cause (sabab) for kaffarah because it is a deterrent (mani') not a conduit (mufdi) to the obligation. In contrast, a wound (jarh) is a cause for kaffarah (if it leads to death) because it is expected to lead to death. The Shafi'i school argues that the oath itself is the cause, analogizing to kaffarah after a wound. The Hanafi response distinguishes the nature of the oath as a deterrent, not a cause. However, the text notes that if someone gives kaffarah prematurely, it cannot be taken back because it becomes a voluntary sadaqah. The commentary also clarifies with a benefit: giving kaffarah before hanth is like giving sadaqah, not kaffarah.

Key Terms
الْحِنْثِ (al-hanth): Breaking an oath by doing what one swore not to do, or not doing what one swore to do.
السَّبَبِ (al-sabab): Cause; here, the factor that necessitates kaffarah. Hanafis say the cause is the hanth, not the oath.
صَدَقَةٌ (sadaqah): Voluntary charity; premature kaffarah becomes sadaqah and cannot be taken back.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is giving kaffarah before breaking the oath invalid?
A) Because the oath is not a valid cause for kaffarah
B) Because kaffarah must be given after the sin
C) Because the wealth is not sufficient
D) Because the poor person must accept it

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, giving kaffarah before breaking the oath is not valid because the cause (sabab) is the act of breaking, not the oath itself.

Oath on a Sin and Oath of a Kafir
Two distinct rulings on oaths: sinful oaths and oaths by disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an oath involving a sin and explain why a disbeliever's oath is invalid.

Core Idea
An oath to commit a sin must be broken and expiated; a disbeliever's oath is void from the start.

This passage from al-Quduri presents two distinct cases: one who swears to commit a sin, and a disbeliever who swears. The underlying principle is that an oath is a form of glorification of Allah. Therefore, a sinful oath contradicts the purpose of an oath and must be remedied, while a disbeliever lacks the qualification to make a valid oath. Understanding these rulings helps avoid sin and clarifies the conditions for a binding oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The text first discusses a person who swears to commit a sin (ma'siyah), giving examples like not praying, not speaking to one's father, or murder. Then it discusses a disbeliever (kafir) who swears an oath.
Note the distinct outcomes: the former requires action; the latter has no consequence.
2
State the ruling for a sinful oath
The one who swears to a sin must break his oath (yanbaghi an yahintha nafsahu) and then give kaffarah (expiation). This is wajib.
Breaking the oath is the better choice because fulfilling the sin brings no remedy.
3
Examine the dalil
The text cites the hadith: 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better, let him do that which is better and then expiate his oath.' The command to do the better implies breaking the sinful oath is better, and expiation follows.
The hadith is a sound (ahad) tradition, with zanni thubut but qati dalalah.
4
Understand the rationale for expiation
The text argues that breaking the oath and giving kaffarah provides a remedy (jabir), while fulfilling the sin offers no remedy. Thus, the only correct course is to break and expiate.
This is a legal reasoning beyond the hadith itself.
5
Determine the ruling for a disbeliever's oath
If a kafir swears and then breaks it (hanth), whether while still in kufr or after embracing Islam, there is no sin (la hantha) and no obligation of kaffarah.
The oath is considered invalid from the outset.
6
Analyze the cause of invalidity
The kafir is not qualified (ahl) for the oath because an oath is made for glorifying Allah, and a disbeliever does not glorify Allah. Also, kaffarah is an act of worship, which a disbeliever cannot perform.
Qualification (ahliyyah) for worship is a key Hanafi concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ عَلَى مَعْصِيَةٍ مِثْلُ اَنْ لَّا يُصَلِّيَ أَوْلَا يُكَلَّمَ آبَاهُ اَوْلَيَقْتُلَنَّ فُلَانًا يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُحْنِتَ نَفْسَهُ وَيُكَفِّرَ عَنْ يَمِينِهِ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ حَلَفَ عَلَى يَمِيْنِ وَرَأَى غَيْرَهَا خَيْرًا مِنْهَا فَلْيَاتِ بِالَّذِي هُوَ خَيْرٌ ثُمَّ لِيُكَفِّرْ عَنْ يَمِينِهِ وَلَانَّ فِيْمَا قُلْنَاهُ تَفْوِيْتُ الْبَرِّ إِلى جَابِرٍ وَهُوَ الْكَفَّارَةً وَ لَا جَابِرَ لِلْمَعْصِيَةِ فِي ضِدِهِ وَإِذَا حَلَفَ الْكَافِرُ ثُمَّ حَنَكَ فِي حَالِ كُفْرِهِ أَوْ بَعْدَ إِسْلَامِهِ فَلَا حِنْثَ عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأَهْلِ لِلْيَمِينِ لِأَنَّهَا تُعْقَدُ لِتَعْظِيمِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَمَعَ الْكُفْرِ لَا يَكُونُ مُعَظِمًا وَلَا هُوَ أَهْلٌ لِلْكَفَّارَةِ لِأَنَّهَا عِبَادَةٌ ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا ہے کہ جس شخص نے کسی نافرمانی یا گناہ کے کام پر قسم کھائی مثلاً وہ نماز نہیں پڑھے گا یا اپنے باپ سے گفتگو نہیں کرے گا یا فلاں شخص کو ضر ور قتل کرے گا تو اسے چاہئے کہ اپنی ایسی قسم توڑ دے اور اس قسم کا کفارہ ادا کر د ہے۔ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے اس فرمان کی وجہ سے کہ جس نے کوئی قسم کھائی اور بعد میں اس کے خلاف کرنے میں ہی بہتری سمجھی تو جسے وہ بہتر سمجھے وہ کر ڈالے پھر اپنی قسم کا کفارہ ادا کردے (مسلم وغیرہ نے اس کی روایت کی ہے ) اور اس دلیل سے کہ جو صورت ہم نے بیان کی ہے اگر اس میں اپنی قسم پوری نہ کر سکے تو کفارہ سے اس کی کچھ تلافی ہو جاتی ہے۔ اور اس کے خلاف کرنے سے یعنی اس معصیت پر عمل کر لینے میں نقصان کے سوا کچھ تلافی نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ تشریح اگر کسی نے گناہ یا نافرمانی کی قسم کھائی تو اسے چاہئے کہ قسم توڑ دے اور کفارہ دے دلیل حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کی حدیث ہے۔ كافر نے حالت كفر میں قسم کھائی یا اسلام لانے کے بعد حانث ہو گیا تو اس پر کفارہ نہیں ترجمه وَاِذَا حَلَفَ الْكَافِرُ ثُمَّ حَنَكَ فِي حَالِ كُفْرِهِ أَوْ بَعْدَ إِسْلَامِهِ فَلَاحِنْتَ عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأَهْلِ لِلْيَمِينِ لِأَنَّهَا تُعْقَدُ لِتَعْظِيمِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَمَعَ الْكُفْرِ لَايَكُونُ مُعَظَمَـا وَلَاهُوَ أَهْلَّ لِلْكَفَّارَةِ لِأَنَّهَا عِبَادَةٌ
He said: And whoever swears to a sin, such as that he will not pray, or will not speak to his father, or will certainly kill so-and-so, he should break his oath and expiate his oath due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better than it, let him do that which is better and then expiate his oath.' And because in what we have said, there is the nullification of the good towards a remedy, which is expiation, and there is no remedy for the sin in its opposite. And if a kafir swears, then he breaks it while in a state of kufr or after his Islam, then there is no sin upon him because he is not qualified for the oath, for it is made for the glorification of Allah Almighty, and with kufr he is not glorifying, and he is not qualified for expiation because it is an act of worship. Translation: Quduri said: Whoever swears to a sin, such as that he will not pray, or will not speak to his father, or will certainly kill so-and-so, he should break his oath and give expiation for it. This is because of the saying of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better than it, let him do that which is better and then expiate his oath.' (Muslim etc. narrated it). And because in the situation we described, if he does not fulfill his oath, then expiation compensates for it. But doing the opposite, i.e., acting upon the sin, brings only harm, no compensation. Explanation: If someone swears to a sin or disobedience, he should break the oath and give expiation. The proof is the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). If a kafir swears an oath while in kufr, or breaks it after becoming Muslim, there is no expiation upon him. Translation: And if a kafir swears, then he breaks it while in the state of his kufr or after his Islam, there is no sin upon him because he is not qualified for the oath, because it is made for the glorification of Allah, and with kufr he is not glorifying, and he is not qualified for the expiation because it is an act of worship.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rulings. First, if one swears to commit a sin (ma'siyah), such as not praying or not speaking to one's father, or to murder, then it is wajib to break the oath and give kaffarah. The proof is the hadith of the Prophet (SAW) which commands doing the better thing and then expiating. The Hanafi reasoning is that by breaking the oath, one incurs the remedy (jabir) of kaffarah, while by fulfilling the sin there is no remedy. Therefore, one must break it. Second, if a kafir swears an oath and then breaks it, whether while still a kafir or after embracing Islam, there is no sin (hanth) upon him. This is because the kafir is not qualified (ahl) for the oath, as an oath involves glorifying Allah, which a kafir does not do. Also, he is not qualified for kaffarah because it is an act of worship (ibadah), which is not valid from a disbeliever.

Key Terms
مَعْصِيَةٍ (ma'siyah): An act of disobedience to Allah; a sin.
حِنْثَ (hanth): The act of breaking an oath; its legal consequence may be sin and kaffarah.
أَهْلَ (ahl): Legal qualification; a kafir is not qualified for oath due to lack of tazim (glorification).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, what should a person do if he swears to do something and then sees something better?
A) Fulfill the original oath to avoid perjury.
B) Do the better thing and then expiate the oath.
C) Ignore both and do nothing.
D) Ask for forgiveness without any action.

Key Takeaway
A sinful oath must be broken with expiation; a disbeliever's oath is inherently invalid and carries no obligation.

The Basic Ruling: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Detailed ruling on a comprehensive 'haram' statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the phrase 'Every halal is haram upon me' and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
A general oath is restricted by custom; istihsan prevents immediate violation.

When someone declares 'Every halal upon me is haram,' a literal reading would include every permissible act, making the oath impossible to keep. The Hanafi school, through istihsan and custom, limits this to food and drink, reflecting a balance between textual generality and practical intent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Statement and Its Literal Scope
Identify that the phrase 'Every halal is haram upon me' is a comprehensive oath. Literally, it covers all halal things (food, drink, speech, movement, etc.).
2
Recognize the Qiyas Objection (Imam Zufar)
Imam Zufar argued that any permissible action after the statement would constitute hanth (breaking the oath). This is based on qiyas: if the statement is general, then performing any halal act violates it.
Imam Zufar's view is recorded but not the final position.
3
Apply Istihsan: The Purpose of the Oath
The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn) uses istihsan: the purpose (maqsud) of the oath is to make a binding prohibition, not to be broken immediately. A literal interpretation would render the oath impossible or trivial, defeating its intent.
Istihsan is a Hanafi proof source.
4
Custom (Urf) Restricts to Food and Drink
Customary usage of 'halal' and 'haram' in such statements is limited to food and drink. The statement is therefore interpreted to apply only to consuming food and drink.
Urf is a subsidiary source used in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Confirm the Ruling: No Immediate Hanth
Under zahir al-riwayah, the statement is an oath on food/drink only. Breaking it requires kaffarah (expiation) but not immediate hanth. The wife is not included unless specifically intended.
This is the earlier established ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَوْ قَالَ كُلُّ حِلَّ عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ فَهُوَ عَلَى الطَّعَامِ وَالشَّرَابِ إِلَّا اَنْ يَّنْوِىَ غَيْرَ ذَالِكَ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يُحْنِثَ كَمَا فَرَغَ نَّهُ بَاشَرَفِعْلًا مُبَاحَا وَهُوَ التَّنَفُسُ وَ نَحْوُهُ وَهَذَا قَوْلُ زُفَرٌ وَجْهُ الْإِسْتِحْسَان أَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ هُوَ الْبَرُّ لَا يَتَحَصَّلُ عَ إِعْتِبَارِ الْعُمُوْمِ وَإِذَاسَقَطَ اعْتِبَارُهُ يَنْصَرِفْ إِلَى الطَّعَامِ وَالشَّرَابِ لِلْعُرْفِ فَإِنَّهُ يُسْتَعْمَلُ فِيمَا يَتَنَاوَلُ عَادَةٌ لَا يَتَنَاوَلُ الْمَرْأَةَ إِلَّا بِالنِّيَّةِ لِإِسْقَاطِ اِعْتِبَارِ الْعُمُومِ وَ إِذَا نَوَاهَا كَانَ إِبْلَاءُ وَ لَا تُصْرَفُ الْيَمِينُ عَنِ لمَا كُولِ وَالْمَشْرُوبَ وَهَذَا كُلُّهُ جَوَابُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَةِ وَمَشَايِخُنَا قَالُوا يَقَعُ بِهِ الطَّلَاقُ عَنْ غَيْرِ نِيَّةٍ لِغَلْبَةِ الْإِسْتِعْمَالِ وَ عَلَيْهِ الْفَتْوَى وَكَذَا يَنْبَغِي فِي قَوْلِهِ حَلَالٌ بَرْوَى حَرَامٌ لِلْعُرْفِ وَاخْتَلَفُوا فِي قَوْلِهِ يَرْجِهُ بَرْ وَسُتِ راست گیرم - بَرُ وِی حَرَامٌ أَنَّهُ هَلْ تُشْتَرَطُ النِّيَّةُ وَالأظْهَرُ انَّهُ يُجْعَلُ طَلَاقَا مِنْ غَيْرِ نِيَّةِ لِلْعُرْفِ
If he said: 'Every halal upon me is haram,' then it applies to food and drink, unless he intends something else. And qiyas would require that he becomes hanth as soon as he finishes it, by performing a permissible act like breathing and similar, and this is the opinion of Zufar. The istihsan reason is that the purpose is the fulfillment of the oath, which is not achieved by considering generality. When its consideration falls, it turns to food and drink because of custom ('urf), for it is used customarily for what is normally consumed; it does not include the wife except by intention, due to dropping consideration of generality. And if he intended her, then it is absolution. The oath is not diverted from food and drink. All of this is the answer of zahir al-riwayah. And our Mashayikh said: By it divorce occurs without intention due to prevalence of usage, and upon this is the fatwa. Similarly, it should be in his saying 'halal' or 'haram' due to custom. And they differed regarding his saying 'yariju bar' (?) 'for him it is haram' whether intention is required? And the most apparent is that it is made divorce without intention due to custom.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex situation when someone says, 'Every halal thing is haram upon me.' The ruling of the Hanafi school (from zahir al-riwayah) is that such a statement is interpreted according to custom ('urf). It generally applies to food and drink (eating and drinking), not to one's wife or other matters, unless the person specifically intended them. The qiyas (analogy) as held by Imam Zufar would make the person immediately guilty of hanth (breaking the oath) as soon as he performs any permissible act like breathing, because those are halal and covered by the general statement. But istihsan overrides that: the purpose (maqsud) is to fulfill the oath by avoiding the things, not to make it impossible. Custom limits the statement to food and drink. The passage then notes a later position of the Mashayikh (our scholars) that such a statement also effects divorce (talaq) to the wife without need for intention, because common usage ('urf) in that context is for divorce. This is the mufta bihi (fatwa position). For the statement 'halal' or 'haram' referring to the wife, custom dictates divorce. There is a difference of opinion among later scholars about whether intention is required, but the most apparent view is that no intention is needed due to custom. This shows the development of Hanafi fiqh: earlier rulings based on literal interpretation ('amm) were refined by istihsan and 'urf, and later rulings (Muta'akhkhirin) further evolved based on practical usage.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of custom or ease; used here to restrict the general wording to food/drink.
عرف (urf): Customary usage; determines the interpretation of ambiguous statements like 'halal' and 'haram'.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath, incurring the obligation of kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwayah Hanafi position, the phrase 'Every halal is haram upon me' is interpreted to apply primarily to:
A) All permissible actions.
B) Food and drink only.
C) The wife if intended.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan and custom to restrict a general oath to food and drink, overriding a literal qiyas that would make the oath impossible to keep.

Later Fatwa: Divorce Without Intention Due to Custom
Later Hanafi fatwa on divorce without intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why later Hanafi scholars ruled that the statement 'Every halal is haram upon me' effects divorce without intention, and identify the custom that supports this change.

Core Idea
Custom evolves; the fatwa shifts to meet common usage.

While the earlier rulings (zahir al-riwayah) required explicit intention for the statement to include the wife, later Hanafi scholars observed that in common parlance, such phrases were used to mean divorce. Relying on the principle that custom ('urf) governs interpretation, they issued a fatwa that the statement effects divorce without need for intention. This lesson explores that development and the accompanying ikhtilaf among later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Earlier Position
From zahir al-riwayah, the phrase applies to food/drink; the wife is only included if intended. This is the baseline ruling.
This is not the final fatwa.
2
Observe the Shift in Custom
Later Mashayikh (our scholars) noted that people commonly used the phrase 'Every halal is haram upon me' to express divorce, not just a food restriction. The custom ('urf) changed.
Urf is dynamic.
3
Apply the Principle: Custom Determines Meaning
Since the prevalent usage was for divorce, the statement is treated as a divorce declaration (talaq) even without explicit intention. The custom (ghalabat al-istimal) overrides the default literal interpretation.
This is a shift from the literalism of zahir al-riwayah.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf Among Later Scholars
Some later scholars still required intention, but the most apparent (al-azhar) view is that no intention is needed due to custom. The text says 'al-azhar annahu yuj'alu talaqan min ghayr niyyah lil urf'.
The fatwa is given to the no-intention view.
5
Apply Similarly to Other Phrasing
The ruling extends to similar expressions like saying 'halal' or 'haram' in reference to the wife. Custom treats these as divorce.
Consistency in the method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَوْ قَالَ كُلُّ حِلَّ عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ فَهُوَ عَلَى الطَّعَامِ وَالشَّرَابِ إِلَّا اَنْ يَّنْوِىَ غَيْرَ ذَالِكَ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يُحْنِثَ كَمَا فَرَغَ نَّهُ بَاشَرَفِعْلًا مُبَاحَا وَهُوَ التَّنَفُسُ وَ نَحْوُهُ وَهَذَا قَوْلُ زُفَرٌ وَجْهُ الْإِسْتِحْسَان أَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ هُوَ الْبَرُّ لَا يَتَحَصَّلُ عَ إِعْتِبَارِ الْعُمُوْمِ وَإِذَاسَقَطَ اعْتِبَارُهُ يَنْصَرِفْ إِلَى الطَّعَامِ وَالشَّرَابِ لِلْعُرْفِ فَإِنَّهُ يُسْتَعْمَلُ فِيمَا يَتَنَاوَلُ عَادَةٌ لَا يَتَنَاوَلُ الْمَرْأَةَ إِلَّا بِالنِّيَّةِ لِإِسْقَاطِ اِعْتِبَارِ الْعُمُومِ وَ إِذَا نَوَاهَا كَانَ إِبْلَاءُ وَ لَا تُصْرَفُ الْيَمِينُ عَنِ لمَا كُولِ وَالْمَشْرُوبَ وَهَذَا كُلُّهُ جَوَابُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَةِ وَمَشَايِخُنَا قَالُوا يَقَعُ بِهِ الطَّلَاقُ عَنْ غَيْرِ نِيَّةٍ لِغَلْبَةِ الْإِسْتِعْمَالِ وَ عَلَيْهِ الْفَتْوَى وَكَذَا يَنْبَغِي فِي قَوْلِهِ حَلَالٌ بَرْوَى حَرَامٌ لِلْعُرْفِ وَاخْتَلَفُوا فِي قَوْلِهِ يَرْجِهُ بَرْ وَسُتِ راست گیرم - بَرُ وِی حَرَامٌ أَنَّهُ هَلْ تُشْتَرَطُ النِّيَّةُ وَالأظْهَرُ انَّهُ يُجْعَلُ طَلَاقَا مِنْ غَيْرِ نِيَّةِ لِلْعُرْفِ
If he said: 'Every halal upon me is haram,' then it applies to food and drink, unless he intends something else. Key Terms
غلبة الاستعمال (ghalabat al-istimal): Prevalence of usage; the fact that a phrase is overwhelmingly used in a specific sense, which becomes its legal meaning.
الأظهر (al-azhar): The most apparent or strongest opinion among multiple views.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi fatwa, the phrase 'Every halal is haram upon me' effects divorce:
A) Only if the speaker intended divorce.
B) Only if food and drink are also intended.
C) Without intention due to custom.
D) Only if the wife is specifically named.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh evolves with custom: a statement that originally required intention for divorce later effects it automatically due to common usage, and this becomes the fatwa.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Interpretation of 'Every Halal Thing is Haram for Me'
Interpretation of oath phrase with qiyas vs istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'kullu hill alayya haram' and explain the role of custom (urf) and istihsan in limiting its scope.

Core Idea
When an oath is too broad to fulfill, custom restricts it to what is commonly used — istihsan overrides qiyas.

This passage addresses the interpretation of a common oath statement. If a person says 'Every halal thing is haram for me,' does that truly mean everything? The Hanafi school, relying on custom and the principle of istihsan, restricts this to food and drink — otherwise the oath would be impossible to keep. But what about the wife? And what if the speaker intends something else? Let's unpack the ruling and its proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its default ruling
The phrase 'kullu hill alayya haram' is an oath. By default (Zahir al-Riwayah), it applies only to food and drink unless the speaker intends otherwise.
This default is based on custom (urf): such expressions are commonly used for consumables.
2
Understand the qiyas objection (Zufar)
Qiyas would imply that any halal act, like breathing, breaks the oath immediately because the general words include all halal things. This is the view of Imam Zufar.
This strict qiyas would make the oath impossible to keep, as no one can stop breathing.
3
See how istihsan and custom override qiyas
The Hanafi school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) because the purpose of an oath is to fulfill it, not to break it. Custom restricts the general meaning to food and drink, making fulfillment possible.
The key principle: al-‘urf yakhuss al-‘amm (custom specifies the general).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the wife
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, the wife is included only if intended, and then it becomes ila (oath of abstinence). Later mashayikh ruled that divorce occurs without intention due to prevalent usage, and this is the mufta bihi.
The fatwa is on the mashayikh's view: divorce is established regardless of intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَوْ قَالَ كُلُّ حِلَّ عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ فَهُوَ عَلَى الطَّعَامِ وَالشَّرَابِ إِلَّا اَنْ يَّنْوِىَ غَيْرَ ذَالِكَ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يُحْنِثَ كَمَا فَرَغَ نَّهُ بَاشَرَفِعْلًا مُبَاحًا وَهُوَ التَّنَفُسُ وَ نَحْوُهُ وَهَذَا قَوْلُ زُفَرٌ وَجْهُ الْإِسْتِحْسَان أَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ هُوَ الْبَرُّ لَا يَتَحَصَّلُ عَ إِعْتِبَارِ الْعُمُوْمِ وَإِذَاسَقَطَ اعْتِبَارُهُ يَنْصَرِفْ إِلَى الطَّعَامِ وَالشَّرَابِ لِلْعُرْفِ فَإِنَّهُ يُسْتَعْمَلُ فِيمَا يَتَنَاوَلُ عَادَةٌ لَا يَتَنَاوَلُ الْمَرْأَةَ إِلَّا بِالنِّيَّةِ لِإِسْقَاطِ اِعْتِبَارِ الْعُمُوْمِ وَ إِذَا نَوَاهَا كَانَ إِبْلَاءُ وَ لَا تُصْرَفُ الْيَمِينُ عَنِ لمَا كُولِ وَالْمَشْرُوبَ وَهَذَا كُلُّهُ جَوَابُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَةِ وَمَشَايِخُنَا قَالُوا يَقَعُ بِهِ الطَّلَاقُ عَنْ غَيْرِ نِيَّةٍ لِغَلْبَةِ الْإِسْتِعْمَالِ وَ عَلَيْهِ الْفَتْوَى وَكَذَا يَنْبَغِي فِي قَوْلِهِ حَلَالٌ بَرْوَى حَرَامٌ لِلْعُرْفِ وَاخْتَلَفُوا فِي قَوْلِهِ يَرْجِهُ بَرْ وَسُتِ راست گیرم - بَرُ وِی حَرَامٌ أَنَّهُ هَلْ تُشْتَرَطُ النِّيَّةُ وَالأظْهَرُ انَّهُ يُجْعَلُ طَلَاقَا مِنْ غَيْرِ نِيَّةِ لِلْعُرْفِ

مستعمل ہوتا ہے جو عادت کے طور پر استعمال کی جاتی ہوں۔ اور ایک مرد کے لئے اس کی اپنی بیوی کو استعمال کرنا بالکل حلال ہوتا ہے اس کے باوجود اس پر شامل نہ ہوگا۔ البتہ اگر اس کی نیت کر لی ہو تب شامل ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر اس نے عورت کی نیت کرنی ہوتو یہ کلمہ ایلا ہ ہو جائے گا۔ اس کے ساتھ ہی اس کے کھانے اور پینے کی چیزوں سے اس کی قسم ختم نہیں ہوگی۔ یہ حکم ظاہر الروایہ کے مطابق ہے۔ اور ہمارے مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ اگر اس نے ہر حلال چیز اپنے اوپر حرام ہونے میں اپنی بیوی کی بھی نیت کر لی ہو تو بغیر نیت اسے طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس جملہ کا غالب استعمال اسی معنی میں ہو گیا ہے۔ اور اسی پر فتویٰ بھی ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر اس نے فاری میں کہا حلال بروے حرام ۔ یعنی حلال چیز اس پر حرام ہے۔ تو اس سے بھی اس کی بیوی پر طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ عرف میں ایسا ہی ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے کہا ہر چہ بر دست راست گیرم برو حرام ۔ یعنی جو کچھ داہنے ہاتھ میں لوں وہ مجھ پر حرام ہے۔ تو مشایخ نے اس میں اختلاف کیا ہے کہ اس میں نیت کی شرط ہوگی یا نہیں۔ اور اظہر یہی ہے کہ نیت کے بغیر بھی طلاق کا حکم ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہی عرف ہے۔ تشریح اگر کسی نے کل خل علی حرام کہا تو قیاس کا یہ تقاضا ہے کہ یہ الفاظ ختم ہوتے ہی سانس لینے یا معمولی حرکت کرنے سے ہی حٹ کا نفاذ ہو جائے کیونکہ یہ چیز کل حل میں داخل ہیں لیکن فقہاء نے استحسان کی راہ نکالی اسلئے کہ یمین کا اصل مقصود یہ ہے کہ اس کی تکمیل کی جائے تو اب عرف عام کی وجہ سے یہ کلمہ ان اشیاء کو شامل ہوگا جو عادتا استعمال کی جائیں اور اگر اپنی زوجہ کی نیت کی تو ایلاء ہو جائے گا۔ یہ حکم ظاہر الروایہ ہے لیکن مشائخ بلخ فرماتے ہیں کہ قائل نے اپنی بیوی کی نیت کی ہو یا نہ کی ہو اس پر طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی۔
If he says 'Every halal thing is haram for me,' it applies to food and drink unless he intends otherwise. Qiyas would require that he breaks his oath immediately upon the utterance, as soon as he does a permissible act like breathing or the like — this is the view of Zufar. The basis of istihsan is that the purpose is to fulfill the oath, which cannot be achieved by considering the general meaning; when general application is dropped, it is restricted to food and drink by custom. For it is used for things that are customarily consumed, and does not include the wife except by intention, because general application is dropped. And if he intends her, it becomes ila (an oath of abstinence). The oath is not diverted from food and drink. All this is the answer of Zahir al-Riwayah. Our mashayikh said: Divorce occurs by it without intention due to the prevalence of use, and the fatwa is upon this. Similarly, it should be in his saying 'halal barway haram' (halal thing is haram) by custom. And they differed regarding his saying 'har cheh bar dast-e-rast giram barway haram' (whatever I take in my right hand is haram) whether intention is required. The most apparent is that it is made divorce without intention due to custom. (Urdu) Translation: And if someone said 'every halal thing is haram for me,' then this applies only to food and drink. However, if he intended something else, it will be considered. Qiyas would require that as soon as he utters the sentence, he becomes a breaker of the oath because immediately after, he did a permissible thing, i.e., he breathed or did similar things. Imam Zufar's saying is also this. But what is mentioned in this book is the ruling of istihsan. The reason is that the main purpose of swearing an oath is to fulfill it, but when the general meaning is too broad, it becomes impossible to fulfill. Therefore, the general meaning is specified, and by common usage, it is limited to food and drink. Such expressions are commonly used for things that are customarily consumed. And for a man, using his own wife is perfectly halal, yet it is not included in this expression unless he intends it. If he intends his wife, it becomes ila. The oath regarding food and drink remains intact. This ruling is according to Zahir al-Riwayah. And our mashayikh said that if in declaring every halal thing haram upon himself, he also intended his wife, divorce occurs without intention because the prevalent usage has come to mean that. And the fatwa is upon this. Similarly, if he said in Farsi 'halal barway haram' i.e., a halal thing is haram for him, then also divorce occurs to his wife because such is the custom. And if he said 'har cheh bar dast-e-rast giram barway haram' i.e., whatever I take in my right hand is haram for me, then the mashayikh differed whether intention is required. The most apparent is that the ruling of divorce is given without intention because of custom. Explanation: If someone said 'kullu hill alayya haram', qiyas requires that as soon as the words finish, by breathing or any minor movement, the oath is broken because these are included in 'kullu hill'. But the fuqaha took the path of istihsan, because the real purpose of an oath is its fulfillment, so by general custom this phrase applies only to things customarily used. If he intended his wife, it becomes ila. This is the ruling of Zahir al-Riwayah, but the mashayikh of Balkh say that whether he intended his wife or not, divorce ba'in occurs.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage deals with the interpretation of the phrase 'kullu hill alayya haram' (every halal thing is haram for me). The Hanafi ruling is that this is restricted to food and drink by custom (urf), unless the speaker specifically intends something else (like his wife). The text discusses a qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference) debate. According to qiyas, as soon as the person says these words, they would break their oath by merely breathing or doing any permissible act, because those acts are included in the general 'every halal thing'. This was the view of Imam Zufar. However, the relied-upon ruling (Zahir al-Riwayah) adopts istihsan, reasoning that the purpose of an oath is to fulfill it, but if the oath is interpreted too broadly, it becomes impossible to keep. Therefore, by custom, it is confined to food and drink. The wife is not included unless specifically intended. If the wife is intended, it becomes ila (an oath of abstinence from marital relations). The text further notes that later mashayikh (scholars) ruled that if the wife is included, divorce occurs even without explicit intention due to prevalent usage, and this is the basis for fatwa. Similarly, Persian phrases like 'halal barway haram' are treated according to custom. There is disagreement on phrases like 'har cheh bar dast-e-rast giram barway haram' (whatever I take in my right hand is haram), but the most apparent view is that divorce occurs without intention due to custom.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Custom or common usage that determines the interpretation of imprecise expressions in oaths and other contracts.
إيلاء (ila): An oath by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period, with legal consequences if he does not return.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides strict qiyas in favor of a more practical or equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All permissible things, including breathing.
B) Only food and drink.
C) Only the wife.
D) Any act that is halal.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses custom (urf) and istihsan to restrict broad oath statements to what is typically understood, ensuring the oath can be fulfilled.

Defining 'Bayt' in Oaths: The Core Principle
Definition of 'bayt' and application to religious buildings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'bayt' for an oath of entry and identify which structures (e.g. mosques, churches) are excluded.

Core Idea
A house in an oath means a place built for overnight stay—not every building.

In this passage, Imam al-Marghinani establishes the key principle for oaths concerning houses: the term 'bayt' is legally defined as a structure prepared for overnight stay (baytutah). This definition determines whether entering a particular building breaks the oath. The author then applies it to religious buildings and other structures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
The passage begins with a person swearing not to enter a 'bayt'. Recognize that this is an oath of abstention; the ruling depends on the linguistic definition of 'bayt'.
The oath is general—'I will not enter any house.'
2
Extract the legal definition
The text states: 'al-bayt ma u'idda li-l-baytutah'—the house is what is prepared for overnight stay. Identify that this is the core criterion.
Baytutah is the key term; it means spending the night.
3
Apply to religious structures
The Ka'bah, mosque, church, synagogue are not built for baytutah. Therefore, entering them does not break the oath.
Even though the Ka'bah is called 'Bayt Allah', it is not a place for sleeping.
4
Note the underlying reasoning (ta'lil)
The author gives the reason: 'because these places were not built for that purpose.' Understand that the ruling hinges on the intended function of the structure, not its name.
This is a clear example of ta'lil in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْيَمِينِ فِي الدُّخُولِ وَالسُّكْنَى وَ مَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَدْخُلُ بَيْتًا فَدَخَلَ الْكَعْبَةَ أَوِ الْمَسْجِدَ اَوِ الْبَيْعَةَ أَوِ الْكَنِيسَةَ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لِاَنَّ الْبَيْتَ مَا أُعِدَّ لِلْبَيْتُوتَةِ وَ هذِهِ الْبَقَاعُ مَا بُنِيَتْ لَهَا وَ كَذَا إِذَا دَخَلَ دِهْلِيْرًا اَوْظُلَّةَ بَاب الدَّارِ لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا وَالظُّلَّةُ مَا تَكُونُ عَلَى السِّكَةِ وَقِيلَ إِذَا كَانَ الدِّهْلِيْرُ بِحَيْثُ لَوْ أُعْلِقَ الْبَابُ يَبْقَى دَاخِلاوَهُوَ مُسَقَّفٌ يَحْنِتُ لِأَنَّهُ يُبَاتُ فِيهِ عَادَةً وَإِنْ دَخَلَ صُفَّةٌ حَيْثُ لأَنَّهَا تُبنى لِلْبَيْتُوْتَةِ فِيهِ فِى بَعْضِ الْأَوْقَاتِ فَصَارَ كَالشَّتوى وَالصَّيْفِي وَقِيلَ هَذَا إِذَا كَانَتِ الصُّفَّةُ ذَاتَ حَوَائِطَ اَرْبَعَةٍ وَ هكَذَا كَانَتْ صِفَافُهُمْ وَقِيلَ الْجَوَابُ مَجْرى عَلَى إِطْلَاقِهِ وَهُوَ الصَّحِيحُ
Chapter: on the oath concerning entering and residing. Whoever swears that he will not enter a house, and then enters the Ka'bah, a mosque, a church, or a synagogue, he does not break his oath because a 'house' (bayt) is what is prepared for overnight stay, and these places were not built for that. Similarly, if he enters a vestibule (dihliz) or a canopy (zullah) of the door of the house, he does not break his oath for the reason we mentioned. And the zullah is what is over the street. And it is said: if the vestibule is such that when the door is closed, he remains inside and it is roofed, then he breaks his oath because usually one stays overnight there. And if he enters a portico (suffah), then he breaks his oath because it is built for overnight stay at some times, so it becomes like a winter or summer apartment. And it is said: this applies when the portico has four walls, and such were their porticos. And it is said: the ruling applies absolutely, and that is correct.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This chapter discusses oaths involving entering a 'bayt' (house). The key principle is that the term 'bayt' in its legal context refers to a structure specifically built for overnight stay (baytutah). Therefore, buildings not intended for sleeping, such as the Ka'bah, general mosques, churches, and synagogues, are not included in the term, and entering them does not break the oath. The text then examines borderline structures: a vestibule (dihliz) and a canopy (zullah) are generally not for overnight stay, so entering them does not break the oath. However, there is a reported opinion (qil) that if the vestibule is roofed and can be closed off, it might be used for sleeping, thus breaking the oath. Similarly, a portico (suffah) is often used for sleeping, especially if it has four walls, so entering it does break the oath. The commentary notes that the correct view is the absolute ruling: the portico always counts as a place of overnight stay, in line with common practice of the people of Kufa.

Key Terms
بيت (bayt): A structure prepared for overnight stay (baytutah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the defining characteristic of a 'bayt' in the context of oaths?
A) It has four walls and a roof.
B) It is prepared for overnight stay (baytutah).
C) It is a permanent dwelling with a kitchen.
D) It is any building that is not a mosque.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a 'house' (bayt) for oath purposes is defined by its primary function—being a place for overnight stay.
