Borderline Structures: Dihliz, Zullah, and Suffah
Analysis of borderline structures: vestibule, canopy, portico

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a vestibule (dihliz), canopy (zullah), or portico (suffah) breaks an oath, and state the preferred Hanafi view on the suffah.

Core Idea
A roofed vestibule or a portico can break an oath if customarily used for sleeping.

After establishing the general definition of 'bayt', Imam al-Marghinani addresses borderline structures that are commonly attached to houses. A vestibule (dihliz) and a canopy (zullah) over a street are not usually for sleeping, but a roofed vestibule might be. A portico (suffah) is often used for resting or sleeping at different times, leading to a difference of opinion. The author favors the absolute view that any suffah breaks the oath because of established custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structures
List the three structures: dihliz (vestibule), zullah (canopy/awning over a street), and suffah (portico/veranda). Understand their physical configurations.
Dihliz is a covered passage inside the entrance; zullah is a canopy projecting over the street; suffah is a covered area attached to a house, often open on one side.
2
Apply the baytutah criterion
For each structure, determine whether it is commonly prepared for overnight stay. Dihliz and zullah are generally not; suffah often is, especially in warmer climates.
Custom plays a role here.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on dihliz
Note the reported opinion (qil) that if the dihliz is roofed and can be closed, it may be used for sleeping, so the oath is broken. Otherwise, it is not.
This shows the application of the principle to specific conditions.
4
Examine the ikhtilaf on suffah
One opinion restricts the suffah to those with four walls (like a room). The correct view is absolute: every suffah is considered a place for baytutah because people commonly sleep there at least at some times. The author declares the absolute view as sahih (correct).
The analogy to seasonal apartments (shatawi/sayfi) is drawn.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is the absolute view: entering any suffah breaks the oath. For dihliz, the default is no break unless it is clearly used for overnight stay.
Al-Marghinani says 'wa huwa al-sahih' for the absolute view on suffah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْيَمِينِ فِي الدُّخُولِ وَالسُّكْنَى وَ مَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَدْخُلُ بَيْتًا فَدَخَلَ الْكَعْبَةَ أَوِ الْمَسْجِدَ اَوِ الْبَيْعَةَ أَوِ الْكَنِيسَةَ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لِاَنَّ الْبَيْتَ مَا أُعِدَّ لِلْبَيْتُوتَةِ وَ هذِهِ الْبَقَاعُ مَا بُنِيَتْ لَهَا وَ كَذَا إِذَا دَخَلَ دِهْلِيْرًا اَوْظُلَّةَ بَاب الدَّارِ لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا وَالظُّلَّةُ مَا تَكُونُ عَلَى السِّكَةِ وَقِيلَ إِذَا كَانَ الدِّهْلِيْرُ بِحَيْثُ لَوْ أُعْلِقَ الْبَابُ يَبْقَى دَاخِلاوَهُوَ مُسَقَّفٌ يَحْنِتُ لِأَنَّهُ يُبَاتُ فِيهِ عَادَةً وَإِنْ دَخَلَ صُفَّةٌ حَيْثُ لأَنَّهَا تُبنى لِلْبَيْتُوْتَةِ فِيهِ فِى بَعْضِ الْأَوْقَاتِ فَصَارَ كَالشَّتوى وَالصَّيْفِي وَقِيلَ هَذَا إِذَا كَانَتِ الصُّفَّةُ ذَاتَ حَوَائِطَ اَرْبَعَةٍ وَ هكَذَا كَانَتْ صِفَافُهُمْ وَقِيلَ الْجَوَابُ مَجْرى عَلَى إِطْلَاقِهِ وَهُوَ الصَّحِيحُ
Chapter: on the oath concerning entering and residing. Whoever swears that he will not enter a house, and then enters the Ka'bah, a mosque, a church, or a synagogue, he does not break his oath because a 'house' (bayt) is what is prepared for overnight stay, and these places were not built for that. Similarly, if he enters a vestibule (dihliz) or a canopy (zullah) of the door of the house, he does not break his oath for the reason we mentioned. And the zullah is what is over the street. And it is said: if the vestibule is such that when the door is closed, he remains inside and it is roofed, then he breaks his oath because usually one stays overnight there. And if he enters a portico (suffah), then he breaks his oath because it is built for overnight stay at some times, so it becomes like a winter or summer apartment. And it is said: this applies when the portico has four walls, and such were their porticos. And it is said: the ruling applies absolutely, and that is correct.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This chapter discusses oaths involving entering a 'bayt' (house). The key principle is that the term 'bayt' in its legal context refers to a structure specifically built for overnight stay (baytutah). Therefore, buildings not intended for sleeping, such as the Ka'bah, general mosques, churches, and synagogues, are not included in the term, and entering them does not break the oath. The text then examines borderline structures: a vestibule (dihliz) and a canopy (zullah) are generally not for overnight stay, so entering them does not break the oath. However, there is a reported opinion (qil) that if the vestibule is roofed and can be closed off, it might be used for sleeping, thus breaking the oath. Similarly, a portico (suffah) is often used for sleeping, especially if it has four walls, so entering it does break the oath. The commentary notes that the correct view is the absolute ruling: the portico always counts as a place of overnight stay, in line with common practice of the people of Kufa.

Key Terms
دهليز (dihliz): Vestibule or entrance hall.
ظلة (zullah): Canopy or awning over a street.
صفة (suffah): Portico or veranda used for sitting and sleeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct (sahih) view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on entering a portico (suffah) in the context of an oath not to enter a house?
A) It does not break the oath unless it has four walls.
B) It does not break the oath because it is open on one side.
C) It always breaks the oath because it is used for sleeping at some times.
D) It breaks the oath only if it has a door.

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) plays a key role in defining borderline structures; any space commonly used for sleeping, even if not fully enclosed, breaks an oath not to enter a house.

Residence Oath and Customary Definition of Sukna
Ruling on residence oath with family staying behind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath not to reside in a house is broken, and apply the customary ('urf) definition of residence to similar cases.

Core Idea
Residence is determined by custom – where family and belongings remain, you are still considered a resident.

In this passage, Al-Quduri presents a clear ruling: a man who swears not to reside in a house and leaves alone while his family and property remain is still considered a resident by custom ('urf), and thus he has broken his oath. The commentary expands on this by comparing a shopkeeper who spends all day at work but still identifies his home as his residence. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly applying oaths and avoiding sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A man swears not to reside in a house, then leaves by himself while his family and belongings remain. The ruling: he becomes hanith (sinful, oath-breaker).
The oath is considered broken despite his physical absence.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author provides a proof from custom ('urf): a market-goer (shopkeeper) spends most of the day in the market but still says he resides in a certain alley. This analogy shows that residence is defined by where one's base is, not where one physically stays.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
The legal link is that 'urf considers a person a resident if his family and belongings are present in a place, even if he is temporarily away. Therefore, the oath is interpreted according to this customary meaning, so the person has violated it.
4
Note the scope and application
The same ruling applies to a house (bayt), a quarter (mahallah), and similar dwellings. The principle is that the oath is based on customary residence, not mere physical presence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم کھائی کہ اس گھر میں نہیں رہوں گا خود نکل گیا اور ساز و سامان اس کے اہل و عیال اسی میں ہیں اور لوٹنے کا ارادہ بھی نہیں ہے حانث ہو جائے گا قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَسْكُنُ هَذِهِ الدَّارَ فَخَرَجَ بِنَفْسِهِ وَمَتَاعُهُ وَاهْلُهُ فِيهَا وَلَمْ يُرِدِ الرُّجُوعَ إِلَيْهَا حَيْثُ لِأَنَّهُ يُعَدُّ سَاكِنَا بِبَقَاءِ أَهْلِهِ وَمَتَاعِهِ فِيهَا عُرْفًا فَإِنَّ السُّوقِيَّ عَامَّةَ نَهَارِهِ فِى السُّوقِ وَيَقُولُ أَسْكُنُ سِكَةٌ كَذَا وَالْبَيْتُ وَالْمُحَلَّةُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الدَّارِ ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا اور جس شخص نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں اب اس گھر میں نہیں رہوں گا پھر وہ خود نکل گیا مگر اس کا سامان اور اس کے متعلقین بال بچے) اسی گھر میں رہ گئے۔ ساتھ ہی اس گھر میں اس کے واپس آنے کا ارادہ بھی نہیں ہے پھر بھی وہ حانث ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ اس مکان میں اس کے بال بچے واسباب ہونے سے عرف میں اس گھر کا باشندہ کہلائے گا۔ جیسا کہ ایک بازاری آدمی ( دوکاندارمثلا ) دن بھر بازار میں رہتا ہے لیکن وہ پوچھنے پر کہتا ہے کہ میں فلاں گلی اور فلاں گھر میں رہتا ہوں۔ بہیت اور محلہ کا حکم دار کے حکم کے جیسا ہے۔
He said: And whoever swears, 'I will not reside in this house,' and he leaves by himself while his belongings and family remain in it, and he does not intend to return to it, he is sinful because he is considered a resident due to the continued presence of his family and belongings in it by custom. Indeed, a market-goer spends most of his day in the market and says, 'I reside in such-and-such alley.' The same applies to a house (bayt) and a quarter (mahallah). Translation: Al-Quduri said: And a person who swears, 'I will not live in this house,' then he himself leaves but his belongings and dependents (children) remain in that house, and he also does not intend to return, yet he becomes sinful. Because due to the presence of his children and belongings in that house, by custom he is called a resident of that house. For example, a bazaar-goer (like a shopkeeper) stays in the bazaar all day, but when asked, he says, 'I live in such-and-such alley and such-and-such house.' The ruling for a house and quarters is like that of a dwelling.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from 'urf (custom). The author states the ruling: a person who swears not to reside in a house but leaves while his family and belongings remain, without intending to return, is considered sinful (hanith) because customarily he is still seen as a resident. The proof is that even a shopkeeper who spends all day in the market still identifies his residence by his home. Therefore, the same applies to a house (bayt), a quarter (mahallah), and similar dwellings. The commentary by Ashraf al-Hidayah explains that the oath is broken due to the continuity of residence through family and property, as established in 'urf.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath or solemn promise, here specifically an oath not to reside in a place.
سكنى (sukna): Residence or dwelling, the act of living in a place.
عرف (urf): Custom or common practice used as a legal basis for determining residence.
حانث (hanith): One who breaks or violates an oath, incurring the ruling of sin and obligation of kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears: 'I will not reside in this house.' He leaves by himself while his family and belongings remain in the house, and he does not intend to return. What is the ruling according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) He is not sinful because he left.
B) He is sinful because 'urf considers him a resident as long as his family and belongings are there.
C) He is sinful only if he intends to return.
D) He is not sinful because he did not permanently move out.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to reside is broken if the person leaves alone while family and belongings remain, because 'urf defines residence by the presence of dependents and property.

Fulfilling an Oath Not to Reside: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Transferring Belongings
Intra-Hanafi dispute on transferring belongings to fulfill an oath not to reside in a house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on transferring belongings to fulfill an oath not to reside in a house, and identify the preferred view (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
For an oath not to reside in a house, the Hanafi school offers three views; the preferred view requires only moving essential household goods.

When someone swears not to reside in a city or house, what constitutes fulfillment? The Hanafi school distinguishes between a city and a house, and within the case of a house, there is a well-known internal dispute among the three leading Imams. This passage from al-Hidayah lays out the positions and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between oath on city and on house
Note that for an oath on a city (misr), moving with family suffices by custom; the dispute arises only for an oath on a house (dar).
The passage initially cites Abu Yusuf's view on cities.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa requires moving all belongings, even a peg. His proof: residence is established by the whole, so any remaining item perpetuates residence.
His view is the strictest.
3
State Imam Abu Yusuf's position and proof
Abu Yusuf requires moving the majority of belongings. His proof: transferring all may be impossible.
This is a practical concession.
4
State Imam Muhammad's position and proof
Muhammad requires moving only what is sufficient for household management. His proof: beyond that is not needed for residence.
This view prioritizes custom and ease.
5
Identify the tarjih and thamarah al-khilaf
The mashayikh (later scholars) prefer Muhammad's view as 'ahsan' (best) and 'arfaq' (most convenient). The practical consequence: a person can fulfill the oath by moving only essential items.
This is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم کھائی اس شہر میں نہیں رہوں گا تو بچے اور سامان منتقل کرنے پر موقوف نہیں وَلَوْ كَانَ الْيَمِينُ عَلَى الْمِصْرِ لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ الْبَرُّ عَلَى نَقْلِ الْمَتَاعِ وَالْأَهْلِ فِيْمَارُوِيَ عَنْ أَبِي يُوْسُفَ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُعَدُّ سَاكِنَ فِي الَّذِي انْتَقَلَ عَنْهُ عُرْفًا بِخِلَافِ الْأَوَّلِ وَالْقَرْيَةِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمِصْرِ فِي الصَّحِيحِ مِنَ الْجَوَابِ ثُمَّ قَالَ ابو حَنِيفَةَ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ نَقْلِ كُلِ الْمَتَاعِ حَتَّى لَوْبَقِيَ وَتَدْيَحْنَتُ لأنَّ السُّكْنى قَدْثَبَتَ بِالْكُلَّ فَيَبْقَى مَابَقِيَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفٌ يُعْتَبَرُ نَقُلُ الْأَكْثَرِ لَاتَ نَقْلَ الْكُلِّ قَدْيَتَعَدَّرُ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ يُعْتَبَرُ نَقْلُ مَا يَقُومُ بِهِ كَدْ خَذَا نِيَتِهِ لِأَنَّ مَاوَرَاءَ ذَالِكَ لَيْسَ مِنَ السُّكْنى قَالُوا هَذَا أَحْسَنُ وَاَرْفَقُ بِالنَّاسِ وَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَنْتَقِلَ إِلَى مَنْزِلِ اخَرَبِلَا تَاخِيْرِ حَتَّى يَرْفَإِنِ انْتَقَلَ إِلَى السَّكَةِ أَوْإِلَى الْمَسْجِدِ قَالُوا لَا يُبَرُّدَ لِيْلُهُ فِي الزِيَادَاتِ أَنَّ مَنْ خَرَجَ بِعَمَالِهِ مِنْ مِصْرِهِ فَمَالَمْ يَتَّخِذُ وَطَنَا اخَرَيَبْقَى وَظُنُّهُ الْأَوَّلُ فِي حَقِّ الصَّلَوةِ كَذَا هَذَا. ترجمہ اور اگر یہ سم کھائی کہ میں اس شہر میں نہیں رہوں گا تو قسم میں سچا ہونا بال بچے اور اسباب کو منتقل کرنے پر موقوف نہیں ہے۔ جیسا کہ امام ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے۔ کیونکہ جس شہر سے منتقل ہو گیا ہے۔ عرف میں اس شہر کا باشندہ نہیں کہلاتا ہے۔ بخلاف پہلی صورت کے اور صحیح قول کے مطابق گاؤں کا حکم شہر کے حکم کے جیسا ہے۔ پھر امام اعظم نے فرمایا ہے کہ تم میں سچا ہونے کے لئے گھر کے ایک ایک سامان کا منتقل کر لینا ضروری ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر میخ ( کیل ) بھی گھر میں رہ گئی تو وہ حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ گھر میں رہائش پورے اسباب سے ثابت ہوئی تھی۔ تو جب تک اس اسباب میں سے کچھ بھی وہاں باقی رہے گا وہاں کی رہائش باقی کبھی جائے گی۔ اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا ہے کہ اکثر سامان منتقل کر لینا ہی کافی ہے۔ کیونکہ کبھی تو پورے سامان و منتقل کر لینا نا ممکن ہو جاتا ہے (اس پر فتویٰ ہے۔ جیسا کہ کافی میں ہے ) اور امام محمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ جتنے سامان کے منتقل کر لینے سے خانہ داری کا انتظام پورا ہو سکتا ہے۔ اس کو منتقل کر لینا کافی ہوگا۔ کیونکہ سکونت کے لئے اس سے زیادہ سامان کا ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے۔ مشایخ نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ قول بہت خوب اور لوگوں کے عملی طور پر زیادہ آسان ہے۔ واضح ہو کہ ایسے شخص کے لئے یہ لازم ہوگا کہ وہ قسم کے فورا بعد بلا تاخیر اپنا سامان دوسرے مکان میں منتقل کرنا شروع کر دے تا کہ وہ اپنی قسم میں سچا ہو۔ اور اگر اس نے اپنا سامان گھر سے نکال کر لی کو چہ میں یا مسجد میں منتقل کیا تو بھی قسم میں سچا نہ ہوگا۔ زیادات میں اس کی دلیل مروی ہے کہ وہ شخص جو اپنے بال بچوں کو لے کر دوسرے شہر میں گیا تو نماز کے معاملہ میں مقیم اور مسافر کے سلسلہ میں) پہلا وطن باقی رہے گا ۔ جب تک کہ دوسرے کو وطن نہ بنا لے ایسا ہی یہاں بھی ہے۔
And if the oath is regarding the city (misr), fulfilling the oath does not depend on transferring the belongings and family, according to what is transmitted from Abu Yusuf, because he is not considered a resident of that city by custom after moving, unlike the first case. A village (qaryah) is like a city in the correct answer. Then Abu Hanifa said: It is necessary to transfer all belongings, even if a peg remains, he becomes sinful, because residence is established by the whole, so it remains as long as anything remains. Abu Yusuf said: Transferring the majority is sufficient, not all, because transferring all may be impossible. Muhammad said: Transferring what is sufficient for running the household is sufficient, because beyond that is not part of residence. They said: This is best and most convenient for people. He should move to another house without delay until... if he moves to an alley or a mosque, they said he does not fulfill his oath. In al-Ziyadat: He who leaves his city with his family, until he takes another homeland, his first homeland remains in the matter of prayer; similarly here. Translation: And if he swears, 'I will not reside in this city,' then being truthful in the oath does not depend on transferring children and belongings, as transmitted from Imam Abu Yusuf. Because by custom, one is not called a resident of the city from which he has moved. Unlike the first case, and according to the correct view, a village is like a city. Then Imam Abu Hanifa stated: To be truthful in the oath, it is necessary to transfer every single item of the house, even if a nail remains, he becomes sinful. Because residence was established by all the items, so whenever any part remains, the residence continues. Imam Abu Yusuf said: Transferring the majority is sufficient, because sometimes transferring all is impossible (and this is the fatwa, as in al-Kafi). Imam Muhammad said: Transferring whatever is necessary to run the household is sufficient, because more than that is not needed for residence. The mashayikh said: This saying is excellent and more practical for people. It is clear that such a person must start moving his belongings to another house immediately after the oath, without delay, so that he fulfills his oath. If he moves his belongings from the house to an alley or a mosque, he does not fulfill the oath. In al-Ziyadat, an analogy is transmitted: a person who takes his children to another city, in the matter of prayer, his first homeland remains until he takes another homeland; similarly here.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath not to reside in a city (misr) versus a house. For a city, according to Abu Yusuf, moving with family is sufficient because custom does not consider him a resident after relocation; the oath is fulfilled without moving all belongings. For a house, Abu Hanifa requires moving all belongings, down to the smallest item, since residence is established by the entirety of possessions. Abu Yusuf considers moving the majority sufficient due to practicality. Muhammad considers moving enough to run the household sufficient, as extra items are not essential for residence. The mashayikh prefer Muhammad's view for its ease. The commentary also notes that if belongings are moved to an alley or mosque, the oath is not fulfilled, drawing an analogy from travel rules: one's original homeland remains in effect until a new home is established.

Key Terms
مصر (misr): A large city or metropolis; in this context, the subject of an oath to leave or not reside.
بر (birr): Fulfillment of an oath, being truthful and righteous in keeping it.
نقل المتاع (naql al-mata'): Transfer of belongings; the act of moving household goods to another location to break residence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, what is sufficient to fulfill an oath not to reside in a house?
A) Moving all belongings including the smallest items.
B) Moving the majority of belongings.
C) Moving only what is necessary for household management.
D) Simply leaving the house with family.

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view in fulfilling an oath not to reside in a house is to move only the possessions necessary for household management, as this balances legal precision with practical convenience.

Oath on Entering a Mosque: Rebuilding Scenarios
Rulings on oath not to enter a mosque that is later rebuilt or altered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios where entering a rebuilt or altered mosque does or does not break an oath, and apply the reasoning to new cases.

Core Idea
The identity of a mosque for oaths persists if rebuilt on the same consecrated site but not if first converted to other use.

When a person swears not to enter a specific mosque, what happens if the building is later demolished, extended, or even converted? The Hanafi scholars have given detailed rulings distinguishing between changes that preserve the mosque's identity and those that do not. These rulings hinge on whether the original waqf (consecration) remains intact. In this lesson, we will examine four scenarios from the classical text and understand the principle behind each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
The passage lists four distinct situations: (1) mosque demolished and rebuilt on same site, (2) mosque turned into a house then rebuilt, (3) extension added, (4) entry via the roof.
Memorize these four to apply the rulings correctly.
2
Determine ruling for each
For each scenario, note whether the person becomes hanith (sinful) or not: (1) hanith, (2) not hanith, (3) not hanith, (4) not hanith (provided non-Arab custom).
The ruling in (4) is conditional on the custom of the person.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The key is continuity of the mosque's identity: as long as the original consecration (waqf) remains on the same site, the oath holds. Converting to a house extinguishes the waqf; extensions and roofs are considered outside the mosque proper.
This principle explains why scenario 1 is hanith and scenario 2 is not.
4
Apply to hypothetical new cases
Test your understanding by imagining new modifications (e.g., relocation, partial collapse) and decide based on whether the consecrated site remains unchanged.
This step solidifies the principle for real-world fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل نمبر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ اس مسجد میں نہیں جاؤں گا۔ بعد میں جبکہ وہ مسجد گرا کر دوبارہ بنائی گئی اور اس میں داخل ہوا تو وہ حانث ہو گیا۔ نمبر اور اگر وہ ٹوٹ پھوٹ گئی اور اسے کسی نے گھر بنا لیا۔ پھر اسے بھی تو ڑ کر دوبارہ مسجد بنائی گئی تو اس میں داخل ہونے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ نمبر اور اگر اصلی مسجد میں کچھ حصہ اور بڑھایا گیا پھر اس حصہ میں وہ داخل ہوا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ نمبر اور اگر یہ تم کائی کہ مسجد میں نہیں جاؤں گا۔ پھر کسی طرح اس کی چھت پر چلا گیا تو وہ حالت نہیں ہوگا بشرطیکہ وہ بھی یعنی وہ غیر عربی ہو۔ اسی پر فتوی ہے۔
Issues: Number: Someone swore, 'I will not enter this mosque.' Later, the mosque was demolished and rebuilt, and he entered it, so he became sinful. Number: And if it became dilapidated and someone made it a house, then it was demolished and rebuilt as a mosque, then entering it does not make him sinful. Number: And if an extension was added to the original mosque and he entered that part, he is not sinful. Number: And if he swore, 'I will not enter the mosque,' then somehow he went onto its roof, he is not sinful provided that he is non-Arab? The fatwa is on this.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage lists several rulings (masail) related to oaths of not entering a mosque. The rulings are: (1) If the mosque is demolished and rebuilt on the same site, the oath still applies and entry is sinful. (2) If the mosque was first turned into a house and later rebuilt as a mosque, the oath is no longer binding because the building's identity changed. (3) An extension to the original mosque is considered a different place, so entry does not break the oath. (4) Going onto the roof of the mosque does not count as entering the mosque itself; the author notes this is the fatwa provided the person is non-Arab (perhaps meaning that in Arabic custom, roof is considered separate).

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): A mosque, the place of worship; in oath rulings, its identity changes if the building is completely repurposed and then rebuilt.
حانث (hanith): One who breaks an oath; in these scenarios, entry after rebuilding on same site makes one hanith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears not to enter a mosque. Later, the mosque is demolished and rebuilt on the same site. He then enters. According to the Hanafi ruling, what is his status?
A) He is not sinful because the building is new.
B) He is sinful because the mosque identity continues.
C) He is sinful only if the prayer hall is rebuilt.
D) He is not sinful if the minaret is changed.

Key Takeaway
The continuity of a mosque's legal identity for oaths depends on whether the consecrated site was maintained or extinguished.

Rulings on Oaths Not to Enter a House
Rulings on oath not to enter a house with variations of coercion, partial entry, and terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an oath not to enter a house is broken (hanith) according to the Hanafi school, distinguishing voluntary from involuntary entry, partial entry, and the effect of local custom on interpreting the term 'bayt'.

Core Idea
An oath is broken only by voluntary, complete entry; custom determines the meaning of 'house'.

This passage from a later Hanafi fatwa collection presents a series of practical scenarios concerning an oath not to enter a house. The rulings clarify what constitutes a violation (hanath) of the oath, focusing on the voluntariness of entry, the extent of entry, and the crucial role of local custom in interpreting the term used in the oath. Understanding these distinctions is essential for issuing accurate fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule for voluntary entry
If the person enters willingly, even if carried by another on his order, the oath is broken.
2
Identify the exception for involuntary entry
If entry occurs against his will (e.g., horse bolts, carried without order, falls), the oath is not broken.
The text lists several examples: a bolting horse, being carried without command, slipping and falling, or being blown by wind.
3
Define what constitutes 'entry'
Partial entry (one foot) does not count; full entry requires the whole body or both feet.
The passage explicitly states that inserting only one foot does not make one hanith.
4
Analyze the scenario of forced exit and re-entry
If someone is forced in, then leaves, then voluntarily re-enters, the oath is broken upon the voluntary re-entry.
This shows that involuntary initial entry does not taint the act; later volition triggers hanath.
5
Apply the rule for interpreting ambiguous terms
If the oath uses 'bayt', the default meaning is 'room' (Arabic idiom), but local custom (urf) dictates the actual meaning. In non-Arab lands, 'bayt' often means 'house', so entering the courtyard breaks the oath.
The fatwa follows local custom, as stated by the mashayikh and recorded in 'Ayni and Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نمبر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں اس گھر میں نہیں جاؤں گا۔ پھر وہ سوار ہو کر یا پیدل یا اس کے کہنے سے اسے کوئی اٹھا کر لے گیا تو وہ حانث ہو جائے گا۔ نمبر اور اگر وہ گھوڑے پر سوارتھا کہ اچانک گھوڑ اہدک کر اس کے روکتے رہنے کے باوجود اسے اس گھر میں لے گیا تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ نمبر اور اگر کوئی دوسرا شخص اس کو اس کے حکم کے بغیر اٹھا کر لے گیا تو بھی حانث نہ ہو گا۔ خواہ دل سے راضی ہو یا راضی نہ ہو۔ خواہ روک سکتا ہو یا نہیں۔ عامہ مشائخ کا یہی قول ہے۔ اور یہی صحیح ہے۔ نمبر اور اگر صرف ایک پاؤں اندر داخل کیا تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ یہی صحیح ہے۔ نمبر اور اگر پھل کر کچھ گھر میں گر پڑایا ہوا کے جھونکے نے اسے اندر گرا دیا میچ یہ ہے کہحان نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر کوئی اسے زبردستی لے گیا پھر نکل آیا۔ پھر اپنے اختیار سے اندر گیا تو فتویٰ یہ ہے کہ حانث ہو جائے گا۔ نمبر ا قسم کھائی کہ اس بیت میں داخل نہ ہوں گا۔ اور اس کی کچھ نیت نہیں ہے پھر وہ اس احاطہ کے اندر آیا جس میں یہ بیت واقع ہے تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔ مشایخ نے کہا ہے کہ وہ عرب کے محاورہ کے مطابق ہے۔ یعنی ان کے محاورہ میں بہیت ایک کمرہ ہے۔ اور جس میں کئی کمرے ہوں وہ منزل ہے اور جس میں کئی منزلیں ہوں وہ دار ہے ۔ مگر ہمارے عرف میں دار و منزل و بیت سب کو گھر ہی کہتے ہیں۔ اس لیئے وہ صحن میں جانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور اس پر فتویٰ ہے۔ یہی عینی و فتح القدیر وغیرہ فقہ کی کتابوں میں مذکور ہے۔
Number: Someone swore, 'I will not enter this house.' Then he entered riding, walking, or was carried by someone on his order, so he becomes sinful. Number: And if he was riding a horse and the horse suddenly bolted and took him into the house despite his efforts to stop, he is not sinful. Number: And if another person carried him without his order, he is not sinful, whether he was pleased internally or not, whether he could have prevented it or not. This is the view of the majority of mashayikh, and it is correct. Number: And if he inserted only one foot, he is not sinful; this is correct. Number: And if he slipped and fell into the house (e.g., blown by wind), he is not sinful. And if someone forced him in, then he left, then he entered by his own choice, the fatwa is that he becomes sinful. Number: He swore, 'I will not enter this bayt (room),' with no specific intention, then he entered the courtyard in which the bayt is located, he is not sinful. The mashayikh said that this is according to Arabic idiom, where 'bayt' means a single room, while a multi-room building is 'manzil' and a multi-manzil building is 'dar'. However, in our custom, dar, manzil, and bayt all mean 'house'. Therefore, he becomes sinful upon entering the courtyard, and on this is the fatwa. This is mentioned in 'Ayni, Fath al-Qadir, and other fiqh books.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage provides multiple rulings on oaths not to enter a house (or bayt). Key points: (1) Voluntary entry, whether walking, riding, or carried by someone at his command, breaks the oath. (2) Involuntary entry, such as a bolting horse or being carried without consent, does not break the oath. (3) Partial entry (one foot) is not considered full entry. (4) Forced entry followed by voluntary re-entry after leaving breaks the oath. (5) A crucial distinction between 'bayt' (room) and 'dar' (house) based on Arabic usage vs. local custom. In Arabic, 'bayt' is a room, while 'dar' is a house; so entering the courtyard of a dar does not break an oath on 'bayt'. But in local (non-Arab) custom, all terms are used interchangeably for 'house', so entering the courtyard would break the oath. The fatwa follows local custom.

Key Terms
بیت (bayt): Specifically a room within a house; in Arabic idiom it means a single dwelling unit, but in common usage it often means 'house'.
دخول (dukhul): Entry; full entry requires both feet or the entire body; partial entry like one foot does not count.
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion; being forced or carried involuntarily does not break an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not enter this house.' Another person carries him into the house without his order, but he is internally pleased. According to the Hanafi ruling, what is his status?
A) He becomes sinful because he was pleased.
B) He becomes sinful because he did not resist.
C) He is not sinful because he did not enter voluntarily.
D) He is not sinful if he could not prevent it.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to enter a house is broken only by voluntary, complete entry; interpretation of the term 'house' follows local custom.

Oath on Wife's Exit: Permission and Yameen Fawr
Ruling on oath regarding wife's exit: each exit needs permission and yameen fawr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two exception clauses ('illa bi-idhnihi vs 'illa an adhana) in oaths and apply the Hanafi concept of yameen fawr to determine whether an oath is violated by delayed action.

Core Idea
Each exit needs permission unless the oath phrase ends it; immediate oaths only apply to the situation at hand.

Oaths about a wife's exit can be tricky: one permission does not cover all future exits unless the wording explicitly ends the oath. Moreover, a conditional oath like 'if you go out' may only target the imminent action, thanks to the principle of yameen fawr. This lesson unpacks these nuances from al-Quduri's matn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath phrases
Recognize 'illa bi-idhnihi' (except with my permission) vs 'illa an adhana' (unless I give you permission) and their distinct legal effects.
The first requires renewal of permission per exit; the second ends the oath with one permission.
2
Trace the legal reasoning
Understand why each exit needs permission (exception only applies to the permitted act) and why 'illa an adhana' acts as a ghayah (finality).
Al-Quduri explains: 'illa an adhana' is like 'hatta' (until), ending the oath.
3
Distinguish yameen fawr
Learn why a conditional oath ('if you go out, you are divorced') does not apply to a delayed action; it is tied to the immediate situation.
Abu Hanifa uniquely articulated this, but it is based on custom (urf).
4
Apply custom (urf)
See how Hanafi reliance on custom overrides literal interpretation of conditional statements in oaths.
The text states: 'mabna al-ayman 'alayhi' – oaths are built upon custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا تَخْرُجُ اِمْرَأَتُهُ إِلَّا بِإِذْنِهِ فَأَذِنَ لَهَا مَرَّةً فَخَرَجَتْ ثُمَّ خَرَجَتْ مَرَّةً أُخْرَى بِغَيْرِ إِذْنِهِ حَنِثَ وَلَا بُدَّ مِنَ الْإِذْنِ فِي كُلِّ خُرُوجٍ لِأَنَّ الْمُسْتَثْنَى خُرُوجٌ مَقْرُونٌ بِالْإِذْنِ وَمَا وَرَاءَهُ دَاخِلٌ فِي الْحَظْرِ الْعَامِ وَلَوْ نَوَى الْإِذْنَ مَرَّةً يُصَدَّقُ دِيَانَةً لَا قَضَاءً لِأَنَّهُ مُحْتَمَلُ كَلَامِهِ لَكِنَّهُ خِلَافُ الظَّاهِرِ وَلَوْ قَالَ إِلَّا أَنْ آذَنَ لَكِ فَأَذِنَ لَهَا مَرَّةً وَاحِدَةً فَخَرَجَتْ ثُمَّ خَرَجَتْ بَعْدَهَا بِغَيْرِ إِذْنِهِ لَمْ يَحْنَثْ لِأَنَّ هَذِهِ كَلِمَةُ غَايَةٍ فَيَنْتَهِي الْيَمِينُ بِهِ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَ حَتَّى آذَنَ لَكِ وَلَوْ أَرَادَتِ الْمَرْأَةُ الْخُرُوجَ فَقَالَ إِنْ خَرَجْتِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ فَجَلَسَتْ ثُمَّ خَرَجَتْ لَمْ يَحْنَثْ وَكَذَلِكَ إِنْ أَرَادَ رَجُلٌ ضَرْبَ عَبْدِهِ فَقَالَ لَهُ آخَرُ إِنْ ضَرَبْتَهُ فَعَبْدِي حُرٌّ فَتَرَكَهُ ثُمَّ ضَرَبَهُ وَهَذِهِ تُسَمَّى يَمِينَ فَوْرٍ وَتَفَرَّدَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ بِإِظْهَارِهِ وَوَجْهُهُ أَنَّ مُرَادَ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ الرَّدُّ عَنْ تِلْكَ الضَّرْبَةِ وَالْخَرْجَةِ عُرْفًا وَمَبْنَى الْأَيْمَانِ عَلَيْهِ
He said: And whoever swears, 'His wife shall not go out except with his permission,' then he gave her permission once, and she went out, then she went out another time without his permission, he becomes hanith. Permission is necessary for each exit, because the excepted thing is an exit accompanied by permission, and what is beyond that is included in the general prohibition. And if he intended permission once, he is believed diyanatan, not judicially, because it is a possible meaning of his words, but it is contrary to the apparent meaning. And if he said, 'Unless I give you permission,' and he gave her permission once, and she went out, then she went out after that without his permission, he does not become hanith, because this word is a term of finality (ghayah), so the oath ends with it, just as if he said, 'Until I give you permission.' And if a woman intended to go out, and he said, 'If you go out, you are divorced,' and she sat (did not go), then later went out, he does not become hanith. Similarly, if a man intended to beat his slave, and another said to him, 'If you beat him, my slave is free,' and he left him, then later beat him. This is called yameen fawr (immediate oath). Abu Hanifa alone made it manifest. Its reasoning is that the speaker's intent, by custom, is to deter that specific beating or exit, and oaths are built upon custom.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings from al-Quduri on oaths related to a wife's exit. First ruling: if a man swears his wife will not go out except with his permission, and he gives permission once, then she goes out again without new permission, he becomes hanith. Reason: the exception applies only to an exit that is explicitly permitted; each subsequent exit requires its own permission. If he intended that one permission suffices, he is believed diyanatan (before Allah) but not in court because his intention contradicts the apparent meaning. Second: if he uses the phrase 'unless I give you permission' (illa an adhana laki), then once he gives permission, the oath ends—any later exit without permission does not cause hanath, because 'illa' here acts as a ghayah (limit), similar to 'hatta' (until). Third: yameen fawr: if the husband says 'if you go out' at a moment when she is about to go out, and she delays then later goes out, no hanath. Similarly, if someone says 'if you beat your slave, my slave is free' when beating is imminent, and the beater desists then later beats, no hanath. This is called yameen fawr (immediate oath) because the condition is tied to the immediate situation. Imam Abu Hanifa uniquely articulated this principle, though others may accept it in practice. The basis is custom (urf): the speaker's aim is to prevent that specific action, not future actions.

Key Terms
يمين فور (yameen fawr): An oath that is tied to an immediate, specific act; if the act is delayed, the oath is not violated.
حظر عام (hazr 'amm): The general prohibition that applies to all exits except those explicitly excepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal effect if a husband says 'if you go out, you are divorced' when his wife is about to go out, but she sits down and then goes out later?
A) Divorce occurs immediately
B) Divorce occurs when she later goes out
C) No divorce occurs
D) It depends on her intention

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the wording of an oath determines its scope; custom overrides literalism, and an immediate condition does not bind future actions.

Oath to Drink from an Empty Cup: The Condition of Possibility
Ruling on oath to drink water from empty cup and its invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of an oath when the sworn act is impossible at the time of swearing, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
An oath to do the impossible is invalid — no sin, no expiation.

Oaths are meant to be fulfilled. But what if you swear to do something impossible at the very moment you swear — like drinking water from an empty cup? This passage from al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether such an oath is valid at all. Understanding this dispute reveals a key principle in Hanafi usul: the condition of تصور البر (conceptual possibility of fulfillment) for the validity of an oath.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding the oath to drink from an empty cup.
Note that they consider the oath invalid from the start.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State Abu Yusuf's differing view: the oath is valid and he becomes sinful after the day passes.
Abu Yusuf also distinguishes between absolute and time-bound oaths.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain Abu Yusuf's dalil: the oath can be concluded because a substitute (kafara) is possible.
He argues that the swearer can still pay expiation even if the original act is impossible.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Show how Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rebut this: the original act must be conceptually possible for a substitute to exist.
They compare it to a false oath (al-ghamus) which does not require expiation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: whether the swearer becomes sinful and must pay expiation.
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, no sin or kafara; according to Abu Yusuf, sin and kafara.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين فى الاكل والشرب.
۹۲
. اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم
قسم کھائی ان لم اشرب الماء الذي في هذا الكوز اليوم فامراته طالق اور
کوزے میں پانی نہیں تھا حانث نہیں ہوگا
وَ مَنْ قَالَ إِنْ لَمْ أَشْرَبِ الْمَاءَ الَّذِي فِى هَذَا الْكُوزِ الْيَوْمَ فَامْرَأَتُهُ طَالِقٌ وَلَيْسَ فِي الْكُوْزِ مَاءً لَمْ يَحْنِثُ فَإِنْ كَانَ فِيْهِ مَاءً فَاهْرِيق قَبْلَ اللَّيْلِ لَمْ يَحْنِتُ وَهِذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَ مُحَمَّدٌ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوْسُفٌ فِي ذَالِكَ كُلِهِ يَعْنِي إِذَا مَضَى الْيَوْمَ وَعَلَى هَذَا الْخِلَافِ إِذَا كَانَ الْيَمِينُ بِاللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَأَصْلُهُ أَنَّ شَرْطَ انْعِقَادِ الْيَمِيْنِ وَبَقَائِهِ التَّصَوُّرُ عِنْدَهُمَا خِلَافًا لَابِى يُوسُفَ لِاَنَّ الْيَمِينَ إِنَّمَا تُعْقَدُ لِلْبَرِ فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْبِرِّلِيُمْكِنَ إِيْجَابُهُ وَلَهُ أَنَّهُ أَمْكَنَ الْقَوْلُ بِالْعِقَادِهِ مُوْجِبًا لِلْبِرِّ عَلَى وَجْهِ يَظْهَرُ فِي حَقِ الْخَلْفِ وَهُوَا لْكَفَّارَةُ قُلْنَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْأَصْلِ لِيَنْعَقِدَ فِي حَقِّ الْخَلْفِ وَهَذَا لَا يَنْعَقِدُ الْغُمُوسُ مُوجِبًا لِلْكَفَّارَةِ وَلَوْ كَانَتِ الْيَمِينُ مُطْلَقَةً فَفِي الْوَجْهِ الْاَوَّلِ لَا يَحْنِتُ عِنْدَهُمَا وَعِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفٌ يَحْنِتُ فِي الْحَالِ وَفِي الْوَجْهِ الثَّانِي يَحْنِتُ فِي قَوْلِهِمْ جَمِيعًا فَابُوْيُوسُفَ فَرَّقَ بَيْنَ الْمُطْلَقِ وَالْمُوَقَتِ وَوَجَهُ الْفَرْقِ اَنَّ التَّاقِيتَ لِلتَّوَسُعَةِ فَلَا يَجِبُ الْفِعْلُ إِلَّا فِي آخِرِ الْوَقْتِ فَلَا يَحْنِتُ قَبْلُهُ وَفِي الْمُطْلَقِ يَجِبُ الْبَرَّكَمَا فَرَغَ وَقَدْ عَجَزَ فَيَحْنِتُ فِي الْحَالِ وَهُمَا فَرَّقَا بَيْنَهُمَا وَوَجْهُ الْفَرْقِ اَنَّ فِي الْمُطْلَقِ يَجِبُ الْبَرَّ كَمَا فَرَغَ فَإِذَافَاتَ الْبَرُّ بِفَوَاتِ مَاعُقِدَ عَلَيْهِ الْيَمِينُ يَحْنِتُ فِي يَمِينِهِ كَمَا إِذَامَاتَ الْحَالِفُ وَالْمَاءُ بَاقِ أَمَّا فِي الْمُوَقَتِ يَجِبُ الْبَرُّفِي الْجُزْءِ الْآخِيْرِ مِنَ الْوَقْتِ وَعِنْدَهُ ذَالِكَ لَمْ يَبْقَ مَحَلَّيَّةِ الْبَرِّ لِعَدْمِ التَّصَوُّرِ فَلَا يَجبُ الْبَرُّفِيهِ وَتَبْطُلُ الْيَمِينُ كَمَا إِذَا عَقَدَهُ ابْتِدَاءً فِي هَذِهِ الْحَالَةِ
ترجمہ اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ اگر میں آج وہ پانی نہ پیوں جو اس پیالہ میں ہے تو میری بیوی کو طلاق ہے۔ حالانکہ وہ پیالہ بالکل خالی ہے تو وہ حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر اس میں پانی ہو مگر رات آنے سے پہلے ہی وہ بہا دیا گیا تب بھی حانث نہیں ہو گا۔ یہ حکم امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد کے ہاں ہے۔ لیکن امام ابو یوسف نے کہا ہے کہ وہ ان تمام صورتوں میں جانث ہو جائے گا۔ یعنی دن گذر جانے کے بعد حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح اگر بیوی کی طلاق کی قسم نہ ہو بلکہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے نام کی قسم ہو تو بھی یہی اختلاف ہے۔ یعنی اگر یہ قسم کھائی کہ واللہ آج کے دن اس پیالہ میں جو پانی ہے اسے پیوں گا۔ تو اس صورت میں بھی یہی اختلاف ہے۔ اور اس اختلاف کی اصل یہ ہے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ و امام محمد کے نزدیک قسم کے منعقد ہونے پھر اس کے باقی رہنے کی شرط یہ ہے کہ اس قسم کو پورا کرنے کا تصور بھی ہو اور امام ابویوسف کے نزدیک اس کی شرط نہیں ہے۔ ان دونوں کی یعنی طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ قسم تو اس لئے کھائی اور مانی جاتی ہے کہ وہ پوری کی جائے۔ اس لئے یہ بات ضروری ہوئی کہ اس کا پورا ہو ناممکن بھی ہوتا کہ قسم واجب کی جائے۔ اور امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ یہاں صرف یہ کہنا ممکن ہے کہ قسم منعقد ہوئی ہے۔ ساتھ ہی اسے پورا کرنا بھی واجب ہے۔ مگر اس طرح سے کہ اس کے خلیفہ میں اثر ظاہر ہو۔ اور اس قسم کا خلیفہ اس کا کفارہ ہوتا ہے۔ ( یعنی جب کہنے والے نے زبان سے قسم نکالی تو ہم بھی اسے فضول اور اور لغو نہیں ہونے دیں گے۔ اب اگر اسے پورا کرنے کا تصور نہ ہو سکے تو کم از کم اس کا کفارہ دینا تو ممکن ہے۔ لہذا اس قسم کا فائدہ یہ حاصل ہوگا کہ وہ کفارہ ادا کر دے۔ اور ہم اس کا جواب یہ دیتے ہیں کہ (خلیفہ ہمیشہ کسی اصل کا ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے اصل کا ایسا ہونا ضروری ہے کہ اس کا تصور کرناممکن بھی ہو۔ تا کہ خلیفہ کے حق میں وہ پایا بھی جائے۔ اس بناء پر قسم غموس کو کفارہ لازم کرنے کا سبب قرار نہیں دیتے ہیں۔ فائدہ یعنی قسم غموس ہونے کی صورت میں یہ نہیں کہا جاتا ہے کہ اگر اصل قسم پوری نہیں ہو سکتی ہے تو کفارہ کو اس کا خلیفہ مان لیا جائے۔ اس لئے

اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم
۹۳
باب اليمين في الاكل والشرب
کہ جب اصل ہی کا وجود نہ ہو تو خلیفہ کا وجود کہاں سے ہوگا۔ اگر اس مذکورہ مسئلہ میں یعنی پیالہ سے پانی پینے کی قسم میں اگر آج کی قید نہ ہوتو پہلی صورت میں یعنی جب کہ پیالہ میں پانی نہیں تھا تو بھی امام ابو حنیفہ امام محمد کے نزدیک حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ البتہ امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک وہ فی الفور حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اور دوسری صورت میں یعنی جبکہ پیالہ کا موجود پانی بہا دیا گیا ہے تو بالا اتفاق سب کے نزدیک حانث ہو جائے گا۔ پس امام ابو یوسف نے ان دونوں صورتوں میں اس طرح فرق کیا ہے کہ اس صورت میں جب کہ آج کی قید لگی ہوئی ہو تو دن گزرتے ہی حانث ٹھہرا اور اس صورت میں کہ وقت کی قید لگی ہوئی نہ ہو تو فی الفور حانث ٹھہرا اور فرق کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ کوئی بھی وقت اس لئے مقرر کیا جاتا ہے کہ اس وقت کے ختم ہونے تک گنجائش رہتی ہے۔ اس لئے جب تک کہ آخری وقت نہ آجائے پیالہ سے پانی پینے کی گنجائش باقی رہیگی ۔ یعنی جب دن گزرے۔ بس دن گزرنے سے پہلے وہ حانث نہ ہوگا۔ اور جس صورت میں وقت کی قید نہ ہو اس میں قسم سے فارغ ہوتے ہی اس کا پورا واجب کرنا ہو گا۔ حالانکہ وہ پانی پینے سے عاجز ہے کیونکہ پیالہ بالکل خالی ہے۔ اس لئے وہ فوزا حانث ہو گیا اور امام البوحنیفہ محمد نے بھی مطلق اور مقید وقت کے درمیان فرق کیا ہے۔ اس میں فرق کرنے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ جس قسم میں وقت کی قید نہیں ہے وہاں قسم سے فارغ ہوتے ہی تم کو پورا کرنایعنی مثل پیلہ کا پانی پینا واجب ہو جاتا ہے۔ اب جبکہ پیالہ میں پانی نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے جس پر قسم کی بنیاد ہے پورا کر ناشتم ہو گیا تو یہ شخص اپنی قسم میں جھوٹا ہو گیا۔ جیسے کہ پیالہ میں یہ پانی رہتے ہوئے قسم کھانے والا اچانک مرجائے تو وہ بھی حانث ہو جاتا ہے اور جس صورت میں وقت کی قید ہے۔ مثلاً آج کے دن کی قید لگائی ہے اس قسم کو پورا کرنا اس وقت سے آخری حصہ میں واجب ہوگا۔ اور آخری حصہ میں داخل ہو جانے میں قسم پوری کرنے کا موقع اور محل باقی نہیں رہتا۔ کیونکہ اب قسم کے پوری کرنے کا تصور بھی نہیں ہو سکتا ہے۔ اس لئے اسے پورا کرنا بھی واجب نہیں ہو
گا اور قسم ہی باطل ہو جائے گی۔ جیسے پانی نہ ہونے کی صورت میں اگر شروع میں قسم منعقد ہو بھی گئی تب بھی باطل ہے۔ فائدہ یعنی جیسے قسم باقی ہونے کے لئے یہ شرط ہے کہ اسے پورا کرناممکن بھی ہوناں ساری قسم و تر ہونے کے واسطے بھی یہ شرط ہے کہ محل موجود ہو۔
Chapter of oath regarding eating and drinking. Whoever says: If I do not drink the water that is in this cup today, then his wife is divorced, and there is no water in the cup, he does not become sinful. If there was water in it and it was spilled before night, he does not become sinful. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said regarding all of that: meaning when the day passes, and upon this difference is when the oath is by Allah the Exalted. The origin is that the condition for the validity of the oath and its continuation is the possibility (imagining) according to them, contrary to Abu Yusuf, because the oath is only taken for fulfillment, so the possibility of fulfillment is necessary so that it can be made obligatory. For him (Abu Yusuf), it is possible to say that the oath is concluded making fulfillment obligatory in a way that appears in the substitute, which is the expiation. We said: The origin must be imaginable for the oath to be concluded regarding the substitute. This does not make a false oath (al-ghamus) a cause for expiation. If the oath is absolute, then in the first case he does not become sinful according to them, and according to Abu Yusuf he becomes sinful immediately. In the second case he becomes sinful according to all of them. So Abu Yusuf distinguished between the absolute and the time-bound. The reason for distinction is that specification of time is for ease, so the act is not obligatory except at the end of the time, so he does not become sinful before it. In the absolute, fulfillment becomes obligatory as soon as he finishes, and he is incapable, so he becomes sinful immediately. They (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) distinguished between them. The reason for distinction is that in the absolute, fulfillment becomes obligatory as soon as he finishes, so when fulfillment is lost due to the loss of what the oath was concluded upon, he becomes sinful in his oath, just as when the swearer dies and the water remains. As for the time-bound, fulfillment becomes obligatory in the last part of the time, and at that point there is no possibility of fulfillment due to lack of possibility, so fulfillment is not obligatory in it and the oath becomes void, just as if he had concluded it initially in this situation.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on an oath to perform an act that is impossible at the time the oath is taken, specifically an oath to drink water from an empty cup. The matn from al-Quduri states that if someone swears 'If I do not drink the water that is in this cup today, then my wife is divorced,' and the cup is empty, he does not become sinful (حنث). Similarly, if the water was present but was spilled before nightfall, he also does not become sinful. This ruling is according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that in both cases the person becomes sinful after the day passes. This disagreement applies equally when the oath is by Allah's name. The underlying principle is that, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the validity and continuation of an oath require that fulfilling it be conceptually possible (تصور البر) at the time of swearing. Abu Yusuf argues that the oath is concluded because a substitute (kafara) can still be required. The Hanafi response is that a substitute depends on the original act being conceptually possible, just as a false oath (الغموس) does not make expiation obligatory. The passage then further examines the distinction between absolute oaths (مطلق) and time-bound oaths (موقت). In an absolute oath, fulfillment is required immediately, so if the object is absent, the swearer becomes sinful at once. In a time-bound oath, fulfillment is only required at the end of the specified time; if the object ceases to exist before then, the oath becomes void because at the time of required fulfillment, the possibility no longer exists. The commentary explains this in detail with analogies and emphasizes the importance of تصور البر for an oath to be valid.

Key Terms
حنث (hanath): Breaking an oath; becoming guilty of perjury by failing to fulfill the sworn act.
تصور البر (tasawwur al-birr): The conceptual possibility of fulfilling the oath; the act sworn must be conceivable at the time of swearing.
خلف (khalaf): Substitute, replacement; used here for expiation (kafara) as a substitute for fulfilling the original oath.
اليمين الغموس (al-yamin al-ghamus): A false oath where the swearer knowingly swears to a lie about the past; such an oath does not require expiation in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, if a person swears 'If I do not drink the water in this cup today, my wife is divorced' but the cup is empty, what is the ruling?
A) The oath is valid and he becomes sinful after the day passes.
B) The oath is valid and he becomes sinful immediately.
C) The oath is not valid and he does not become sinful.
D) The oath is valid but he must pay expiation immediately.

Key Takeaway
An oath to perform an impossible act is invalid from the start according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; the condition of conceptual possibility (تصور البر) is essential for the oath's validity.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The Definition of Permission in Oaths: Knowledge vs. Consent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether permission requires knowledge or mere consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi definitions of idhn and rida and apply the correct definition to determine oath-breaking in cases of uncommunicated permission.

Core Idea
Permission (idhn) requires communication or hearing, not mere internal consent.

When a person swears not to do something except with someone's permission, does that permission need to be communicated to the swearer, or is the permission-giver's internal consent enough? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of 'idhn' and its practical consequences for oath-breaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
Consider a person who swears not to speak to Zaid except with Zaid's permission. Zaid gives permission silently without informing the swearer, who then speaks to Zaid. The question: does the swearer break the oath?
The dispute hinges on the definition of 'idhn'.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They hold that the swearer becomes a sinner (hanth) because 'idhn' implies hearing or notification; mere internal consent is insufficient.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that the swearer does not sin because 'idhn' simply means permission or allowing, like 'rida' (satisfaction), which does not require knowledge.
Abu Yusuf draws an analogy between idhn and rida.
4
Analyze the linguistic evidence
The word 'idhn' is derived from 'adhan' (announcement) or 'udhun' (ear), indicating that it inherently involves communication or hearing. In contrast, 'rida' is an act of the heart, so the analogy fails.
This derivation supports Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's position.
5
Apply to the wife's case from Nawazil
A husband swears that his wife will not go out without his permission; he gives permission in a way she cannot hear. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it is not permission; according to Abu Yusuf (and another transmission from Muhammad), it is permission. The mufta bihi is the first view.
The practical difference is whether the wife's departure breaks the oath.

اور اگر اس بات کی قسم کھائی کہ فلاں شخص مثلا زید سے گفتگو نہیں کروں گا مگر اس (زید) کی اجازت سے پھر اس شخص (زید ) نے اسے اجازت دیدی مگر اس کی اجازت کی اسے خبر معلوم نہیں ہوئی اس کے باوجود زید سے بات کر لی تو حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اذن کا لفظ اذان سے مشتق ہے جس کے معنی خبر دینے کے ہیں۔ یا اذن یعنی کان میں آواز آنے سے مشتق ہے اور باخبر ہونا یا کان میں آواز کا آنا بغیر سنے سے نہیں پایا جا سکتا ہے اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا ہے کہ اس طرح حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اذن کے معنی اطلاق کے ہیں یعنی اجازت دینا اور مباح کرنا اور یہ بات صرف اجازت دینے سے ہی پوری ہو جاتی ہے۔ جیسے رضاء صرف راضی ہونے سے پوری ہو جاتی ہے اور ہم یہ کہتے ہیں کہ رضاء تو دل کے اعمال میں سے ہے اور اذن کا یہ حال نہیں ہے۔ جیسا کہ اوپر بتلایا جا چکا ہے۔
فائدہ اور قادی صفری دقتہ میں نوازل کے حوالے سے ہے کہ ایک شخص نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ اس کی بیوی اس کی اجازت کے بغیر نہیں نکلے گی۔ پھر اس طرح سے اسے اجازت دی کہ وہ نہیں سن سکی تو امام ابوحنیفہ امام محمد حما کے قول میں یہ اذن نہیں ہے۔ لیکن ابو یوسف ومحمد رحمہم اللہ علیہا کے قول میں اذن ہے۔
Literal translation of Urdu as above.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of 'permission' (idhn) in the context of oaths. If a person swears not to speak to Zaid except with Zaid's permission, and Zaid gives permission without the swearer knowing, the swearer becomes a sinner according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad because 'idhn' is derived from 'adhan' (announcement) or from 'udhun' (ear), implying knowledge or hearing. Since the swearer did not know, the permission is ineffective. Abu Yusuf disagrees: for him, 'idhn' simply means consent, like 'rida' (satisfaction), which does not require knowledge. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that consent is a matter of the heart, while permission requires external communication. This is supported by a practical case from Nawazil: a man swears that his wife will not go out without his permission; if he gives permission in a way she cannot hear, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it is not permission, while Abu Yusuf says it is.

Key Terms
اذن (idhn): Permission that requires the permission-giver's awareness and communication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The wife may go out without sin.
B) The husband becomes a sinner if she goes out.
C) The permission is valid and the oath is not broken.
D) The oath is invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
Permission in oaths requires actual communication or hearing; mere internal consent is insufficient in the Hanafi school.

Oath Not to Speak: Speaking to a Sleeping Person
Ruling on oath-breaking when speaking to a sleeping person who can hear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath not to speak is broken by speaking to a sleeping person, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the condition of waking.

Core Idea
An oath is broken when speech reaches the hearer's ear, even if he is asleep; but later authorities require waking him.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a subtle question: if a person swears not to speak to someone, then speaks to that person while they are asleep within earshot, is the oath broken? The matn holds yes, but a narration in al-Mabsut adds a condition that the speaker must wake the sleeper. The later mashayikh prefer the stricter view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states that speaking to a sleeping person within earshot breaks the oath.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa.
2
Trace the analogy (qiyas)
The ruling is based on qiyas to a heedless person: in both cases, the sound reaches the ear, even if not understood.
The illa is wusul al-sawt ila al-sam'.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some riwayah in al-Mabsut require waking the sleeper.
This is a difference in riwayah, not between the founding imams.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The mashayikh (later authorities) adopt the stricter opinion that waking is required.
The text states 'upon it are our mashayikh' (wa 'alayhi mashayikhuna).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلَّمُ فُلَانًا فَكَلَّمَهُ وَهُوَ بِعَيْثُ يَسْمَعُ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ نَائِمٌ حَنَكَ لأَنَّهُ قَدْ كَلَّمَهُ وَوَصَلَ إِلَى سَمْعِهِ لَكِنَّهُ لَمْ يَفْهُمْ لِنَوْمِهِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا نَادَاهُ وَهُوَ بِحَيْثُ يَسْمَعُ لَكِنَّهُ لَمْ يَفْهُمْ لِتَغَافُلِهِ وَفِي بَعْضِ رِوَايَاتِ الْمَبْسُوطِ شُرِطَ ان يُوقِظَهُ وَعَلَيْهِ مَشَائِحُنَا لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ يَتَتَبَّهَ كَانَ كَمَا إِذَا نَادَاهُ مِنْ بَعِيدٍ وَهُوَ بِحَيْثُ لَا يَسْمَعُ صَوْتَهُ
He said: Whoever swears that he will not speak to so-and-so, then speaks to him while he is in a place where he can hear, except that he is asleep, he becomes oath-breaker because he did speak to him and it reached his hearing, but he did not understand due to his sleep. So it is like when he calls him in a place where he can hear, but he does not understand due to his heedlessness. And in some narrations of al-Mabsut, it is conditioned that he wakes him up, and our shuyukh adhere to this, because if he does not become alert, it is like when he calls him from afar in a place where he cannot hear his voice.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Quduri's matn with commentary from al-Hidayah. The core issue: A person swears not to speak to X, then speaks to X while X is asleep but within earshot. The ruling according to Abu Hanifa (and the matn) is that the oath is broken (hanith). The reasoning: The act of speaking occurred and the sound reached the listener's ear; the listener's failure to comprehend due to sleep is irrelevant, just as it is irrelevant if he fails to comprehend due to intentional heedlessness (taghaful). The text then notes a variant narration from al-Mabsut which requires that the speaker actually wakes the sleeper for the oath to be broken. The author's shuyukh (mashayikh) follow this stricter opinion. Their reasoning: if the sleeper does not become alert (lam yatabah), the situation is like calling someone from too far away to hear, where no oath is broken. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf in riwayah, not a difference between the founding imams.

Key Terms
حَنِثَ (hanitha): To become an oath-breaker, incurring the obligation of kaffarah
تَغَافُل (taghaful): Feigned heedlessness or intentional disregard
مَشَائِخُنَا (mashayikhuna): Our senior scholars (the Hanafi authorities of the later period)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon opinion of the mashayikh in this passage, what must the oath-taker do when speaking to a sleeping person for the oath to be broken?
A) Speak loudly enough for a wakeful person to hear
B) Wake the sleeper up before speaking
C) Ensure the sleeper responds
D) Have a witness present

Key Takeaway
An oath not to speak is broken when speech reaches the listener's ear, but the later Hanafi authorities require that the speaker wake the sleeper for the oath to be broken.

Permission and Knowledge: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether permission (idhn) requires knowledge of the oath-taker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the majority Hanafi position requires knowledge for permission to prevent oath-breaking, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Permission (idhn) requires the oath-taker's knowledge; unknown permission does not prevent hanth.

This passage deals with a common scenario in oaths: if a person swears not to do something except with permission, but then the permission is given without the oath-taker's knowledge, does the oath break? The Hanafi school has an internal difference, with the majority requiring knowledge. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
The oath: 'I will not speak to him except with his permission.' Permission is given but the oath-taker does not know. Does he break the oath?
2
State the majority position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the oath is broken (hanith). Their proof: the word 'idhn' is derived from 'adhan' (informing) or 'idhn' (ear), both requiring hearing or knowledge.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf: the oath is not broken. He interprets 'idhn' as 'itlaq' (release), which is complete by the act of giving permission, like consent (rida).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority refutes by distinguishing permission from consent: rida is an internal act of the heart, not requiring external manifestation; idhn is not like that.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text defends the majority view, indicating that the relied-upon position is that unknown permission does not prevent hanth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُهُ إِلَّا بِإِذْنِهِ فَاذِنَ لَهُ وَلَمْ يَعْلَمُ بِالْإِذْنِ حَتَّى كَلَّمَهُ حَنَثَ لِأَنَّ الْإِذْنَ مُشْتَقٌّ مِنَ الْآذَانِ الَّذِي هُوَ الْإِعْلَامُ أَوْمِنَ الْوُقُوعِ فِي الْإِذْنِ وَكُلُّ ذَالِكَ لَا يَتَحَقَّقُ إِلَّا بِالسَمَاعِ وَقَالَ ابْوَيُؤْسُفٌ لَا يَحْنِتُ لِاَنَّ الْإِذْنَ هُوَ الإطلاق وَانَّهُ يَتِمُّ بِالْإِذْنِ كَالرِّضَاءِ قُلْنَا الرِّضَاءُ مِنْ اَعْمَالِ الْقَلْبِ وَلَا كَذَالِكَ الْإِذْنُ عَلَى مَا مَرَّ
And if he swore that he will not speak to him except with his permission, then he gave him permission, but he did not know about the permission until he spoke to him, he becomes oath-breaker, because permission (idhn) is derived from adhan which is informing, or from occurrence in the ear (wuqu' fi al-idhn), and all of that is not realized except by hearing. And Abu Yusuf said: He does not become oath-breaker, because permission is release (itlaq), and it is completed by the permission itself like consent (rida). We (Hanafis) say: Consent is from actions of the heart, and permission is not like that, as has passed.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa (and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Abu Yusuf. The case: A man swears 'I will not speak to Zayd except with his permission.' Zayd gives permission, but the oath-taker does not know about it, then speaks. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that the oath is broken (hanith). Their reasoning: The word idhn (permission) is derived either from adhan (announcement/informing) or from idhn (ear), both of which require the recipient to hear or be informed. Since the oath-taker did not hear or know of the permission, the condition (permission) was not met, so the oath is broken. Abu Yusuf disagrees: He interprets idhn as itlaq (release, authorization), which is complete by the mere act of the person giving permission, regardless of the oath-taker's knowledge. He compares it to rida (consent), which is a state of the heart, not requiring external awareness. The Hanafi response (from Abu Hanifa/Muhammad) distinguishes: Rida is from the actions of the heart (a'mal al-qalb), which do not require external notification, but idhn is not like that; it is an external act that requires awareness. The lesson underscores the importance of linguistic derivation (ishtiqaq) in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission; derived from adhan (informing) or idhn (ear), thus requiring communication
اطلاق (itlaq): Release, authorization; Abu Yusuf's interpretation of idhn as a unilateral act
رضاء (rida'): Consent, satisfaction; an internal state of the heart

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that unknown permission does not prevent oath-breaking?
A) Because permission requires the oath-taker's explicit acceptance
B) Because the word 'idhn' is derived from 'adhan' or 'idhn' which require hearing or knowledge
C) Because the oath is absolute and conditions are invalid
D) Because the permission must be in writing

Key Takeaway
Permission (idhn) in Hanafi law requires the recipient's knowledge because the term is linguistically derived from informing or hearing.

Interpreting 'Month' in Oaths: Silence vs Fasting
Ruling that the month in an oath of silence begins at the time of the oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an oath of abstention and an oath of action regarding the starting point of a specified month, and apply the Hanafi rule of 'dalalat al-hal' to determine the appropriate interpretation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the start of a month in an oath depends on whether the act can be performed perpetually; silence can, fasting cannot.

This lesson draws from Imam Muhammad’s Jami’ al-Saghir. It presents a subtle but critical distinction in oath interpretation: when someone swears not to speak for a month, the month begins immediately; but when they swear to fast for a month, the month is not automatically from the oath time. The difference lies in the default status of the oath if no time is mentioned. Understanding this principle will sharpen your skills in legal inference and contextual reading of oath formulas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two oath formulas: 'I will not speak to him for a month' (oath of abstention) and 'I will fast for a month' (oath of action). Note that the rulings differ.
2
Analyse the default (without month)
For the silence oath, if 'month' were omitted, the oath would be perpetual (ta’abbud al-yamin) – he would never speak to him again. For the fasting oath, omitting 'month' does not make it perpetual because fasting cannot be continuous (forbidden on certain days).
This default perpetuity is key: it tells us why 'month' was mentioned.
3
Apply dalalat al-hal
In the silence oath, the context (dalalat al-hal) suggests the oath-taker is angry and wants an immediate limitation, so the month starts now. In the fasting oath, 'month' is merely to specify the duration, but it is ambiguous (munkar) and requires further specification.
Dalalat al-hal is a recognized Hanafi interpretative tool in oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَ إِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُهُ شَهْرًا فَهُوَ مِنْ حِيْنَ حَلْفٍ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْلَمْ يَذْكُرِ الشَّهْرَ تَتَابَّدُ الْيَمِينُ وَذِكْرُ الشَّهْرِ لِإِخْرَاجِ مَا وَرَاءَهُ فَبَقِيَ الَّذِي يَلِى يَمِيْنَهُ دَاخِلًا عَمَلًا بِدَلَالَةِ حَالِهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا قَالَ وَاللَّهِ لَا صُوْمَنَّ شَهْرًا لِأَنَّهُ لَوْلَمْ يذكر الشَّهْرَ لَا يَتَأَبَّدُ الْيَمِينُ فَكَانَ ذِكْرُهُ لِتَقْدِيرِ الصَّوْمِ بِهِ وَإِنَّهُ مُنكِرْ فَ التَّعَيُّنُ إِلَيْهِ
He said: And if he swears that he will not speak to him for a month, then it is from the time of the oath, because if he had not mentioned the month, the oath would become perpetual, and the mention of the month is to exclude what is beyond it. So what follows his oath remains included, acting upon the indication of his state. Contrary to when he says: 'By Allah, I will surely fast for a month', because if he did not mention the month, the oath would not become perpetual, so his mention is for specifying the fast with it, and it is rejected, so specification is needed.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is from Imam Muhammad's Jami' al-Saghir. It addresses a rule of interpretation for oaths involving a time period. When a person swears 'I will not speak to him for a month', the month begins from the moment of the oath. The reasoning: Without the mention of 'month', the oath would be perpetual (mu'abbad) — he would never speak to him again. Mentioning the month removes the time after the month, but the time immediately following the oath is included. This is based on the 'indication of his state' (dalalat al-hal) — the context of anger suggests immediacy. The text contrasts this with an oath to fast: 'By Allah, I will fast for a month.' Here, if 'month' were not mentioned, the oath would not be perpetual because fasting is an act of worship that cannot be continuous (due to forbidden days etc.). Therefore, mentioning 'month' is intended to specify the duration, but it is ambiguous (munkar) and requires specification (ta'ayyun) — i.e., the month is not necessarily from the time of the oath; it could be any month. This contrast highlights that the same wording can have different legal effects depending on the nature of the act and the default ruling on perpetuity.

Key Terms
تأبد اليمين (ta'abbud al-yamin): The oath becomes perpetual, lasting forever unless broken by a future act
دلالة الحال (dalalat al-hal): Indication from the context or circumstances, used to interpret the oath-taker's intent
منكر (munkar): Ambiguous or unclear; here it means the mention of 'month' in the fasting oath is not definitive regarding timing

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the month in an oath of silence begin from the oath time, but in an oath to fast, it does not necessarily?
A) Because silence is harder than fasting
B) Because without mentioning a month, the silence oath would be perpetual, while the fasting oath would not
C) Because the oath-taker is presumed angry only at the oath moment
D) Because fasting can only be done in Ramadan

Key Takeaway
When interpreting a month in an oath, the Hanafi school examines whether the act would be perpetual without the time limit; if so, the month begins immediately, otherwise it is ambiguous.

Interpreting 'Day' in Conditional Divorce Oaths
Ruling on whether 'yawm' includes night in conditional divorce oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether 'yawm' in a conditional divorce includes night, and apply the principle of 'yudayyan' to determine judicial acceptance of a claimed intent.

Core Idea
'Yawm' with a non-continuous action means absolute time, including night, unless the husband's intent is accepted in court.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced issue: when a conditional divorce oath uses the word 'yawm' (day), does the condition trigger only during daylight or also at night? The answer hinges on linguistic analysis and the concept of 'yudayyan' (being believed in court). An intra-Hanafi difference emerges between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf on whether the husband's claimed specific intent should be accepted judicially.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional phrase
Read the husband's statement: 'The day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced.' Note that the verb 'speak' is non-continuous (it does not stretch over time).
The action is instantaneous, not habitual.
2
Apply the linguistic rule
When 'yawm' is paired with a non-continuous action, it denotes absolute time (mutlaq al-waqt), which includes both day and night. Confirm with the Quranic proof: 'yawm' in Surah al-Anfal 8:16 refers to the time of battle, which spans day and night.
The rule is established in Hanafi usul: al-yawm idha qurina bi fi'lin la yamtaddu yuradu bihi mutlaq al-waqt.
3
Determine the default ruling
The default ruling: speaking at any time (day or night) triggers the divorce. The word 'yawm' encompasses both.
4
Examine the exception (yudayyan)
If the husband claims he intended only the daytime, Abu Hanifa holds he is believed in court (yudayyan) because 'yawm' is also used specifically for daytime. The court accepts his oath as to his intention.
This is based on the principle that a person knows his own intent better than others.
5
Contrast Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf disagrees: the husband should not be believed because the specific intent contradicts common custom ('urf). The court should not accept the claimed intention.
This difference produces a practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) in case of nighttime speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْقَالَ يَوْمَ أَكَلَّمُ فُلَانًا فَامْرَأَتُهُ طَالِقٌ فَهُوَ عَلَى اللَّيْلِ وَالنَّهَارِ لِاَنَّ إِسْمَ الْيَوْمِ إِذَا قَرَنَ بِفِعْلٍ لَا يَمْتَدُّ يُرَادُ بِهِ مُطْلَقُ الْوَقْتِ قَالَ اللهُ تَعَالَى وَمَنْ يُوَلِهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ دُبُرَهُ وَالْكَلَامُ لَا يَمْتَدُّ وَإِنْ عَلَى النَّهَارَ خَاصَّةً دُيِّنَ فِي الْقَضَاءِ لِأَنَّهُ مُسْتَعْمَلْ فِيهِ أَيْضًا وَعَنْ أَبِى يُوْسُفٌ أَنَّهُ لَا يَدَيَّنُ فِي الْقَضَاءِ لِأَنَّهُ خِلَافُ الْمُتَعَارِفِ وَلَوْ قَالَ لَيْلَةٌ أُكَلَّمُ فُلَانًا فَهُوَ عَلَى اللَّيْلِ خَاصَّةٌ لِأَنَّهُ حَقِيقَةٌ فِي سَوَادِ اللَّيْلِ كَالنَّهَارِ لِلْبَيَاضِ خَاصَّةً وَمَا جَاءَ اسْتِعْمَالُهُ فِي مُطْلَقِ الْوَقْتِ
If someone says, 'The day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced,' then it applies to both night and day because the word 'yawm' when associated with a non-sustaining action is intended to mean absolute time. Allah the Exalted said: 'And whoever turns his back to them on that day...' and speech is not sustaining. If he intended only the day specifically, he is to be believed in judicial proceedings (yudayyan) because it is also used in that meaning. From Abu Yusuf it is reported that he is not to be believed in judicial proceedings because it is contrary to common usage. If he said, 'On a night I speak to so-and-so,' then it applies specifically to night because it is literally used for the darkness of night, just as 'day' is specifically for brightness, and it is not used for absolute time.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage deals with the interpretation of conditional divorce oaths using temporal terms. The scenario is a man who says: 'The day I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced.' The ruling is that this applies to both day and night. The reason is that the word 'yawm' (day) when paired with an action that is not continuous (like speaking) is taken to mean absolute time (mutlaq al-waqt). The text provides a Quranic proof: 'And whoever turns his back to them on that day...' (Surah al-Anfal 8:16), where 'yawm' here means the time of battle, including day and night. Since speaking is not a sustained action, the condition is triggered whether the speaking occurs during the day or night. However, if the person specifically intended only the daytime, he is to be believed in qada (judicial proceedings) because the word can also be used specifically for the daytime. Abu Yusuf has a differing opinion: he holds that the person should not be believed in qada because this specific meaning is contrary to common custom (mutafarif). The text then contrasts the use of 'laylah' (night): if he says 'the night I speak,' it applies only to night because 'laylah' is literally the darkness, just as 'yawm' literally means brightness. It is not used for absolute time. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah, with an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Abu Yusuf diverging on the judicial treatment).

Key Terms
يَوْم (yawm): In this context, when used with a non-continuous verb, it means absolute time (including day and night).
دَيَّنَ (dayyana): To believe someone in judicial proceedings regarding their intended meaning in an oath or divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says 'Yawma ukallimu fulanan fa imra'ati taliq' and speaks at night, does divorce occur?
A) Yes, because 'yawm' includes both day and night.
B) No, because 'yawm' means only daytime.
C) Only if the husband intended night.
D) Only if the judge decides.

Key Takeaway
The word 'yawm' in a conditional divorce oath includes both day and night for non-continuous actions, and the husband may be believed in court if he claims he intended only the daytime.
