Conditional Oaths: Effect of Death of the Exception Person
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional oaths with a pending exception event

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional oath when the exception condition becomes impossible (e.g., death) and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
When a condition in an oath becomes impossible, the oath drops—because the prohibition was tied to a possible event.

In Hanafi fiqh, conditional oaths often include a limiting condition (ghayah) such as 'except that Zaid arrives' or 'until Zaid permits'. This lesson explores what happens when that condition becomes impossible—specifically when the person on whom the exception depends dies. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ on whether the oath then drops or becomes perpetual. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on conditional divorces and oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath structure
Recognize the conditional phrase: 'If I speak to so-and-so, except that Zaid arrives/permits' (or 'until Zaid arrives/permits'). This creates a limit (ghayah) on the prohibited act.
The oath is valid only before the limit is reached.
2
Determine the normal operation
If the condition occurs (Zaid arrives/permission granted), the oath ends—speaking after that does not break the oath. If he speaks before, the oath is broken.
This is straightforward and agreed upon.
3
Analyze the impossibility scenario
What if Zaid dies before the condition can occur? The condition becomes impossible to fulfill.
Death makes the event inconceivable (ghayr mutasawwar al-wujud).
4
Compare Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's positions
Abu Hanifa: The oath drops completely (sagatat al-yamin) because the prohibition was limited by a possible event; impossibility ends the oath. Abu Yusuf: The oath becomes perpetual because the limit failing does not end the oath; its conceivability is not a condition.
The text states 'khilafan li Abi Yusuf' indicating the first view is the one relied upon.
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's: the oath drops, so speaking after death does not trigger divorce.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ إِنْ كَلَّمْتُ فَلَانًا إِلَّا أَنْ يُقْدُمَ فُلَانٌ أَوْ قَالَ حَتَّى يَقْدُمَ فُلَانٌ أَوْ قَالَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَأْذَنَ فُلَانٌ أَوْ حَتَّى يَأْذَنَ فُلَانٌ فَامْرَأَتُهُ طَالِقٌ فَكَلَّمَهُ قَبْلَ الْقُدُومِ وَالْإِذْنِ جَنَتَ وَلَوْ كَلَّمَهُ بَعْدَ الْقُدُومِ وَالْإِذْنِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لِأَنَّهُ غَايَةٌ وَالْيَمِينُ بَاقِيَةٌ قَبْلَ الْغَايَهِ وَ مُنْتَهِيَةٌ بَعْدَهَا فَلَايَحْنِكَ بِالْكَلَامِ بَعْدَ انْتِهَاءِ الْيَمِينِ وَإِنْ مَاتَ فُلَانٌ سَقَطَتِ الْيَمِينُ خِلَافًا لا بِى يُوسُفَ اَنَّ الْمَمْنُوعَ عَنْهُ كَلَامٌ يَنتَهِي بِالْإِذْنِ وَالْقَدُومِ وَلَمْ يَبْقَ بَعْدَ الْمَوْتِ مُتَصَوَّرُ الْوُجُوْدِ فَسَقَطَتِ الْيَمِينُ وَعِنْدَهُ التَّصَرُّرُ لَيْسَ بِشَيْطٍ فَعِنْدَ سُقُوطِ الْغَايَةِ يَتَابَّدُ الْيَمِينُ
If someone says, 'If I speak to so-and-so, except that Zaid arrives,' or 'until Zaid arrives,' or 'except that Zaid permits,' or 'until Zaid permits,' then my wife is divorced. If he speaks before the arrival or permission, he breaks the oath. If he speaks after the arrival or permission, he does not break the oath because it is a limit (ghayah); the oath remains before the limit and ends after it. So he does not break the oath by speaking after the oath has ended. If Zaid dies, the oath drops (sagatat al-yamin) contrary to Abu Yusuf, because the prohibited thing (speech) is limited by permission or arrival, and after death it is not possible to conceive of its occurrence, so the oath drops. According to him (Abu Yusuf), its conceivability is not a condition; when the limit fails, the oath becomes perpetual.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage discusses conditional divorce oaths that include a limiting condition (ghayah). The examples: 'If I speak to so-and-so, except that Zaid arrives' or 'until Zaid arrives' etc. The first ruling: if the person speaks before the condition occurs (Zaid's arrival or permission), he breaks the oath and divorce occurs. If he speaks after Zaid arrives or gives permission, he does not break the oath because the oath ended with the arrival/permission. The key issue is: what if Zaid dies before the condition can occur? According to Abu Hanifa, the oath drops completely (sagatat al-yamin). The reason is that the prohibited act (speaking to so-and-so) was only forbidden until the condition (Zaid's arrival/permission). Since Zaid's death makes the condition impossible to fulfill, the prohibition no longer applies, and the oath is void. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds that the oath becomes perpetual. According to him, the fact that Zaid died does not matter; the absence of the condition means the prohibition remains forever, so if the person ever speaks to so-and-so, he breaks the oath. The text clarifies that the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is that the oath drops, and the authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah. The response to Abu Yusuf is that the limiting condition was meant to end the prohibition; death achieves the same effect. Abu Yusuf's view is considered weak and not relied upon.

Key Terms
غَايَة (ghayah): A limit or end point for a conditional statement; the oath is valid only until that limit.
سَقَطَتِ الْيَمِين (sagatat al-yamin): The oath becomes void and no longer has any effect; the person is free from its obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says: 'If I speak to Zaid except that Amr arrives, my wife is divorced.' Amr dies before arriving. According to Abu Hanifa, what happens if he then speaks to Zaid?
A) Divorce occurs because the oath is perpetual.
B) Divorce does not occur because the oath dropped.
C) The oath requires expiation.
D) The divorce is suspended until the Day of Judgment.

Key Takeaway
When a condition in an oath becomes impossible (e.g., death of the person on whom the exception depends), the oath drops entirely according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Oath with a Condition and Death of the Person
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of a third party's death on a conditional oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an oath conditioned on a future event becomes impossible due to the death of the third party, and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's positions.

Core Idea
When the condition of an oath becomes impossible, the oath drops; it does not become perpetual.

In Hanafi law, oaths are often tied to specific conditions that mark their duration. But what happens when that condition becomes impossible — such as when the person whose arrival or permission was the limit dies? This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute between the school's founders, Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi jurists evaluate the interaction between conditional intent and practical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath structure
The oath is conditional: speaking to a person is prohibited until a specified event occurs (arrival or permission of a third party). The condition is formulated as 'unless' or 'until'.
The passage uses phrases like إِلَّا أَنْ يُقْدُمَ or حَتَّى يَقْدُمَ to set a limit.
2
Determine the effect when the condition is met
If the condition (arrival/permission) occurs before any speech, the oath ends and speaking after that does not break the oath.
The text says: وَلَوْ كَلَّمَهُ بَعْدَ الْقُدُومِ وَالْإِذْنِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ
3
Analyze the death scenario
Now the third party dies before the condition can be fulfilled. The condition becomes impossible (لم يبق متصور الوجود). Ask: does the oath continue or end?
The key question is whether the oath remains binding after the condition becomes impossible.
4
Compare the two Hanafi positions
Imam Abu Hanifa says the oath drops because it was dependent on that limit; Imam Abu Yusuf says it becomes perpetual because the limit is not a condition for the oath's existence.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the prohibition was only until the limit, and with no limit possible, the prohibition ends. Abu Yusuf's reasoning: the oath was validly made and the condition's impossibility does not void it.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and its reasoning
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's. The oath drops, and the swearer may speak without penalty.
This is the view in Zahir al-Riwayah and preferred by Al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ إِنْ كَلَّمْتُ فَلَانًا إِلَّا أَنْ يُقْدُمَ فُلَانٌ أَوْ قَالَ حَتَّى يَقْدُمَ فُلَانٌ أَوْ قَالَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَأْذَنَ فُلَانٌ أَوْ حَتَّى يَأْذَنَ فُلَانٌ فَامْرَأَتُهُ طَالِقٌ فَكَلَّمَهُ قَبْلَ الْقُدُومِ وَالْإِذْنِ جَنَتَ وَلَوْ كَلَّمَهُ بَعْدَ الْقُدُومِ وَالْإِذْنِ لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لِأَنَّهُ غَايَةٌ وَالْيَمِينُ بَاقِيَةٌ قَبْلَ الْغَايَهِ وَ مُنْتهِيَةٌ بَعْدَهَا فَلَايَحْنِكَ بِالْكَلَامِ بَعْدَ انْتِهَاءِ الْيَمِينِ وَإِنْ مَاتَ فُلَانٌ سَقَطَتِ الْيَمِينُ خِلَافًا لا بِى يُوسُفَ اَنَّ الْمَمْنُوعَ عَنْهُ كَلَامٌ يَنتَهِي بِالْإِذْنِ وَالْقَدُ وَم وَلَمْ يَبْقَ بَعْدَ الْمَوْتِ مُتَصَوَّرُ الْوُجُوْدِ فَسَقَطَتِ الْيَمِينُ وَعِنْدَهُ التَّصَرُّرُ لَيْسَ بِشَيْطٍ فَعِنْدَ سُقُوطِ الْغَايَةِ يَتَابَّدُ الْيَمِينُ
And if he said: 'If I speak to so-and-so, unless so-and-so arrives' or he said 'until so-and-so arrives' or he said 'unless so-and-so permits' or 'until so-and-so permits' and his wife is divorced, then he spoke before arrival and permission, he breaks his oath. And if he spoke after arrival and permission, he does not break his oath, because it is a limit, and the oath remains before the limit and ends after it, so he does not break by speech after the oath ends. And if so-and-so dies, the oath drops, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Because the prohibited speech is that which ends by permission and arrival, and after death its occurrence is no longer conceivable, so the oath drops. And according to him, the conceivability is not a condition, so upon the dropping of the limit, the oath becomes perpetual.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The author states the ruling: if a person makes an oath conditional upon the arrival or permission of a third party, speaking after that condition is met does not break the oath because the oath has ended. However, if the third party dies, there is a disagreement. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the oath drops because the condition can never be fulfilled. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the oath remains perpetual because the condition is not a prerequisite for the oath to be binding; the oath becomes a general prohibition forever. The author presents Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position, citing the reasoning that the oath was only meant to prevent speech until that limit, and with death the limit is impossible, so the oath becomes void. This is a logical argument (dalil aqli) based on the nature of conditional oaths.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāyah): A limit or endpoint that defines the duration of an oath.
حانث (ḥānith): One who breaks an oath, incurring the penalty (kaffarah or, here, talaq).
يمين (yamīn): An oath; in this context, a conditional divorce oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears 'If I speak to Zaid unless Amr arrives' and then Amr dies, what happens to the oath according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The oath drops and he may speak without penalty.
B) The oath becomes perpetual and he must never speak to Zaid.
C) The oath is suspended until the Day of Judgment.
D) He must seek a fatwa from the qadi.

Key Takeaway
When the condition of a conditional oath becomes impossible due to death of the third party, the oath drops (saqatat) according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Oath on an Unspecified Slave, Wife, or Friend: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on oaths concerning persons identified by their relation to a third party, when that relation ends

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether an oath on a person identified by affiliation breaks after the affiliation ends, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
An oath tied to an affiliation is lifted when the affiliation ends — unless the affiliation was merely for identification.

When a person swears not to speak to 'Zaid's wife' or 'Zaid's friend', the legal effect after a divorce or falling out depends on whether the oath was attached to the relationship or to the person themselves. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this subtle point, rooted in the purpose of the affiliation (iḍāfah). In this lesson, we will examine the reasoning of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and the dissenting view of Imam Muhammad, and see how this affects the validity of the oath.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish types of affiliation
Recognize that the passage divides affiliations into iḍāfat al-milk (ownership, e.g., 'slave of Fulan') and iḍāfat al-nisbah (relationship, e.g., 'wife of Fulan', 'friend of Fulan'). The ruling for the first is by agreement; the dispute is about the second.
2
State the agreed ruling for ownership
All three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that if the slave is sold, the oath does not break because the prohibition was tied to the ownership affiliation which no longer exists.
3
Present the dispute for relationship affiliation
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the oath also does not break for wife or friend after divorce/enmity. According to Muhammad, it does break because the affiliation is only for identification (taʿrīf), not the basis of the prohibition.
4
Analyze the reasoning of each side
The majority view (from al-Jami' al-Saghir) holds that the swearer likely intended to avoid the person due to their tie to Fulan, so once the tie is gone, the oath is lifted due to doubt. Muhammad (from al-Ziyadat) argues that the oath is on the person themselves, and the affiliation merely identifies them, so the prohibition remains.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: If a man swore not to speak to 'Zaid's wife' and she is divorced, the majority allows him to speak to her without breaking the oath; Muhammad says he must not speak or he breaks the oath.
This affects whether kaffarah is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلَّمُ عَبْدَفُلَانِ وَلَمْ يَنْوِ عَبْدًا بِعَيْنِهِ أَوِ امْرَأَةَ فُلَانِ اَوْ صَدِيقَ فُلَانِ فَبَاعَ فُلَانٌ عَبْدَهُ أَوْبَانَتْ مِنْهُ امْرَأَتُهُ أَوْ عَادَى صَدِيقَهُ فَكَلَّمَهُمْ لَمْ يَحْنِتُ لأَنَّهُ عَقَدَيَمِيْتُهُ عَلَى فِعْلٍ وَاقِعٍ فِي مَحَلَّ مُضَافٍ إِلَى فَلَانٍ أَمَّا إِضَافَةُ مِلْكِ أَوْإِضَافَهُ نِسْبَةٍ وَلَمْ يُوجَدْ فَلا يَحْنِتُ قَال هَذَا فِي إِضَافَةِ الْمِلْكِ بِالْاتِفَاقِ وَفِي إِضَافَةِ النَّسْبَةِ عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٌ يَحْدِثُ كَالْمَرْأَةِ وَالصَّدِيقِ قَالَ فِى الرِّيَادَاتِ لِاَنَّ هَذِهِ الْإِضَافَةَ لِلتَّعْرِيفِ لَانَّ الْمَرْأَةَ. وَالصَّدِيقَ مَقْصُودَ ان بِالهِجْرَانِ فَلَا يُشْتَرَطُ دَوَامُهَا فَيَتَعَلَّقُ الْحُكْمُ بِعَيْنِهِ كَمَا فِي الْإِشَارَةِ وَوَجْهُ مَا ذُكِرَهُهُنَا وَهُوَرِوَايَةُ الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ انَّهُ يَحْتَمِلُ اَنْ يَكُونَ غَرْضُهُ هِجْرَانَهُ لَاجَلِ الْمُصَافِ إِلَيْهِ وَلِهَذَا لَمْ يُعَيّنهُ فَلَا يَحْنِتُ بَعْدَ زَوَالِ الْإِضَافَةِ بِالشَّكِ
And if someone swears that he will not speak to Fulan's slave, and he did not intend a specific slave, or Fulan's wife, or Fulan's friend, then Fulan sold his slave, or his wife became separated by bain divorce, or he became hostile to his friend, and then he spoke to them, he does not break his oath because he placed his oath on an action occurring in a locus that is affiliated to Fulan—either by ownership affiliation or relationship affiliation—and it no longer exists, so he does not break. He said: this is in the ownership affiliation by agreement, and in relationship affiliation according to Muhammad he breaks, as with the wife and friend. He said in al-Ziyadat: because this affiliation is for identification, since the wife and friend are intended by the abandonment, so their continuity is not required, thus the ruling attaches to their specific selves as in the case of pointing. And the explanation of what is mentioned here, and it is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, is that it is possible his purpose is to abandon them on account of the affiliated person, and therefore he did not specify them, so he does not break after the removal of the affiliation due to doubt.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage present a ruling and a intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths involving persons described by their relation to a third party (e.g., 'Fulan's slave', 'Fulan's wife', 'Fulan's friend'). The core rule: if the relation (ownership or relationship) ceases, according to the majority view, the oath does not break because the prohibition was linked to that relation. For ownership (slave), all three scholars (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that breaking the oath only occurs if the relation existed at the time of speaking. For relationship (wife, friend), Imam Muhammad holds that the oath continues even after divorce or enmity because the affiliation is merely for identification; the real target is the person themselves. The author notes that the view presented here (that the oath does not break in all cases of affiliation) is from al-Jami' al-Saghir, and another view from al-Ziyadat (attributed to Muhammad) says the oath breaks for wife and friend. The reasoning behind the Jami' al-Saghir view is that the swearer's intention was likely to avoid the person due to their tie to Fulan, not due to their own person, so when the tie ends, the oath ends due to doubt.

Key Terms
إضافة (iḍāfah): The grammatical annexation of a person to another, indicating ownership or relationship.
تعريف (taʿrīf): Identification; in this context, the purpose of the affiliation is to identify the person, not to base the prohibition on the relation.
هجران (hijrān): Abandonment or avoidance; the object of the oath is to avoid certain persons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if a man swears 'I will not speak to Zaid's friend' and later Zaid and his friend become enemies, what happens if the swearer speaks to the friend?
A) He breaks his oath because the friend is still the same person.
B) He does not break his oath because the affiliation (friendship) no longer exists.
C) He breaks his oath only if he intended the specific friend by name.
D) He must offer kaffarah because of doubt.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath on a person identified by affiliation is generally tied to that affiliation, so if the affiliation ends, the oath does not break unless the affiliation was merely for identification.

Oath on Specific Designated Person with Indication
Ruling on oaths where a specific slave, wife, or friend is designated by pointing, and the effect of change in ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths by indication and apply it to slave vs. wife/friend, distinguishing the effective cause ('illa).

Core Idea
An oath by indication on a slave is tied to ownership; on a wife/friend it is tied to their person.

This lesson explores an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths made on specific individuals designated by pointing (ishārah). The key question: if the underlying relationship changes (e.g., a slave is sold, a wife is divorced), does the oath continue to bind? The answer depends on the effective cause (maʿnā) of the oath—whether it is attached to the person themselves or to the affiliation. We will examine two positions: that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and that of Muhammad and Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage discusses oaths on: (1) a specific slave of someone, (2) a specific wife of someone, and (3) a specific friend of someone. Note that each is designated by indication (pointing).
The indication ('this') makes the identification independent of the name.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the oath does not break for the slave after a change in ownership, but does break for the wife and friend. Muhammad and Zufar hold that the oath breaks for all three.
The dispute is about the underlying motive (dāʿī) behind the oath.
3
Understand the reasoning of Muhammad and Zufar
They argue that the indication (ishārah) is stronger than simple affiliation (iḍāfah). Pointing cuts off all partnership, making the identification absolute. Therefore, the oath attaches to the person themselves, regardless of later changes in relationship.
They compare it to the case of a wife or friend where the oath attaches to the person.
4
Understand the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They argue that the motive for the oath lies in the affiliated person (muṣāf ilayh)—the owner, not the slave. People do not abandon or hate slaves for their own sake, but because of their master. Thus, the oath is tied to the existence of ownership. Once ownership ends, the oath ends. For a wife or friend, the enmity is directed at their own persons, so the oath continues after divorce or enmity.
This is the mufta bihi position.
5
Apply the same reasoning to a house
The passage extends the same dispute to a house: if someone swears not to enter 'this house of Zaid' and Zaid sells it, the same two positions apply. The effective cause is whether the avoidance is due to the owner or the object itself.
Houses, like slaves, are not avoided for their own sake.

وَإِنْ كَانَتْ يَمِينُهُ عَلَى عَبْدٍ بِعَيْنِهِ بأن قَالَ عَبْدَ فُلان هذا أوامْرَأَةَ فَلان بعَيْنِهَا أَوْصَدِيقَ فَلَانَ بِعَيْنِهِ لَمْ يَحْنِتُ فِي الْعَبْدِ وَحَنَتْ فِي الْمَرْأَةِ وَالصَّدِيقِ وَهَذَا قَوْلُ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَابِى يُوْسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ ۚ يَحْنِثُ فِي الْعَبْدِ أَيْضًا وَ هُوَ قَوْلُ زُفَرِّ وَ إِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَدْخُلُ دَارَفُلان هذِهِ فَبَاعَهَا ثُمَّ دَخَلَهَا فَهُوَ عَلى هَذَا الْاِخْتِلَافِ وَجْهُ قَوْلِ مُحَمَّدٍ وَزُفَرَ اَنَّ الْإِضَافَةَ لِلتَّعْرِيفِ وَالْإِشَارَةُ أَبْلَغَ مِنْهَا لِكَوْنِهَا قَاطِعَةٌ لِلشَّرُكَةِ بِخِلَافِ الْإِضَافَةِ فَاعْتُبِرَتِ الْإِشَارَةُ وَلَغَتِ الْإِضَافَةُ وَصَارَ كَالصَّدِيقِ وَالْمَرْأَةِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّ الدَّاعِى إِلَى الْيَمِينِ مَعْنِّى فِي الْمُصَافِ إِلَيْهِ لِأَنَّ هَذَا الْأَعْيَانَ لَاتُهُجَرُ وَلَا تُعَادَى لِذَوَاتِهَا وَكَذَا الْعَبْدُ لِسُقُوطِ مَنْزِلَتِهِ بَلْ لِمَعْنِّي فِي مَلَّا كِهَا فَتَقَيَّدَ الْيَمِينُ بِحَالِ قِيَامِ الْمِلْكِ بِخِلَافِ مَاذَا كَانَتِ الْإِضَافَةُ نِسْبَةٍ كَالصَّدِيقِ وَ الْمَرْأَةِ لِأَنَّهُ يُعَادَى لِذَاتِهِ فَكَانَتِ الْإِضَافَةُ لِلتَّعْرِيفِ وَالدَّاعِى لِمَعْنَى فِى الْمُصَافِ إِلَيْهِ غَيْرُ ظَاهِرٍ لِعَدْمِ التَّعْيِينِ بِخِلَافِ مَا تَقَدَّمَ
And if his oath is upon a specific slave, such that he said 'this slave of Fulan' or a specific wife of Fulan or a specific friend of Fulan, he does not break in the slave, and he breaks in the wife and friend. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: he breaks in the slave as well, and this is the saying of Zufar. And if he swore 'I will not enter this house of Fulan', then he sold it, then he entered it, it is upon this difference. The face of Muhammad and Zufar's saying is that the affiliation is for identification, and the indication is more emphatic than it because it cuts off partnership, unlike simple affiliation, so the indication is considered and the affiliation is ignored, and it becomes like the friend and wife. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): the motivator for the oath is a meaning in the one to whom the affiliation refers, because these specific individuals are not abandoned or hated for their own selves, and similarly the slave due to his low status, but rather for a meaning in their owner, so the oath is restricted to the state of ownership. Contrary to when the affiliation is of relationship, like friend and wife, because he hates them for their own selves, so the affiliation is for identification, and the motivator for a meaning in the affiliated person is not apparent due to the lack of specification, unlike what preceded.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage deals with oaths on specific individuals designated by indication (pointing). There are three Imāms mentioned: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (with Zufar agreeing with Muhammad). The ruling: if the oath is on a specific slave (e.g., 'this slave of Zaid'), and the slave is later sold, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the oath does not break if he speaks to him after the sale, because the true motivation is the ownership relation—people do not abandon slaves for their own sake but rather because of their master. However, for a specific wife or friend designated by indication, the oath does break even after divorce or enmity, because the hatred is directed at the person themselves, not the relation. Muhammad and Zufar argue that the indication is stronger than simple affiliation; it identifies the person with specificity, so the affiliation (e.g., 'of Zaid') is irrelevant. Therefore, they hold that the oath breaks in all three cases (slave, wife, friend) even after the relation changes, because the person themselves remains. The author then provides an analogy: if someone swears not to enter 'this house of Zaid' and Zaid sells it, the same dispute applies. The reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is that people do not avoid houses for their own sake but because of the owner, so the oath is tied to ownership. This is an example of qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because the effective cause ('illa) is the motivational factor behind the oath.

Key Terms
إشارة (ishārah): Pointing or indication that identifies a specific individual or object.
معنى (maʿnā): The underlying motivation or effective cause for the oath.
مُصَاف (muṣāf): The person to whom the affiliation refers (e.g., the owner, the husband).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a man swears 'I will not speak to this slave of Zaid' (pointing to a specific slave) and later Zaid sells the slave, what happens if he then speaks to the slave?
A) He breaks his oath because the slave is still the same person.
B) He does not break his oath because the oath was tied to Zaid's ownership, which has ended.
C) He breaks his oath because the indication makes the oath absolute.
D) He must perform kaffarah for the oath.

Key Takeaway
An oath by indication on a slave or house is tied to its owner; for a wife or friend, it is tied to their person—thus the ruling changes based on the effective cause.

Stillbirth Fulfills Conditional Oaths
Ruling that a stillborn child fulfills the condition in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a stillborn child fulfills a conditional oath of divorce or manumission and explain the legal reasoning based on reality, custom, and shari'ah effects.

Core Idea
A stillborn child fulfills the condition 'if you bear a child' because it is a mawlūd, recognized legally for iddah, nifas, and umm walad.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling: a husband who says 'if you bear a child, you are divorced' finds his wife divorced even if she delivers a stillborn. The author of al-Hidayah provides a multi-layered proof that a stillborn is a walad in reality, custom, and Islamic law. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi fiqh defines 'child' for legal purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional oath
Understand the scenario: a man makes a conditional statement to his wife or slave woman, linking divorce or manumission to the birth of a child.
The condition uses the word 'walad' (child).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling is that the condition is fulfilled by a stillborn, so divorce or manumission takes effect.
This is the agreed position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The text provides four reasons: the stillborn is a mawlūd (born) in reality; it is called a child in custom ('urf); shari'ah treats it as a child for iddah, nifas, and umm walad.
The proof integrates linguistic, customary, and legal considerations.
4
Consider cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools may require a live birth, but the Hanafi view is based on the broader definition of walad.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in non-Hanafi jurisdictions, a stillbirth might not trigger conditional oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في العتق والطلاق باب ، آزادی اور طلاق کے بارے میں قسم کھانے کا بیان بیوی سے کہا جب تو بچہ جنے تجھے طلاق اس نے مردار بچہ جنا طلاق ہو جائے گی وَمَنْ قَالَ لِامْرَأَتِهِ إِذَا وَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا فَانْتِ طَالِقٌ فَوَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا مَيْتًا طُلِقَتْ وَكَذَالِكَ إِذَا قَالَ لِأَمَتِهِ إِذَا وَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا فَأَنْتِ حُرَّةٌ لِأَنَّ الْمَوْجُودَ مَوْلُودٌ فَيَكُونَ وَلَدَا حَقِيقَةٌ وَيُسَمَّى بِهِ فِي الْعُرْفِ وَيُعْتَبَرُ وَلَدًا فِي الشَّرْعِ حَتَّى تَنْقَضِيَ بِهِ الْعِدَّةُ وَالدَّمُ بَعْدَهُ نِفَاسٌ وَأُمُّهُ أُمُّ وَلَدِلَهُ فَيَتَحَقَّقُ الشَّرْطُ وَهُوَ وِلَادَةُ الْوَلَدِ ترجمہ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے اس طرح کہا کہ جب تم کو بچہ پیدا ہو تو تم کو طلاق ہے۔ اس کے بعد اس کو ایک مرا ہوا بچہ پیدا ہوا تو اسے طلاق ہو جائے گی۔ اسی طرح اگر موٹی نے اپنی باندی سے کہا اگر تو نے لڑ کا جنا تو تو آزاد ہے اسلئے کہ مولود حقیقت میں پیدائشی بچہ ہے عرف میں بھی اسے بچہ کہا جاتا ہے اور شریعت میں بھی حتی کہ اس بچے سے عدت گذر جاتی ہے اور اس کی پیدائش کے بعد نفاس شمار کیا جاتا ہے اور ان کی ماں ام ولد بن جاتی ہے پس شروط پائی گئی جو کہ بچہ کی پیدائش ہے۔
Chapter of oath concerning manumission and divorce. Expression: When a wife bears a child, she is divorced. If she gives birth to a dead child, she is divorced. Similarly, if a man said to his slave woman 'if you bear a child, you are free', because the born entity is a mawlūd, so it is a walad in reality, called by that name in custom, and considered a walad in shari'ah, such that the iddah is completed with it, the blood after it is nifas, and the mother becomes umm walad. So the condition is fulfilled, which is the birth of a child. Translation: If a man said to his wife, when the child is born, you are divorced, and then she gives birth to a dead child, she is divorced. Similarly for a slave woman. Because the born entity is actually an offspring; in custom it is called a child, and in shari'ah it is considered a child even for iddah, nifas, and umm walad, so the condition is realized.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage establishes that a stillborn child fulfills the condition in oaths of divorce or manumission. The author provides four proofs: (1) In reality, the dead child is a mawlūd (offspring). (2) In custom ('urf), it is called a child. (3) Shari'ah recognizes it as a child for three purposes: iddah (waiting period) ends with its birth; maternal bleeding is nifas; the mother becomes umm walad. (4) Therefore, the condition 'if you bear a child' is satisfied. The ruling applies equally to wives and slave women. The reasoning integrates reality, custom, and legal effects, showing the Hanafi method of combining linguistic, 'urf, and shari'i considerations.

Key Terms
ولد (walad): Child; includes live and stillborn, as it is a born entity.
عدة (iddah): Waiting period; ends with delivery even if stillborn.
نفاس (nifās): Postpartum bleeding; applies after stillbirth as well.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child to her master; she gains freedom upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a person says to his wife 'If you give birth to a child, you are divorced,' and she gives birth to a stillborn, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The wife is not divorced because the child was dead.
B) The wife is divorced because a stillborn is legally a child.
C) The divorce depends on whether the child was alive.
D) The oath is invalid due to uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a stillborn child is legally a 'walad' for the purpose of fulfilling conditional oaths of divorce and manumission.

Minimum Days in an Oath of Service: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on minimum days in an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi interpretation of 'ayyaman kathirah' in oaths and distinguish between the views of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, including the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'many days' in an oath is fixed: ten days according to Abu Hanifa, seven according to the Sahibayn, with language determining which view applies.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the minimum number of days intended by the phrase 'ayyaman kathirah' (many days) in an oath of service. The difference hinges on linguistic interpretation and customary practice. Understanding this dispute illuminates how the Hanafi school applies usul principles to derive specific rulings from ambiguous expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath context
The scenario: a master says to his slave, 'If you serve me many days, you are free.' The ambiguity lies in 'many days'.
The oath triggers a conditional manumission; fulfillment requires serving a specific number of days.
2
Recognize the disputed term
The key term is 'ayyaman kathirah' (many days). The Hanafi school must fix a numerical minimum to determine when the slave becomes free.
The term 'ayyam' (days) is plural but indeterminate; 'kathirah' suggests quantity.
3
State Imam Abu Hanifa's view
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that ten days are intended because in Arabic, the word 'ayyam' most commonly encompasses up to ten days (i.e., the maximum for the plural of 'day').
Dalil: 'because it is the maximum that the name 'days' encompasses'.
4
State the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that seven days are intended because anything beyond seven is a repetition (takrar) of the weekly cycle.
They argue the basic unit of days is the week; 'many' beyond one cycle is repetition.
5
Note the language-based exception
If the oath is in Persian or Urdu, all Hanafi authorities agree on seven days because in those languages the singular 'roz' (day) is used instead of a plural form, limiting the scope to one week.
This shows the role of 'urf (custom) and linguistic usage in determining the meaning.
6
Determine the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: If the slave serves exactly 8 or 9 days, according to Abu Hanifa he is not freed (since less than 10), but according to the Sahibayn he is freed (since more than 7).
For Persian/Urdu oaths, 7 days is the agreed-upon minimum, so 8 days suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غلام کو کہا ان خدمتنى اياما كثيرة فانت حر وَمَنْ قَالَ لِعَبْدِهِ إِنْ حَدَمْتَنِي أَيَّامًا كَثِيرَةً فَأَنْتَ حُرِّ فَالْأَيَّامُ الْكَثِيرَةُ عِندَابِي حَنِيفَةٌ عَشَرَةُ أَيَّامٍ لِأَنَّهُ أَكْثَرُ مَا يَتَنَاوَلُهُ اِسْمُ الْأَيَّامُ وَقَالَا سَبْعَةُ أَيَّامٍ لِاَنَّ مَازَادَ عَلَيْهَا تَكْرَارٌ وَقَلِيْل لَوْ كَانَ الْيَمِينُ بِالْفَارِسِيَّةِ يَنْصَرِفُ إِلَى سَبْعَةِ أَيَّامٍ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَذْكُرُفِيهَا بِلَفْظِ الْمُفْرَدِ دُونَ الْجَمْعِ ترجمہ اگر کسی نے اپنے غلام سے کہا ( عربی میں) ان خدمتی اياما كثيرة یعنی اگر تم نے میری خدمت بہت ایام کی تو تم آزاد ہو۔ تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک دس دن مراد ہوں گے کیونکہ ایام میں زیادہ دس ہی دن ہوتے ہیں اور صاحبین نے کہا ہے کہ سات دنوں سے جو زیادہ ہوں گے وہ مکر رہو جائیں گے۔ اسلئے سات ہی دن برابر ہوں گے اور بعض مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ اگر فارسی زبان میں قسم کھائی اور اس طرح کہا اگر مرا روز بسیار خدمت کر دی آزادی شدی تو یہ قسم سات ہی دنوں تک کے لئے ہوگی۔ کیونکہ فارسی اور اردو میں روز مفرد ہی مستعمل ہوا کرتا ہے جمع کے لفظ سے ذکر نہیں کیا جاتا ہے۔ تشریح مذکورہ صورت میں ایام کثیرہ سے مراد امام صاحب کے ہاں دس دن ہیں دلیل یہ ہے کہ اسم عدد کی یہ انتہاء ہے اور صاحبین کے ہاں سات دن مراد ہوں گے اس لئے کہ ان کے ہاں اصل سات دن ہیں پھر یہی ایام مکر ر ہوتے ہیں اسلئے ساتھ دن مراد لیئے جائیں گے۔
If someone says to his slave (in Arabic): 'If you serve me many days, then you are free.' So according to Imam Abu Hanifa, ten days are intended, because the maximum that the word 'days' encompasses is ten. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: seven days, because what exceeds that is repetition. And some elders said: if the oath is in Persian, and he said such, then the oath is limited to seven days, because in Persian and Urdu, the singular 'day' is used, not the plural.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interpretation of 'ayyaman kathirah' (many days) in an oath. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that ten days are intended because in Arabic usage, 'ayyam' (days) most commonly refers to ten days. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) argue that seven days are intended, as anything beyond that is considered a repetition of the week. Some later scholars note that if the oath is in Persian (or Urdu), the reference is to seven days because in those languages, the plural form is not used; instead, the singular 'roz' (day) is used, and thus the scope is limited to a week. The commentary explains that the basis of Imam Abu Hanifa's view is that ten is the maximum number for the term 'ayyam', while the Sahibayn consider seven as the basic unit.

Key Terms
أَيَّامًا كَثِيرَةً (ayyaman kathirah): In this passage, it means 'many days' in an oath; the number is disputed between ten and seven.
تَكْرَارٌ (takrar): Repetition; the Sahibayn consider days beyond seven as repetitive cycles of the week.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view for an Arabic oath in the Hanafi school, how many days are intended by 'ayyaman kathirah'?
A) Seven days
B) Ten days
C) Thirty days
D) It depends on the master's intention

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ambiguous phrase 'ayyaman kathirah' in an oath is interpreted as ten days for Arabic oaths and seven days for Persian/Urdu oaths.

Divorce Upon Birth of a Dead Child
Ruling with proof: stillborn triggers conditional divorce/manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce when the child is stillborn and explain the reasoning that a stillborn qualifies as a 'walad'.

Core Idea
A stillborn child fulfills the condition 'if you give birth to a child' because it is a child in reality, custom, and law.

In this lesson, we examine a specific conditional divorce scenario: a husband says 'if you give birth to a child, you are divorced,' and the wife delivers a stillborn. Does the divorce occur? The Hanafi school answers yes, and we will explore the multilayered proof that establishes a stillborn as a legal 'child'. This ruling also extends to manumission of a slave-girl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and event
Recognize the husband's conditional statement: 'If you give birth to a child, you are divorced.' The event is the birth of a stillborn child.
The condition is the birth of any child, not specifically a live child.
2
Note the ruling
The wife is divorced immediately upon delivery of the stillborn. The same applies to a slave-girl who is promised freedom.
Divorce takes effect immediately, not delayed.
3
Examine the reasoning (talil)
The author provides three levels of proof: (1) Reality: anything born is a 'mawlud' and thus a child. (2) Custom: people commonly refer to a stillborn as a child. (3) Law: a stillborn completes the 'iddah, its blood is nifas, and its mother becomes an umm walad.
These proofs together confirm that a stillborn is legally a 'walad'.
4
Confirm analogous case
The same reasoning applies to a slave-girl: if the master says 'if you give birth, you are free' and she delivers a stillborn, she becomes free.
The principle is consistent across conditional statements.

Source Text

وَمَنْ قَالَ لِامْرَأَتِهِ إِذَا وَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا فَانْتِ طَالِقٌ فَوَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا مَيْتًا طُلِقَتْ وَكَذَالِكَ إِذَا قَالَ لِأَمَتِهِ إِذَا وَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا فَأَنْتِ حُرَّةٌ لِأَنَّ الْمَوْجُودَ مَوْلُودٌ فَيَكُونَ وَلَدَا حَقِيقَةٌ وَيُسَمَّى بِهِ فِي الْعُرْفِ وَيُعْتَبَرُ وَلَدًا فِي الشَّرْعِ حَتَّى تَنْقَضِيَ بِهِ الْعِدَّةُ وَالدَّمُ بَعْدَهُ نِفَاسٌ وَأُمُّهُ أُمُّ وَلَدِلَهُ فَيَتَحَقَّقُ الشَّرْطُ وَهُوَ وِلَادَةُ الْوَلَدِ ترجمہ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے اس طرح کہا کہ جب تم کو بچہ پیدا ہو تو تم کو طلاق ہے۔ اس کے بعد اس کو ایک مرا ہوا بچہ پیدا ہوا تو اسے طلاق ہو جائے گی۔ اسی طرح اگر موٹی نے اپنی باندی سے کہا اگر تو نے لڑ کا جنا تو تو آزاد ہے اسلئے کہ مولود حقیقت میں پیدائشی بچہ ہے عرف میں بھی اسے بچہ کہا جاتا ہے اور شریعت میں بھی حتی کہ اس بچے سے عدت گذر جاتی ہے اور اس کی پیدائش کے بعد نفاس شمار کیا جاتا ہے اور ان کی ماں ام ولد بن جاتی ہے پس شروط پائی گئی جو کہ بچہ کی پیدائش ہے۔
And if someone says to his wife: 'When you give birth to a child, you are divorced,' and she gives birth to a dead child, she is divorced. Similarly, if he says to his slave-girl: 'When you give birth to a child, you are free,' (the same applies). Because what is born is a 'mawlud' (born one), so it is a child in reality, and it is called a child in custom, and it is considered a child in law, such that the 'iddah is completed by it, and the blood after it is nifas, and its mother becomes an umm walad. Thus the condition - which is the birth of a child - is fulfilled.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with supporting reasoning (talil). It establishes that a stillborn child is legally a 'child' (walad) for the purpose of conditional divorce and manumission. The author gives several proofs: (1) Linguistically, anything born is a 'mawlud' and thus a 'walad' in reality. (2) Customarily, people refer to a stillborn as a child. (3) Legally, a stillborn counts for completing the 'iddah (waiting period) and the postpartum bleeding is considered nifas (lochia). Also, the mother becomes an 'umm walad' (mother of a child) for the purpose of manumission upon the owner's death. Therefore, the condition of 'birth of a child' is fulfilled by a stillborn, triggering the divorce or freedom.

Key Terms
وَلَدٌ مَيْتٌ (waladun mayyitun): A stillborn child, which is considered a 'child' for legal rulings such as conditional divorce, 'iddah, and nifas.
أُمُّ وَلَدٍ (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child to her master, gaining freedom upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The divorce is suspended until a live child is born

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a stillborn child is legally considered a 'walad' for purposes of conditional divorce, manumission, 'iddah, and nifas.

Manumission of a Live Child After Stillbirth: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on manumission under oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on which child is freed when a slave-girl gives birth to a stillborn then a live child under an oath, and explain the legal reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
A stillborn cannot receive freedom; Abu Hanifa restricts the oath to a live child, while the Sahibayn say no child is freed.

When a master says to his slave-girl, 'If you give birth to a child, he is free,' and she delivers a stillborn and then a live child, which child is freed? This lesson unpacks a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa frees only the live child, while his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), rule that no child is freed. The disagreement hinges on whether the term 'child' is implicitly restricted by the attribute of life when freedom is the intended recompense.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa: only the live child is freed. Note that the mufta bihi view is Abu Hanifa's.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that neither child is freed because the condition is fulfilled by the stillborn, but the stillborn cannot receive freedom, so the oath expires without recompense.
They consider the stillborn a 'child' for the condition but not a valid recipient.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the birth of a stillborn satisfies the condition 'give birth to a child,' as the term includes both live and stillborn births. Since the oath is conditional, its fulfillment dissolves the oath, but the recompense (freedom) cannot apply to a dead child.
They analogize to earlier rulings on divorce where a stillborn triggers the condition without restriction.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa restricts the word 'walad' (child) by the attribute of life (hayat) because the oath intends to bestow freedom, which is a benefit that only a living being can enjoy. Thus the oath is implicitly conditional on a live child.
He distinguishes this from divorce oaths, where the condition does not require a live child because the recompense (divorce) does not depend on the child's state.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, the live child is freed. Under the Sahibayn, no child is freed, and the oath expires. The master's intent to grant freedom is upheld by Abu Hanifa.
This affects the status of the child and the master's financial liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ إِذَا وَلَدْتِ وَلَدًا فَهُوَ حُرِّفَوَلَدَتْ وَلَدًا مَيّتًا ثُمَّ آخَرَ حَيَّا عَتَقَ الْحَيُّ وَحْدَهُ عِنْدَابِي حَنِيفَةً وَقَالَا لَا يَعْتِقُ وَاحِدٌ مِنْهُمَا لِأَنَّ الشَّيْطَ قَدْتَحَقَّقَ بِوِلَادَةِ الْمَيِّتِ عَلَى مَا بَيَّنَّا فَيَنْحَلُّ الْيَمِينُ لَا إِلَى جَزَاءِ لِاَنَّ الْمَيِّتَ لَيْسَ بِمَحَلَّ لِلْحُرِّيَّةِ وَهِيَ الْجَزَاءُ وَلَابِي حَنِيفَةٌ أَنَّ مُطْلَقَ اِسْمِ الْوَلَدِمُقَيَّدٌ بِوَصْفِ الْحَيَوةِ لِأَنَّهُ قُصِدَ إِثْبَاثَ الْحُرِّيَّةِ جَزَاءً وَهِيَ قَوَّةٌ حِكْمِيَّةٌ تَظْهَرُ فِي دَفْعِ تَسَلُّطِ الْغَيْرِ وَلَا تَبْتُ فِي الْمَيِّتِ فَيَتَقَيَّدُ بِوَصْفِ الْحَيوةِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَ اِذَاوَلَدْتِ وَلَدَا حَيَّا بِخِلَافِ جَزَاءِ الطَّلَاقِ وَحُرِّيَّةِ الأُمِّ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَصْلُحُ مُقَيّدًا ترجمہ اگر اپنی باندی سے کہا کہ اگر تم کو بچہ ہوا تو وہ آزاد ہو گا۔ اس کے بعد اس باندی کو پہلے ایک مردہ بچہ پیدا ہو پھر دوسرا بچہ زندہ پیدا ہو تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک فقط زندہ آزاد ہوگا اور صاحبین نے کہا ہے کہ ان دونوں میں سے ایک بھی آزاد نہ ہوگا کیونکہ مرے ہوئے بچہ کی پیدائش سے شرط پائی گئی کیونکہ مردہ بچہ بھی پیدا شدہ بچہ ہوتا ہے تو قسم بغیر جزاء کے واقع ہوئی کیونکہ مردہ بچہ تو کسی طرح بھی آزادی پانے کے قابل نہیں ہے۔ حالانکہ قسم کی جزاء میں آزاد ہونا بھی ہے۔ اس لئے یہ قسم جزاء کے بغیر پائی گئی اور امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مطلقاً بچہ اس کو کہا جاتا ہے جس میں علامت زندگی موجود ہو کیونکہ مولیٰ نے شرط کی جزاء کے طور پر اس کی آزادی کا ارادہ کیا ہے اور آزادی تو حکما ایک ایسی قوت کا نام ہے جو دوسرے کسی کی زبردستی اور تسلط کو اپنے اوپر برداشت نہیں کرتی ہے بلکہ اسے دور کرتی ہے۔ مگر ایسی قوت تو کسی مردہ میں ثابت نہیں ہوسکتی ہے۔ لہذا بچہ میں زندگی کے وصف پائے جانے کا اعتبار کیا گیا ہے اور یہ سمجھا جائے گا کہ مولیٰ نے یہ کہا تھا کہ اگر تم نے زندہ بچہ جنا تو وہ بچہ آزاد ہوگا اور یہ مسئلہ طلاق یا ام ولد کی آزادی کی جزاء کے برخلاف ہے۔ یعنی پہلے مسئلہ میں بچہ کے زندہ ہونے کی شرط نہیں ہے کیونکہ بیوی کی طلاق اور باندی کی آزادی ایسی چیز نہیں ہے جو بچہ کے زندہ ہونے کا تقاضا کرتی ہو۔
And if he said: 'When you give birth to a child, he is free,' and she gave birth to a dead child, then another live one, the live one alone is freed according to Abu Hanifa. And they (the two) said: none of them is freed, because the condition is already fulfilled by the birth of the dead one, as we explained, so the oath is dissolved without a recompense, because the dead one is not a locus for freedom which is the recompense. And for Abu Hanifa, the absolute name 'child' is restricted by the attribute of life, because he intended to establish freedom as a recompense, and freedom is a legal force that appears in repelling the domination of others, and it is not established in the dead, so it is restricted to the attribute of life. Thus it becomes as if he said: 'When you give birth to a live child.' Unlike the recompense of divorce or freedom of the mother, because that does not require restriction.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about which child is freed when a slave-girl gives birth first to a stillborn and later to a live child, under the oath 'if you give birth to a child, he is free.' Imam Abu Hanifa frees only the live child because he argues that the term 'child' in the context of manumission is implicitly restricted to a live child, since freedom is a quality that cannot be bestowed upon a dead body. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that no child is freed because the condition was already fulfilled by the stillborn, but since a stillborn cannot receive freedom, the oath becomes void. They analogize this to the earlier ruling on divorce where a stillborn triggers the condition, but Abu Hanifa distinguishes divorce from manumission: divorce does not require the child to be alive because it is a legal dissolution irrespective of the child's state, whereas freedom is a grant of a benefit (the ability to own oneself) that only makes sense for a living being. Therefore, the oath is interpreted as conditional on a live birth.

Key Terms
جَزَاءٌ (jaza'): The recompense or consequence of the oath, i.e., freedom in this context.
مَحَلٌّ (mahall): The locus or subject; the dead child is not a valid locus for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Neither child is freed
B) Only the stillborn is freed
C) Only the live child is freed
D) Both children are freed

Key Takeaway
For Abu Hanifa, the recipient of freedom must be alive, so the oath is interpreted as conditional on a live child, resulting in the live child's freedom.

Interpreting 'First Slave' in an Oath of Manumission
Ruling on interpretation of 'first slave' in manumission oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi interpretation of 'awwal' (first) to determine which slave is freed under an oath of manumission involving multiple purchases.

Core Idea
'First' requires both singularity and precedence; without both, no slave is freed.

This passage from al-Hidayah explores the linguistic precision of oaths. The word 'awwal' (first) is not merely chronological but also implies that the purchase is solitary. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying conditional manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath wording and condition
Read the oath carefully. In the first scenario, the condition is 'The first slave I purchase is free.' In the second, '...alone is free.'
2
Define 'first' (awwal) in Hanafi legal terminology
Awwal means a singular individual that precedes all others. It requires both that the purchase be solitary (no other purchased at the same time) and that it be the earliest (no prior purchase).
This is a qualitative, not merely chronological, definition.
3
Apply to a single purchase scenario
If the master purchases one slave alone, that slave meets both conditions: he is the only one at that time (singularity) and he is the first (precedence). He is freed.
4
Apply to simultaneous purchase of two slaves
If two slaves are purchased together, neither is 'first' because each lacks singularity (the other is present). The third later purchase is not first because it lacks precedence (there were purchases before it).
The condition fails completely; no slave is freed.
5
Apply with the addition of 'alone' (wahdahu)
Adding 'alone' shifts the condition to require that the purchase be solitary, not that it be chronologically first. Thus, if two are bought together and then one later, the later one is the first solitary purchase and is freed.
Linguistically, 'wahdahu' qualifies the state of purchase, not the order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قَالَ أَوَّلُ عَبْدٍ أَشْتَرِيهِ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ فَاشْتَرَى عَبْدًا عَتَقَ لِأَنَّ الْأَوَّلَ اسْمٌ لِفَرْدٍ سَابِقٍ فَإِنِ اشْتَرَى عَبْدَيْنِ مَعًا ثُمَّ آخَرَ لَمْ يَعْتِقْ وَاحِدٌ مِنْهُمْ لِانْعِدَامِ التَّفَرُّدِ فِي الْأَوَّلَيْنِ وَالسَّبْقِ فِي الثَّالِثِ فَانْعَدَمَتِ الْأَوَّلِيَّةُ وَإِنْ قَالَ أَوَّلُ عَبْدٍ أَشْتَرِيهِ وَحْدَهُ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ عَتَقَ الثَّالِثُ لِأَنَّهُ يُرَادُ بِهِ التَّفَرُّدُ فِي حَالَةِ الشِّرَاءِ لِأَنَّ وَحْدَهُ لِلْحَالِ لُغَةً وَالثَّالِثُ سَابِقٌ فِي هَذَا الْوَصْفِ
And if he said, 'The first slave I purchase is free,' and he purchased a slave, he becomes free because 'first' is a name for a preceding individual. If he purchased two slaves together, then another, none of them becomes free due to the absence of singularity in the first two and precedence in the third, so 'firstness' is absent. And if he said, 'The first slave I purchase alone is free,' the third becomes free because by it is meant singularity in the state of purchase, since 'alone' (wahdahu) refers to the state linguistically, and the third is preceding in this description.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage explains the interpretation of 'first' in an oath of manumission. The condition requires that the purchased slave be both singular (no other purchased simultaneously) and preceding (no earlier purchase). If the master purchases one slave alone, that slave is freed. If he purchases two at the same time, neither is 'first' because they lack singularity (each is not the only one). A subsequent third purchase is not first because it is not preceding. However, if the master says 'the first slave I purchase alone' (adding 'wahdahu' meaning 'alone'), then the third purchase (after the simultaneous two) qualifies because it is the first purchase that was solitary. The word 'alone' qualifies the state of purchase, not the absolute order. Thus, the third slave is the first one purchased in a solitary state.

Key Terms
أَوَّل (awwal): A qualitative term requiring both singularity (no other with it) and precedence (no earlier).
وَحْدَهُ (wahdahu): Alone; indicates that the purchase state is solitary, not that the slave is the first chronologically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both are free
B) The first one mentioned is free
C) Neither is free
D) The cheaper one is free

Key Takeaway
The word 'awwal' in oaths of manumission requires both singularity and precedence; adding 'wahdahu' changes the condition to solitary purchase only.

Timing of Manumission: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on timing and inheritance scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on when the 'last slave' becomes free, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf involving inheritance from all wealth vs. one-third.

Core Idea
The slave is free from purchase because death merely reveals existing lastness.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath to free the last slave purchased. The key question is whether the slave's freedom takes effect at the time of purchase or at the master's death. The answer determines whether the slave is valued from the entire estate or from one-third, affecting heirs' shares.

Guided Path
1
Understand the oath and its conditions
The master says: 'The last slave I purchase is free.' This creates a suspended manumission (ta'liq) that activates upon the condition being met. Identify that the term 'last' requires a preceding purchase; a single purchase cannot be 'last'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
If only one slave is bought, that slave is not free because there is no preceding purchase to make him 'last'. If two are bought sequentially, the second becomes 'last'.
3
Compare Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on timing
Abu Hanifa holds the slave becomes free from the day of purchase (retroactively upon death). The Sahibayn hold freedom occurs at death. This is the core dispute.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa: death is a revealer (mu'arrif) of what was already true—the slave was actually the last from purchase because no later purchase occurred. The Sahibayn: lastness is only certain at death, so the condition is fulfilled then.
The dispute hinges on whether lastness is a present fact or future-dependent.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
If freedom is from purchase, the slave is treated as free from that moment, so his value comes from the entire estate (not subject to the one-third limit on bequests). If at death, it is like a bequest, limited to one-third.
Practical implications: contracts made with the slave between purchase and death (e.g., sale, marriage) are affected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ آخَرُ عَبْدٍ أَشْتَرِيهِ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ فَاشْتَرَى عَبْدًا وَمَاتَ لَمْ يَعْتِقْ لِأَنَّ الْآخَرَ اسْمُ فَرْدٍ لَاحِقٍ لَا سَابِقَ لَهُ فَلَا يَكُونُ لَاحِقًا وَلَوِ اشْتَرَى عَبْدًا ثُمَّ عَبْدًا ثُمَّ مَاتَ عَتَقَ الْآخَرُ لِأَنَّهُ فَرْدٌ لَاحِقٌ فَاتَّصَفَ بِالْآخِرِيَّةِ وَيَعْتِقُ يَوْمَ اشْتَرَاهُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ حَتَّى يُعْتَبَرَ مِنْ جَمِيعِ الْمَالِ وَقَالَا يَعْتِقُ يَوْمَ مَاتَ حَتَّى يُعْتَبَرَ مِنَ الثُّلْثِ لِأَنَّ الْآخِرِيَّةَ لَا تَثْبُتُ إِلَّا بِعَدَمِ شِرَاءٍ غَيْرِهِ بَعْدَهُ وَذَلِكَ يَتَحَقَّقُ بِالْمَوْتِ فَكَانَ الشَّرْطُ مُتَحَقِّقًا عِنْدَ الْمَوْتِ فَيُقْتَصَرُ عَلَيْهِ وَلِأَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ الْمَوْتَ مُعَرِّفٌ فَأَمَّا اتِّصَافُهُ بِالْآخِرِيَّةِ مِنْ وَقْتِ الشِّرَاءِ فَيَثْبُتُ مُسْتَنِدًا
And if he said, 'The last slave I purchase is free,' and he purchased a slave and died, it is not free because 'last' is the name of a subsequent individual without any preceding one, so it is not subsequent. If he purchased a slave, then another slave, then died, the later one becomes free because it is a subsequent individual and thus qualifies as last. And it is considered free on the day he purchased it according to Abu Hanifa, so it is accounted for from all of his wealth. And the two (Sahibayn) said: It becomes free on the day he died, so it is accounted for from one-third. Because 'lastness' is only established by the absence of any other purchase after it, and that is realized by death, so the condition is realized at death and so it is confined to it. And for Abu Hanifa, death is a revealer; as for its qualification as 'lastness' from the time of purchase, it is established retroactively.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage addresses an oath where a master says, 'The last slave I purchase is free.' If he purchases a single slave and dies without purchasing any other, that slave is not free because 'last' implies a subsequent slave after a previous one. However, if he purchases two slaves sequentially (first one, then another) and then dies, the second slave is the last and becomes free. The dispute arises regarding the timing of freedom. Abu Hanifa holds that the slave becomes free from the moment of purchase (retroactively upon death), so the slave is considered free from the time of purchase, and thus the manumission is accounted from the entire estate. The Sahibayn argue that the condition of being 'last' is only certain at death, so freedom takes effect at the moment of death, and therefore the slave is freed from one-third of the estate (like a bequest). Abu Hanifa responds that death merely reveals what was already the case: that no further purchase occurred, so the 'lastness' existed from the time of purchase. The practical difference appears in matters of inheritance and transactions involving the slave between purchase and death.

Key Terms
آخِر (akhir): The last; a subsequent individual with no further purchase after it.
آخِرِيَّة (akhariyya): The quality of being last; established by the absence of any purchase after it.
ثُلُث (thuluth): One-third of the estate; the limit up to which bequests are valid from the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says, 'The last slave I purchase is free.' He purchases one slave, then later another, then dies. According to Abu Hanifa, when does the second slave become free?
A) Immediately upon his death
B) Retroactively from the day he was purchased
C) At the moment of the master's death
D) He never becomes free

Key Takeaway
In a conditional manumission for the 'last slave', the timing of freedom depends on whether 'lastness' is considered a present fact (Abu Hanifa: from purchase, from all wealth) or a future certainty (Sahibayn: at death, from one-third).

Last Wife Triple Divorce: When Does It Take Effect?
Intra-Hanafi dispute: timing of suspended triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the timing of a suspended triple divorce on the 'last wife' and state its effect on inheritance.

Core Idea
Death reveals the last wife, but the timing of the divorce determines inheritance.

This brief passage extends a classic Hanafi dispute from the context of manumission (last slave) to divorce. The scenario: a man says, 'The last woman I marry is divorced by three.' He marries two women sequentially and dies. The key question: when does the divorce take effect — at the moment of marriage (upon death) or at the moment of death? The answer determines whether the second wife inherits from him. This intra-madhhab difference has a concrete practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
A man makes a suspended condition: 'The last woman I marry is divorced by three.' He marries a woman, then another, then dies. This triggers the dispute.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa holds that the divorce of the second wife is effective from the time of marriage (retroactively upon death), so she is no longer a wife at the moment of death and does not inherit.
This is based on the view that death reveals who is the 'last', but the condition is fulfilled at the time of the marriage.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the divorce takes effect at the moment of death, because it is only then that the condition is certain. Hence, she is still his wife at death and inherits.
For the Sahibayn, death is the effective cause (sabab) that completes the condition.
4
Trace the Analogy to the Last Slave
The same reasoning applies as in the case of a man who says, 'The last slave I buy is free.' The two imams differ on whether the freedom (or divorce) is effective at the time of purchase (Abu Hanifa) or at death (Sahibayn).
This analogy is the dalil for both positions.
5
Extract the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference is inheritance: according to Sahibayn, the wife inherits; according to Abu Hanifa, she does not. This affects estate distribution.
The mufta bihi in many Hanafi texts is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعَلَى هَذَا الْخِلَافِ تَعْلِيقُ الطَّلَقَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ بِهِ وَفَائِدَتُهُ تَظْهَرُ فِي جَرَيَانِ الْإِرْثِ وَعَدَمِهِ
And on this difference is the suspension of three divorces by it, and its benefit appears in the flow of inheritance and its absence.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This brief passage applies the same dispute regarding 'last slave' to the context of divorce. If a man says, 'The last woman I marry is divorced by three,' and he marries a woman and then another and then dies, the same khilaf applies. According to Abu Hanifa, the second wife is considered divorced from the time of marriage (retroactively upon death), so she does not inherit from him because a divorced woman does not inherit. According to the Sahibayn, the divorce takes effect at the moment of death, so she is still his wife at that moment and thus inherits. This is a practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) that affects inheritance rights.

Key Terms
تعلِيقُ الطَّلَقَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ (ta'liq al-talaqat al-thalath): Suspension of triple divorce on a condition.
إِرْث (irth): Inheritance; here, whether the wife inherits from the husband.
خِلَاف (khilaf): Difference of opinion; here, the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because her divorce is effective from the time of marriage, so she was never his wife.
B) Because the divorce takes effect at death, so she is still his wife at that moment.
C) Because triple divorce does not prevent inheritance.
D) Because the condition is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the timing of a suspended divorce on the 'last wife' affects inheritance: Abu Hanifa says no inheritance, Sahibayn says yes.

The Last Slave Purchased: When Does Freedom Take Effect?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on when the last slave is considered purchased for freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the timing of freedom for a conditionally freed last slave, and identify the practical consequences for inheritance and bequests.

Core Idea
The attribute of 'last' attaches at purchase (Abu Hanifa) or death (Sahibayn), altering whether the slave's value is deducted from all wealth or one-third.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The issue is whether a slave conditionally freed as 'the last I buy' becomes free from the moment of purchase or only upon the owner's death. This seemingly narrow question reveals a deeper principle about the timing of conditional outcomes (ta'alluq) and their effect on estate distribution. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how differing views on the nature of a condition produce divergent legal outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and the two scenarios
The condition is: 'The last slave I buy is free.' The text gives two scenarios: if the owner buys only one slave and dies, that slave is not freed because there is no prior slave to make him 'last'. If he buys two slaves and dies, the second is freed because he is the last.
The attribute of 'last' requires a preceding individual (sābiq).
2
State the point of dispute
Both sides agree the second slave is freed. The dispute is about the timing of that freedom: Abu Hanifa says the slave becomes free on the day of purchase; the Sahibayn say he becomes free on the day of death.
The timing directly affects from which part of the estate his value is deducted: all wealth (Abu Hanifa) or one-third (Sahibayn).
3
Follow the proof of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn argue: The attribute of being 'last' can only be definitively known at the moment of death, because only then is it certain that no further purchase will occur. Hence, the condition is fulfilled at death, and freedom takes effect at that moment.
This is a logical argument based on the need for certainty (yaqīn) in legal conditions.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's rebuttal
Abu Hanifa responds that death is merely an identifier (mu'arraf), not a condition of the attribute's existence. The slave was truly the last from the moment of purchase; death reveals that fact but does not create it. Therefore, the condition was satisfied at the time of purchase, and freedom is retroactive to that day.
This illustrates the Hanafi concept of istinād (retroactive attachment) – a condition can operate retrospectively.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If freedom dates from purchase, the slave's value is a debt on the entire estate, deducted before heirs' shares. If freedom dates from death, it is treated as a bequest limited to one-third of the estate. This difference can affect whether the slave is fully freed or must compensate the estate.
For example, if the slave's value exceeds one-third, under the Sahibayn he is not fully free unless the heirs agree.
6
Apply to the analogous case of conditional divorce
The same dispute applies if a man says: 'The last woman I marry is divorced thrice.' According to Abu Hanifa, the divorce occurs at the time of marriage, so she does not inherit (since a divorced woman does not inherit). According to the Sahibayn, the divorce occurs at death, so she does inherit (because the divorce was not final until death).
This demonstrates the broader application of the principle to conditional legal acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ آخِرُ عَبْدٍ أَشْتَرِيهِ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ فَاشْتَرَى عَبْدًا وَمَاتَ لَمْ يَعْتِقْ لِأَنَّ الْآخِرَ فَرْدٌ لَا حِقٌ لَهُ سَابِقٌ فَلَا يَكُونُ لَاحِقًا وَلَوِ اشْتَرَى عَبْدًا ثُمَّ عَبْدًا ثُمَّ مَاتَ عَتَقَ الْآخِرُ لِأَنَّهُ فَرْدٌ لَاحِقٌ فَاتَّصَفَ بِالْآخِرِيَّةِ وَيَعْتِقُ يَوْمَ اشْتَرَاهُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ حَتَّى يُعْتَبَرَ مِنْ جَمِيعِ الْمَالِ وَقَالَا يَعْتِقُ يَوْمَ مَاتَ حَتَّى يُعْتَبَرَ مِنَ الثُّلْثِ لِأَنَّ الْآخِرِيَّةَ لَا تَثْبُتُ إِلَّا بِعَدَمِ شِرَاءِ غَيْرِهِ بَعْدَهُ وَذَلِكَ يَتَحَقَّقُ بِالْمَوْتِ فَكَانَ الشَّرْطُ مُتَحَقِّقًا عِنْدَ الْمَوْتِ فَيُقْتَصَرُ عَلَيْهِ وَلِأَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ الْمَوْتَ مُعَرِّفٌ فَأَمَّا اتِّصَافُهُ بِالْآخِرِيَّةِ مِنْ وَقْتِ الشِّرَاءِ فَيَثْبُتُ مُسْتَنِدًا وَعَلَى هَذَا الْخِلَافِ تَعْلِيقُ الطَّلَقَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ بِهِ وَفَائِدَتُهُ تُظْهَرُ فِي جَرَيَانِ الْإِرْثِ وَعَدَمِهِ
And if he said, 'The last slave I buy is free,' then he bought a slave and died, he is not freed because 'the last' is an individual with no preceding individual, so he is not a follower. And if he bought a slave then a slave then died, the last is freed because he is a follower and thus described as last. And he becomes free on the day he bought him according to Abu Hanifa, so that he is considered from all the wealth. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He becomes free on the day he died, so that he is considered from the third, because the attribute of being last is only established by the absence of buying another after him, and that is realized by death, so the condition is realized at death, so it is restricted to that. And for Abu Hanifa, death is an identifier; as for his being described as last from the time of purchase, it is established retroactively. And upon this difference is the suspension of three divorces upon it, and its benefit appears in the running of inheritance or not.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the condition of being the last slave purchased. The text first presents two scenarios: if the owner buys only one slave and dies, that slave is not freed because there is no prior slave to make him 'last'. If he buys two slaves and dies, the second is freed because he is the last. The dispute arises regarding the timing of freedom: Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is freed from the day of purchase, so his value is deducted from the entire estate before distribution to heirs. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that freedom occurs at the moment of death, so the slave is considered from only one-third of the estate, as if it were a bequest. The reasoning: the attribute of being 'last' can only be definitively known at death, when no further purchase is possible. Abu Hanifa counters that death merely reveals what was true from the time of purchase. The same difference applies to a conditional divorce: if a man says, 'The last woman I marry is divorced thrice,' then marries two women and dies, the second wife inherits according to the Sahibayn (because divorce at death does not prevent inheritance), but according to Abu Hanifa, the divorce is retroactive to the time of marriage, so she does not inherit.

Key Terms
آخر (ākhir): The last in a sequence; in this context, the slave who is the final one purchased.
الآخريّة (al-ākhiriyya): The attribute of being the last; its establishment is the point of dispute.
الثُّلْث (ath-thulth): One-third of the estate, the limit for bequests and conditional freedoms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the moment of purchase
B) From the moment of death
C) From the moment the owner becomes ill
D) From the moment the owner's will is executed

Key Takeaway
The timing of freedom for the last purchased slave depends on whether the attribute of 'last' is considered to attach at purchase (Abu Hanifa) or only at death (Sahibayn), with significant consequences for inheritance and bequest limits.
