Buying One's Father for Expiation: Cause vs. Condition
Inter-madhhab dispute on oath and expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on buying one's father for oath expiation, identify the opposing Shafi'i/Zufar position, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf arising from the different classifications of 'illa and shart.

Core Idea
Ownership is the cause of freedom; kinship is a condition, so buying father suffices for expiation.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute about whether buying one's father can fulfill the expiation of an oath. The Hanafi school affirms it, while the Shafi'i school and Imam Zufar deny it. At the heart of the dispute lies a fundamental question: what is the effective cause of freedom—ownership or kinship? By working through this dispute, you will strengthen your ability to analyze ikhtilaf and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of different legal theories.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: buying one's father with the intention of oath expiation is valid and fulfills the expiation.
This is based on ownership as the cause of freedom.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar hold that it does not suffice; they consider kinship (father-son relationship) as the cause of freedom.
For them, ownership is merely a condition.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent's dalil is that the cause of freedom in buying a relative is the inherent relationship (qarabah), not the act of purchase. Therefore, the intention for expiation is not connected to the correct cause.
They analogize to other forms of freeing relatives.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that ownership (milk) is the cause and kinship is a condition. The hadith 'Lan yajzi waladun walidahu...' shows that buying and freeing a father has special merit, and the Arab linguistic example 'saqahu fa-rawahu' demonstrates that when two actions are connected with 'fa', the second results from the first. Thus, the purchase is the cause of freedom.
The hadith is cited as direct proof.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: If a person buys his father for expiation, Hanafis rule the expiation is fulfilled; Shafi'is/Zufar rule it is not, requiring a separate expiation.
This affects the validity of the oath and the obligation to repeat expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في العتق والطلاق. اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم. وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ امام مسلم اور اصحاب سنن اربعہ نے اس کو روایت کیا ہے۔ اس میں خریدنے کو ہی آزادی قرار دیا ہے۔ کیونکہ اس حدیث میں دوسری کوئی بھی شرط نہیں لگائی۔ تو اس کی نظیر عرب کا یہ قول ہوا کہ سفاہ فارواہ۔ یعنی اسے پانی پلا کر سیراب کر دیا ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے فلاں متعین شخص کو خرید کرنے پر کہا کہ وہ (فلاں) آزاد ہے، پھر اس نے کفارہ قسم سے آزاد کرنے کی نیت سے خرید کر لیا تو اس صورت میں اس ( حالف) کا کفارہ ادا نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ علت عتق ( آزادی کی وجہ ) قسم کے ساتھ اتصال نیست شرط ہے۔ یعنی جب بھی اسے (فلاں کو) خریدا تو وہ (فلاں) خریدتے ہی آزاد ہو گیا۔ اور کفارہ کا جواز تب ہوتا کہ علت عشق یعنی کفارہ بالیمین کی نیست متصل ہو۔ چنانچہ یہاں یہ شرط موجود نہیں ہے بلکہ حق کی شرط شراء ( خریداری) کے ساتھ متصل ہوئی ہے لہذا کفارہ ادا نہ ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ مذکورہ صورت میں محقق پہلی (سابقہ) قسم ہے۔ اور شراء ( خریداری) عشق کی شرط ہے۔ چونکہ یہاں شراء کے ساتھ نیت متصل ہو گئی لہذا کفارہ ادا نہ ہوگا۔ با این وجہ کہ ادائے کفارہ کیلئے نیت کا علت کے ساتھ متصل ہونا مشروط ہے۔ چونکہ حقیقت میں علت عتق حالف کا قول هو حر ہے۔ لیکن تعلیق کے وقت اگر چہ اس کا علت ہونا ظاہر نہیں ہوتا۔ لیکن وجود شرط کے وقت محقق کا حکم سابقہ علت کی طرف منسوب ہوگا۔ چنانچہ یہ صورت یوں ہوگی کہ علت مقدم ہونے کے حوالے سے فقہاء کی تصریح ہے کہ قسم کھاتے وقت حالف کیلئے اہلیت شرط ہے، لیکن شرط کے تحقق (ثبوت) کیلئے اس کی اہمیت مشروط نہیں۔ اگر کسی نے کفارہ یمین کی نیت سے اپنے باپ کو خرید کر آزاد کر دیا تو احناف کے نزدیک ادائے کفارہ کی یہ صورت جائز ہوگی۔ جبکہ امام شافعی اور امام زفر کے ہاں (کفارہ کی مذکورہ صورت) جائز نہ ہوگی۔
مذکورہ صورت میں اختلاف کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ ادائے کفارہ میں کفارہ کے ادا ہونے کیلئے علت محقق کے ساتھ کفارہ کی نیت کا متصل ہونا تمام فقہاء کے نزدیک ضروری ہے۔ مذکورہ صورت میں امام زفر اور امام شافعی نے قرابت (باپ کی خریداری) کو آزادی کی وجہ ( علت عنق ) اور ملکیت کو اسکی شرط قرار دے دیا ہے۔ جب کہ ہمارے ائمہ ثلاثہ امام مالک، امام مجد اور امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اس صورت کا حکم بر عکس ہے (یعنی ملکیت عشق کی علت ہے اور قرابت اس کی شرط ہے) چونکہ شریعت نے قریب کی خریداری کو اعتماق (آزاد ہوتا ) قرار دیا ہے۔ چنانچہ ایسی صورت میں لامحالہ ملکیت ہی محقق کی علت قرار پائے گی ، لہذا حالف کا اپنے باپ کو بہ نیت کفارہ خرید نانیت کو علت عشق کے متصل کردے گا۔ چنانچہ کفارہ ادا ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن ہر دو حضرات ( زفر و شافعی نے چونکہ قرابت کو علت قرار دیا ہے اس لئے علت نیت سے متصل نہ ہوئی لہذا کفارہ ادا نہ ہوگا لیکن ہمارا (احناف وغیرہ) قول ارشاد نبوی صلى الله عليه وسلم ( جسے بخاری کے علاوہ تمام محدثین نے روایت کیا ہے۔ اس حدیث کو متن میں بھی بیان کیا گیا ہے) عن سهل ابن أبي صالح عن ابيه عن ابي هريرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الله قَالَ لَنْ يُجْزِى وَلَدٌ وَالِدِهِ إِلَّا أَنْ يُجِدَهُ مَمْلُوكًا .... الخ
بیٹا اپنے باپ کی مکافات نہیں کرسکتا الا یہ کہ باپ کوکس کا مملوک (غلام) پا کر خرید کر لے پھر وہ آزاد ہو جائے) رواه مسلم والاربعہ، یعنی ترمزی و ابو داود ونسائی وابن ماجہ ۔ سے مؤید ہونے کی بنا پر رائج ہے۔ مزید یہ کہ ہمارے قول کی تائید میں اہل عرب کا مقولہ سقاه فارواہ بطور نظیر موجود ہے، جس کا مفہوم وخلاصہ یہ ہے جب اعتناق کا عطف شراء" - حرف ناء کے ساتھ ہو تو وہ کلام عرب میں تراخی بالزمان کا مقتضی ہوتا ہے۔ جب ایک فعل کا عطف دوسرے فعل پر حرف فاء کے ساتھ ہوتو دوسر افعل پہلے فعل کے ساتھ ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ کلام عرب میں اس کی مثال ہے۔ ضـربـه فـاوجـعه، اطعمه فاشیعه و سقاه فارواہ مذکورہ نظیر میں سقا فعل ہے اور اردو ابھی فعل ہے، ان دونوں ( فعلوں) کا عطف حرف ف کیسا تھ ہے۔ جس کا معنی ہے اس کو پانی پلا کر سیراب کر دیا۔ کلام عرب کی اس نظیر ( مثال) سے مذکورہ مسئلہ کی مطابقت (جو کہ ہماری یعنی احناف وغیرہ کی تائید کرتی ہے ) یوں ہوگی کہ جس طرح پانی پلانے کے فعل سے (پینے والے کے ) سیراب ہونے کا فعل ثابت ہوتا ہے اسی طرح قرابت کے خریدنے پر منی فعل سے عشق کا فعل ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
Chapter of oath regarding emancipation and divorce. Ashraf al-Bidaya sharh Urdu Hidaya volume six. He becomes free. Imam Muslim and the authors of the four Sunan narrated it. In it, buying is considered emancipation because no other condition is set in this hadith. Its analogy is the Arab saying 'saqahu fa-rawahu' meaning he gave him drink so he quenched. Explanation: If someone said about a specific person 'if I buy so-and-so, he is free', then bought him with the intention of expiating an oath, his expiation is not fulfilled. Because the cause of freedom must be connected to the oath; here the intention was connected to the purchase, not the oath. However, if one buys his father with intention of expiation, the Hanafis say it is valid, while Shafi'i and Zufar say it is not. The summary of the difference: all agree that intention must be connected to the cause. Zufar and Shafi'i consider kinship as the cause and ownership as condition; our three imams (Malik, [Mujd?], Abu Hanifa) consider ownership as the cause and kinship as condition. Supported by hadith: 'Lā yujzī waladun wālidahu illā an yajidahu mamlūkan...' narrated by Muslim and the four, and the Arab analogy 'saqahu fa-rawahu'.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute about whether buying a specific person with the intention of fulfilling an oath expiation counts as valid. The passage begins by stating the general ruling: if a person swears 'if I buy so-and-so, he is free' and then buys him with the intention of expiation, the expiation is not fulfilled because the cause of freedom (the oath statement) was not connected to the intention at the moment of purchase. The intention was attached to the purchase, not the oath cause. However, in the case of buying one's father, the Hanafi position is that it does fulfill the expiation, because according to the Hanafis (and Malik and another imam), ownership is the cause of freedom and kinship is a condition. The Shafi'i and Zufar hold the opposite: kinship is the cause, ownership is condition. The Hanafi proof is the hadith about a child not being able to compensate a father except by finding him as a slave and freeing him, and the Arab linguistic example 'saqahu fa-rawahu' which shows that when two actions are connected with 'fa' the second results from the first. Similarly, buying a relative causes freedom. The authority weight is from the Hidayah commentary, likely based on Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): The legal cause that brings about the ruling; here the cause of emancipation.
كفارة يمين (kaffarat yamin): Expiation for a broken oath, by freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing.
شرط (shart): A condition that must exist for the ruling to apply, but is not the direct cause.
قرابة (qarabah): Kinship, the familial relationship (here father-son).
تملك (tamalluk): Ownership, the act of acquiring possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the cause ('illa) of freedom when a person buys his father with the intention of oath expiation?
A) Kinship (father-son relationship)
B) The act of purchase (ownership)
C) The oath itself
D) The intention of expiation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that buying one's father for oath expiation is valid because ownership is the cause of freedom, whereas Shafi'i and Zufar hold kinship as the cause, leading to opposite practical rulings.

Buying One's Own Umm Walad for Oath Expiation
Ruling on buying one's own Umm Walad for oath expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using purchase of one's own Umm Walad for oath expiation and explain why it does not suffice.

Core Idea
Freedom already established by another cause cannot be attributed to an oath expiation.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Al-Hidayah: if a man buys his own Umm Walad with the intention of fulfilling an oath expiation, does it count? The answer is no, despite the slave becoming free. Understanding this subtle distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of expiation and the special status of the Umm Walad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The man has a slave-girl who is his Umm Walad (she bore him a child). He makes a conditional oath: 'If I buy you, you are free as expiation for my oath.' Then he buys her.
The condition is fulfilled upon purchase.
2
Determine the first legal effect: freedom
Because the condition of purchase occurred, she becomes free. Her freedom is actualized.
This is a direct result of the condition.
3
Determine the second legal effect: expiation
The freedom does not count as expiation for the oath. Why? Because her freedom was already established (mustahiqqa) by her status as Umm Walad.
The freedom is attributed to her Umm Walad status, not to the oath.
4
Contrast with a regular slave-girl
If the same condition is applied to a regular slave-girl (not Umm Walad), then upon purchase, both freedom and expiation are valid. The difference is that the regular slave's freedom is not pre-established by another cause.
The key is the prior entitlement to freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ لَوِ اشْتَرَى أُمَّ وَلَدِهِ لَمْ يَجُزْ وَ مَعْنَى هَذَا الْمَسْأَلَةِ أَنْ يَقُولَ لَامَةٍ قَدِ اسْتَوْلَدَهَا بِالنِّكَاحِ إِنِ اشْتَرَيْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ حُرَّةٌ عَنْ كَفَّارَةِ يَمِيْنِى ثُمَّ اشْتَرَاهَا فَإِنَّهَا تَعْتِقُ لِوُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ وَلَا يُجْزِيْهِ عَنِ الْكَفَّارَةِ لِأَنَّ حُرِّيَّتَهَا مُسْتَحِقَّةٌ بِالاسْتِبْلَادِ فَلَا تُضَافُ إِلَى الْيَمِينِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا قَالَ لِقِنَّةٍ إِنِ اشْتَرَيْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ حُرَّةٌ عَنْ كَفَّارَةِ يَمِينِ حَيْثُ يُجْزِيْهِ عَنْهَا إِذَا اشْتَرَاهَا لَاتَ حُرِّيَّتَهَا غَيْرُ مُسْتَحِقَّةٌ بِجِهَةٍ أُخْرَى فَلَمْ يَخْتَلَّ الْإِضَافَةُ إِلَى الْيَمِينِ وَقَدْ قَارَنَتْهُ الْبَيِّه
And if he buys his Umm Walad, it does not suffice. The meaning of this case: he says to a slave-girl whom he has made an Umm Walad through marriage (i.e., had a child with), 'If I buy you, you are free as expiation of my oath,' then he buys her. She becomes free due to the existence of the condition, but it does not suffice for his expiation because her freedom is already established by being an Umm Walad (by her childbearing), so it is not attributed to the oath in any way. Unlike if he says to a pure slave-girl (who is not an Umm Walad), 'If I buy you, you are free as expiation of my oath,' and he buys her, then it does suffice because her freedom is not already established by another cause, so the attribution to the oath is not impaired, and the intention accompanied it.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Hidayah. The case: a man who has a slave-girl who is his Umm Walad (i.e., she bore him a child) swears 'if I buy you, you are free as expiation for my oath.' When he buys her, she becomes free because the condition (buying) occurred, but this does not count as the expiation for his oath. Why? Because her freedom was already due to her status as Umm Walad (she becomes free upon his death anyway, and some say upon purchase she becomes free due to that status). Since the freedom is already established by another cause (being an Umm Walad), it cannot be attributed to the oath. In contrast, if he says this to a regular slave-girl (not his Umm Walad), then buying her does count as expiation because her freedom is not already established. The key principle: the freedom must be solely due to the oath for the expiation to be valid. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who bore a child to her master; she has special status and becomes free upon master's death.
كفارة يمين (kaffarat yamin): Expiation for a broken oath, here attempted via freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man buys his own Umm Walad (slave-girl who bore his child) with the intention of fulfilling an oath expiation, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The expiation is fulfilled and she is free.
B) The expiation is not fulfilled but she becomes free due to the purchase.
C) The expiation is fulfilled but she does not become free.
D) Neither the expiation nor the freedom occurs.

Key Takeaway
Freedom already established by another cause (like Umm Walad status) cannot be used to fulfill an oath expiation in Hanafi fiqh.

Oath Regarding Intercourse with a Slave-Girl: Scope of the Penalty
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath involving intercourse with a slave-girl, distinguish between owned and subsequently purchased slaves, and apply the qiyas with the divorce case.

Core Idea
The penalty of freedom only applies to slaves owned at the time of oath; later purchases are not covered.

This lesson examines a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding an oath where a man says, 'If I have intercourse with a slave-girl, she is free.' The passage clarifies that the indefinite noun 'slave-girl' generalizes to all individually owned slaves at the moment of the oath, but does not extend to slaves acquired afterward. We will explore the legal reasoning, the dispute with Imam Zufar, and the qiyas with a similar divorce case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (oath with condition of intercourse) and the default ruling (freedom for pre-owned slave-girls).
The act is an oath; the penalty is freeing the slave.
2
Analyze the linguistic generality
The word 'jāriyah' (slave-girl) is indefinite (munkarrah) in the condition, which in Hanafi usul generalizes to every individual slave-girl. Understand that this generality only covers those in one's possession at the time of the oath.
Indefinite noun in a condition does not automatically cover future acquisitions.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare the majority position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) with Zufar's view that mentioning intercourse implies ownership, so any slave with whom intercourse occurs is covered. Note the difference in outcome.
Zufar's qiyas differs by treating the mention of intercourse as equivalent to mention of ownership.
4
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal via qiyas
The Hanafi majority uses qiyas with a divorce case: saying to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, my slave is free' does not trigger freedom if he later marries her and divorces her, because the penalty does not attach to a condition that arises after the oath. Similarly, buying a new slave after the oath does not trigger freedom.
The condition (ownership) is a necessity for validity but does not extend to the penalty.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority position is relied upon for fatwa: only pre-owned slaves are covered. Zufar's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.
Authority weight: Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ قَالَ إِنْ تَسَرَّيْتُ جَارِيَةً فَهِيَ حُرَّةٌ فَتَسَرَى جَارِيَةٌ كَانَتْ فِي مِلْكِهِ عَتَقَتْ لِأَنَّ الْيَمِيْنَ الْعَقَدَتْ فِي حَقِهَا لِمُصَادَفَتِهَا الْمِلْكَ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ الْجَارِيَّةَ مُنَكِّرَةٌ فِي هَذَا الشَّرْطِ فَيَتَنَاوَلُ كُلَّ جَارِيَةٍ عَلَى الْإِنْفِرَادِ وَإِنِ اشْتَرَى جَارِيَةً فَتَسَرَّاهَا لَمْ تَعْتِقْ بِهَذِهِ الْيَمِينِ خِلَافًا لِزُفَرَ فَإِنَّهُ يَقُولُ التَّسَرِّي لَا يَصِحُ إِلَّا فِي الْمِلْكِ فَكَانَ ذِكْرُهُ ذِكْرَ الْمِلْكِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَ لِأَجْنَبِيَّةٍ إِنْ طَلَّقْتُكِ فَعَبْدِي حُرٌّ يَصِيرُ التَّزَوُّجُ مَذْكُورًا وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْمِلْكَ يَصِيرُ مَذْكُورًا ضَرُورَةَ صِحَّةِ التَّسَرِّي وَهُوَ شَرْطٌ فَيَتَقَدَّرُ بِقَدْرِهِ فَلَا يَظْهَرُ فِي حَقِّ صِحَّةِ الْجَزَاءِ وَهُوَ الْحُرِّيَّةُ وَفِي مَسْأَلَةِ الطَّلَاقِ إِنَّمَا يَظْهَرُ فِي حَقِّ الشَّرْطِ دُونَ الْجَزَاءِ حَتَّى لَوْ قَالَ لَهَا إِنْ طَلَّقْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا فَتَزَوَّجَهَا وَطَلَّقَهَا لَا تُطَلَّقُ ثَلَاثًا فَهَذِهِ وِزَانُ مَسْأَلَتِنَا
And whoever says 'If I have intercourse (tasarri) with a slave-girl, she is free,' and then he has intercourse with a slave-girl that is already in his ownership, she becomes free because the oath takes effect regarding her since it coincides with ownership. This is because the word 'slave-girl' is indefinite in this condition, so it covers every single slave-girl. But if he buys a slave-girl and then has intercourse with her, she does not become free by this oath, contrary to Zufar, who says that tasarri is only valid in ownership, so mentioning it is mentioning ownership, so it becomes like if he said to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, my slave is free' where marriage becomes mentioned. Our argument: ownership becomes mentioned necessarily for the validity of tasarri, and it is a condition, so it is limited to its own extent and does not appear in the validity of the penalty (freedom). And in the divorce case, it appears in the condition not the penalty, such that if he says to her 'If I divorce you, you are thrice divorced,' then marries her and divorces her, she is not thrice divorced. This is analogous to our case.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof from the Hanafi perspective, along with an intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar. The ruling: if a person says 'If I have intercourse with a slave-girl, she is free,' and he then has intercourse with a slave-girl already in his possession, she becomes free. Why? Because the oath attached to the act of intercourse, and at the time of the oath, the indefinite noun 'slave-girl' applies to each individual slave-girl he owns. However, if after the oath he buys a new slave-girl and then has intercourse with her, she does not become free by this oath. Zufar disagrees, arguing that mentioning intercourse implies ownership (since intercourse is only valid with one's own slave), so it is like mentioning ownership. The Hanafi response: yes, ownership is implied as a condition for the validity of intercourse, but it remains just a condition and does not extend to the penalty (freedom). They draw an analogy: if someone swears to a foreign woman 'If I divorce you, my slave is free,' then marries her and divorces her, the slave does not become free because the divorce (penalty) cannot occur in that context. Similarly, here the freedom (penalty) is not triggered by intercourse with a subsequent purchase. The authority weight is from the Hidayah commentary; the ruling is part of Zahir al-Riwayah, while Zufar's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
تسري (tasarri): Having sexual intercourse with a slave-girl that one owns; also refers to taking a slave-girl as a concubine.
مُنَكَّرَة (munkarrah): An indefinite noun; here 'jāriyah' without alif lam means it applies to any slave-girl, not a specific one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (majority), if a person says 'If I have intercourse with a slave-girl, she is free' and later buys a new slave-girl and has intercourse with her, what is the ruling?
A) The new slave-girl becomes free.
B) The new slave-girl does not become free.
C) The oath is invalid.
D) The new slave-girl becomes free only if the person intended her at the time of the oath.

Key Takeaway
The penalty of an oath attaches only to the situation existing at the time of the oath; later acquisitions do not trigger the penalty, as demonstrated by the qiyas with the divorce case.

Generalized Oath of Manumission: Which Slaves Are Freed?
States ruling for each slave category upon a generalized oath of freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule regarding generalized oaths of manumission to different categories of slaves (maktab, umm walad, mudabbar) and justify the distinction based on the completeness of possessory rights (yad).

Core Idea
A general oath to free all slaves frees only those with full possessory rights; a maktab is excluded unless specifically intended.

When a master swears 'Every slave I own is free,' does the oath cover all slaves? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the completeness of the master's possessory rights (yad). In this lesson, we explore why a maktab (slave under contract of manumission) is not automatically freed, while an umm walad and mudabbarah are. The key lies in the legal criterion of full ownership (milk) encompassing both person (raqaba) and possession (yad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a generalized oath of manumission. The ruling is that it frees all slaves over whom the master has full ownership (raqaba and yad).
2
Examine the exception
The maktab is not freed unless the master specifically intends him. Why does the exception exist?
3
Analyze the proof (talil)
The proof is that the master's ownership does not include full possessory rights over the maktab. Evidence: the master does not own the maktab's earnings and cannot have intercourse with a female maktab.
This indicates incomplete yad.
4
Contrast with other categories
For umm walad and mudabbarah, the master has full yad: he can use their services and has full rights. Thus they are included automatically.
5
Derive the conditional
The generalization applies only when the master has complete yad; otherwise, specific intent is needed for the oath to take effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين فى العتق والطلاق. الْـمِلْكُ ثَابِتٌ فِيهِمْ رَقَبَةً وَيَدًا، وَلَا يَعْتِقُ مُكَاتَبُوهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يَنْوِيَهُمْ، لِأَنَّ الْـمِلْكَ غَيْرُ ثَابِتٍ يَدًا، وَلِهَذَا لَا يَمْلِكُ اكْتِسَابَهُ وَلَا يَحِلُّ وَطْءُ الْـمُكَاتَبَةِ، بِخِلَافِ أُمِّ الْوَلَدِ وَالْـمُدَبَّرَةِ، فَاخْتَلَفَ الْإِضَافَةُ فَلَا بُدَّ مِنَ النِّيَّةِ.
Chapter of oaths regarding manumission and divorce. Ownership is established over them in both person (raqaba) and possession (yad). His maktab slaves are not freed unless he intends them, because ownership is not established in terms of possession; therefore he does not own his earnings and intercourse with the maktab slave woman is not permissible, contrary to umm walad and mudabbarah. Thus the attribution differs, so intent is necessary.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on oaths concerning manumission and divorce. It gives the rule when someone says 'Every slave I own is free.' The Hanafi position: all slaves over whom full ownership (both raqaba and yad) is established are freed immediately. This includes umm walad (mother of his child) and mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death). However, a maktab (slave under contract of manumission) is not freed by this general oath unless the master specifically intended him. The reason is that while the master owns the maktab's person (raqaba), he does not have full possessory rights (yad) because the maktab owns his own earnings and can manage his affairs. Additionally, the master cannot have intercourse with a maktab slave woman, whereas he can with umm walad and mudabbarah. This difference in legal effects shows the master's relation to the maktab is incomplete, so the oath's general wording does not apply to him without specific intent.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitabah with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thus gaining certain rights and eventual freedom upon master's death.
مدبرة (mudabbarah): A female slave whose master has declared that she will be free upon his death (tadbir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a maktab slave not become free when the master swears 'Every slave I own is free'?
A) Because the maktab is not owned at all.
B) Because the master does not have full possessory rights (yad) over the maktab.
C) Because the maktab is not Muslim.
D) Because the oath only applies to male slaves.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a general oath to free all slaves only applies to those over whom the master has full possessory rights (yad); a maktab requires specific intention.

Divorce with 'Or' and 'And' – Determining the Intended Wife
Determines which wives are divorced when using 'or' and 'and' in a single statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi linguistic rule for the particles 'aw' and 'wa' to determine the legal effect of a divorce statement involving multiple wives.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'this one or this one and this one,' only the last is divorced; the first two are at his option.

This lesson examines a specific legal scenario where a husband addresses multiple wives with a statement that mixes 'or' and 'and'. Understanding how Hanafi jurists interpret these Arabic particles is crucial for determining which wives are divorced and which remain under the husband's choice. The ruling hinges on the disjunctive function of 'aw' (option) and the conjunctive function of 'wa' (sharing the ruling).

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement structure
Note the phrasing: 'This one is divorced or this one and this one.' The first two wives are linked by 'or' (aw), and the third is joined to them by 'and' (wa). This structure is the basis of the ruling.
The statement is addressed to three wives, but the particles divide them into two groups.
2
Parse the effect of 'aw' (or)
In Arabic, 'aw' indicates a choice between options. Here, it gives the husband an option regarding the first two wives: he intended one of them, but not both. Therefore, neither is divorced immediately; he must choose which he meant.
This is a standard Hanafi interpretation; other schools may differ in contexts like oaths.
3
Parse the effect of 'wa' (and)
The conjunction 'wa' (and) indicates that the third wife shares the ruling of the previous phrase. Since the previous phrase ('this one is divorced or this one') does not yield a fixed divorce for the first two, the conjunction attaches to the concept of divorce itself, making the third wife divorced definitively.
The text compares this to saying 'One of you two is divorced, and this one (the third).'
4
Combine to derive the ruling
The third wife is immediately divorced. The first two wives are subject to the husband's choice; if he chooses one (by subsequent statement or action), that one is divorced. If he never chooses, no divorce occurs for them.
The husband's choice is a khiyar in the sense of determining his original intent, not a right to revoke.
5
Recognize the analogy (qiyas) to manumission
The same reasoning applies if a master says to his slaves: 'This one is free or this one and this one.' The last slave is freed, and the master has a choice regarding the first two.
This shows the rule is based on general linguistic principles, not specific to divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ لِنِسْوَةٍ لَهُ: هَذِهِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ هَذِهِ وَهَذِهِ طُلِقَتِ الْآخِرَةُ، وَلَهُ الْخِيَارُ فِي الْأَوَّلَيْنِ، لِأَنَّ كَلِمَةَ "أَوْ" لِإِثْبَاتِ أَحَدِ الْمَذْكُورَيْنِ، وَقَدْ أَدْخَلَهَا بَيْنَ الْأُولَيَيْنِ، ثُمَّ عَطَفَ الثَّالِثَةَ عَلَى الْمُطَلَّقَةِ، لِأَنَّ الْعَطْفَ لِلْمُشَارَكَةِ فِي الْحُكْمِ فَيَخْتَصُّ بِمَحَلِّهِ، فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَ: إِحْدَاكُمَا طَالِقٌ وَهَذِهِ. وَكَذَا إِذَا قَالَ لِعَبِيدِهِ: هَذَا حُرٌّ وَهَذَا وَهَذَا، عَتَقَ الْآخِرُ وَلَهُ الْخِيَارُ فِي الْأَوَّلَيْنِ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا.
And whoever says to his women: 'This one is divorced or this one and this one,' the last one is divorced, and he has a choice regarding the first two, because the word 'or' is for establishing one of the two mentioned, and he has inserted it between the first two, then he has conjuncted the third to the divorced one, because conjunction is for sharing in the ruling, so it attaches to its place. So it is as if he said: 'One of you two is divorced, and this one.' And likewise if he says to his slaves: 'This one is free and this one and this one,' the last one is free, and he has a choice regarding the first two for the reason we have explained.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a ruling dealing with a statement where a man addresses his wives using 'or' (أو) between the first two, then uses 'and' (و) for the third. The Hanafi ruling: only the last named wife is divorced; the first two are subject to his choice (he may decide which one of them he intended). The reasoning: the word 'or' in Arabic indicates a choice between two things, so it creates an option between the first two wives. The conjunction 'and' on the third wife joins her to the ruling of divorce, but since the first two are not definitively divorced (due to the 'or'), the conjunction applies to the effect that the third is divorced regardless, and the first two remain under his option. The text analogizes this to saying 'One of you two is divorced' and then 'and this one' to a third. The same rule applies to manumission of slaves. This is a linguistic analysis of the particles 'or' and 'and' in Islamic law.

Key Terms
أو (aw): The particle 'or' in Arabic, used for choice (takhyeer) between two or more options.
عطف (ʻaṭf): Grammatical conjunction; here, the use of 'wa' (and) to join the third wife to the previous statement, sharing the ruling of divorce.
خيار (khiyār): Option; the husband has the option to choose which of the first two wives he intended for divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the statement: 'This woman is divorced or this woman and this woman,' how many divorces occur immediately?
A) Three divorces.
B) Two divorces.
C) One divorce (the last one).
D) No divorce until choice is made.

Key Takeaway
In a divorce statement using 'or' between first two and 'and' for a third, only the third is immediately divorced; the first two are subject to the husband's choice.

Wakeel's Action in Personal Status Breaks Oath
Ruling on wakeel's action breaking oath in personal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that appointing a wakeel to marry, divorce, or manumit breaks the oath not to do so, and explain the legal reason (the wakeel as safir).

Core Idea
In personal status, the wakeel is a messenger; his act is the principal's, so the oath is broken.

This lesson examines a key nuance in Hanafi oath law: when an agent's action causes the principal to break an oath. While a wakeel in financial transactions acts independently, in marriage, divorce, and manumission he is considered a mere messenger (safir). The passage establishes the ruling and its reasoning, and also addresses the limitation of a subjective interpretation in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The person swears not to marry, divorce, or manumit. Then appoints a wakeel to do that act. The ruling: he breaks his oath (hantha).
2
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof is the legal reasoning (talil): the wakeel in these matters is a safir (messenger) and mu'abbar (interpreter). Therefore his action is attributed to the principal, not to himself.
3
Understand the concept of safir
A safir is a pure conduit of the principal's will, with no independent discretion. The rights and obligations of the contract return to the principal.
4
Contrast with financial agency
In financial transactions (e.g., sale), the wakeel is a true agent (wakil) with authority to contract, so his action does not automatically break an oath not to sell.
5
Consider the interpretation claim
If the swearer claims he meant only the verbal utterance (i.e., he swore not to speak the words), that interpretation is not accepted in court, though it may be valid between him and Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَتَزَوَّجُ أَوْ لَا يُطَلِّقُ أَوْ لَا يُعْتِقُ، فَوَكَّلَ بِذَلِكَ حَنِثَ، لِأَنَّ الْوَكِيلَ فِي هَذَا سَفِيرٌ وَمُعَبِّرٌ، وَلِهَذَا لَا يُضِيفُهُ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ بَلْ إِلَى الْآمِرِ، وَحُقُوقُ الْعَقْدِ تَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْآمِرِ لَا إِلَيْهِ. وَلَوْ قَالَ: عَنَيْتُ أَنْ لَا أَتَكَلَّمَ بِهِ لَمْ يُدَيَّنْ فِي الْقَضَاءِ خَاصَّةً، وَسَنُشِيرُ إِلَى الْمَعْنَى فِي الْفَرْقِ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى.
And whoever swears that he will not marry, or not divorce, or not manumit, then appoints a wakeel for that, he breaks his oath, because the wakeel in this matter is a messenger and an interpreter; therefore he does not attribute it to himself but to the commander, and the rights of the contract return to the commander, not to him. And if he says: 'I meant that I would not speak it,' he is not trusted in the judiciary specifically, and we will indicate the meaning in the difference, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents a contrasting ruling to the previous one. If someone swears not to marry, divorce, or manumit (free a slave), and then appoints a wakeel to do so, he does break his oath (hantha). The reason is that in these personal status acts—marriage, divorce, manumission—the wakeel is considered a mere messenger (safir) and expression conduit (mu'abbar). The act is legally attributed to the principal (the oath-taker), not to the agent. All rights and obligations flow to the principal. Therefore, the wakeel's action is treated as the oath-taker's own action. The text also adds a rule about interpretation: if the swearer claims he meant only the verbal utterance (i.e., he swore not to speak the words of divorce, etc.), that interpretation is not accepted in court (though it might be valid between him and Allah). This distinction is crucial for understanding the Hanafi position on agency in oaths: for financial transactions, the wakeel is a true agent; for personal status, he is a messenger.

Key Terms
سفير (safīr): A messenger or intermediary who merely conveys the principal's intention, without independent authority.
معبر (muʻabbir): An interpreter or expression-conduit; the wakeel simply gives effect to the principal's will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does appointing a wakeel to perform a divorce break the oath not to divorce, while appointing a wakeel to sell does not break the oath not to sell?
A) Because divorce is more severe than sale.
B) Because in divorce the wakeel is a messenger, while in sale he is a true agent.
C) Because the swearer can control the wakeel in divorce.
D) Because sale can be done by anyone.

Key Takeaway
In marriage, divorce, and manumission, the wakeel is a safir (messenger), so his action is attributed to the principal, breaking the oath.

Oath Not to Hit: Does Jest Excuse Pain?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on jesting painful acts breaking oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether jesting painful acts break an oath not to hit, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute with its tarjih.

Core Idea
Any act causing pain is hitting, even in jest—the reality of pain overrides intent.

A man swears not to hit his wife, but then pulls her hair, strangles her, or bites her in jest. Is his oath broken? The answer hinges on the definition of 'hitting' (darb). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi linguistic analysis and the dispute among later scholars, clarifying the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being considered (pulling hair, strangling, biting) and whether it constitutes a violation (hanth) of the oath not to hit.
The act must be examined for its effect—pain.
2
Define 'darb' (hitting) linguistically
Understand that in Hanafi legal language, 'darb' is defined as any act that causes pain (ilam), not limited to a specific type of blow.
This definition is key to the ruling.
3
Apply the definition to the act
If the act causes pain, it falls under darb, and thus breaches the oath. Even if done in jest, the pain is real.
Presence of pain is the decisive factor.
4
Consider the minority view (mashayikh)
Some later Hanafi scholars argue that in jest, the act is termed 'mumazaha' (play), not darb, so the oath stands.
This view relies on conventional naming over objective effect.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The author notes that the khulasa (summary) deems the first view correct. The mufta bihi is that the oath is broken even in jest.
The ruling is based on the illa (cause) of pain being present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اليمين في القتل والضرب و غيره
تشریح ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ اپنی بیوی کو نہیں ماروں گا، لیکن اس کے سر کے بال کھینچے یا اس کا گلا دبایا وغیرہ، حانث ہوگا یا نہیں
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَضْرِبُ امْرَأَتُهُ فَمَدَّ شَعْرَهَا أَوْ خَنَقَهَا أَوْعَضَّهَا حَنَتْ لِأَنَّهُ اِسْمٌ لِفِعْل مُؤْلِمٍ وَقَدْ تَحَقَّقَ الْإِيْلَامُ وَقِيلَ لَا يَحْنِتُ فِي حَالِ الْمُلَاعَبَةِ لِأَنَّهُ يُسَمَّى مُمَازَحَةً لَا ضَرْبًا
ترجمہ اگر کسی نے یہ قسم کھائی کہ میں اپنی بیوی کو نہیں ماروں گا لیکن اس کے سر کے بال کھینچے یا اس کا گلا دبایا یا دانت سے اس کا بدن دبا دیا تو حانث ہو جائے گا اگر چہ درستگی اور ہنسی مذاق کا موقع ہو کیونکہ مارنا ایک ایسے کام کا نام ہے جس سے تکلیف پہنچتی ہو اور ان تمام کاموں میں یہ دکھ پہنچانا پایا گیا۔ اور بعض مشائخ نے فرمایا ہے کہ اسی مذاق کی حالت میں حانث نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس حالت میں مار نہیں بلکہ دل لگی اور دل بستگی ہوتی ہے۔
فائدہ .... خلاصہ میں اس کو صحیح کہا ہے۔ تشریح ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
Chapter of oath in killing, hitting, etc. Commentary: The translation is clear. If someone swore not to hit his wife, but pulled her hair, strangled her, etc., will he be sinful? "And whoever swore not to hit his wife, then pulled her hair, or strangled her, or bit her, he becomes sinful (hanith) because it is a name for a painful act, and pain has been realized. And it is said he does not become sinful in the case of jest, because it is called play, not hitting." Translation: If someone swore not to hit his wife, but pulled her hair, strangled her, or bit her body with his teeth, he becomes sinful even if it was in earnest or jest, because hitting is a name for a painful act, and all these acts involve causing pain. And some mashayikh said he does not become sinful in the state of jest, because in that state it is not hitting but amusement and affection. Benefit: In the summary, it is considered correct. Commentary: The translation is clear.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage concerns the definition of 'hitting' (darb) in the context of oaths. The author states the general ruling: any act that causes pain (ilam) falls under striking, so pulling hair, strangling, or biting causes the oath to be broken (hanth). The proof is linguistic: the word 'darb' is a term for a painful action. A secondary view is mentioned, attributed to some mashayikh, that if the act is done in jest (mula'aba) it is not considered hitting but rather play (mumazaha), so the oath is not broken. However, the author notes that in the summary (khulasa) the first view is considered correct (sahih). The Hanafi relied-upon position is that even jesting acts cause hanth because the defining characteristic (pain) is present.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath or becoming sinful by failing to fulfill it.
ضرب (darb): Hitting; defined as any act causing pain, not limited to striking with an object.
ملاعبة (mula'aba): Playing or jesting, as opposed to serious intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view, if a man swears not to hit his wife and then pulls her hair in jest, is his oath broken?
A) Yes, because pulling hair causes pain and is considered hitting.
B) No, because it was in jest and not serious hitting.
C) Yes, because any contact with her body is hitting.
D) No, because the oath only covers serious hitting.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon view is that any act causing pain breaks an oath not to hit, regardless of whether the act is done in jest.

Oath to Kill a Dead Person: Conditions and Knowledge
Ruling on oath to kill a known dead person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for an oath on killing a dead person to be contracted and identify when hanth occurs based on knowledge.

Core Idea
An oath on a dead person is valid if divine power can conceive it, but hanth occurs immediately due to customary impossibility.

In the Hanafi school, oaths are not invalid simply because the action seems impossible. This lesson examines a specific case: swearing to kill a person who has already died. The ruling hinges on whether the oath-taker knew of the death. We'll see how the school balances divine omnipotence with human custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and condition
Recognize that the oath is conditional on killing a specific person, and that person is dead at the time of the oath.
The passage assumes the oath-taker says 'If I do not kill so-and-so, my wife is divorced.'
2
Distinguish knowledge vs ignorance of death
The ruling differs based on whether the oath-taker knew the person was dead. Knowledge affects whether the oath is contracted and whether hanth occurs.
This distinction is the key point of the passage.
3
Explain why the oath is contracted if the person is known dead
Even though the person is dead, it is conceivable that Allah could revive them. Therefore, the oath is contracted (mun'akid).
Hanafis consider the possibility of divine intervention; nothing is absolutely impossible for Allah.
4
Explain immediate hanth due to customary inability
Although the oath is contracted, the oath-taker becomes sinful (hanith) immediately because, customarily, no one can kill a dead person. This is 'ajz 'adi (customary inability).
Customary inability triggers hanth at the moment the oath is made.
5
Identify consequence when death is unknown
If the oath-taker did not know the person was dead, the oath is not contracted. The basis of the oath (the person's life) is non-existent, so no hanth occurs.
This is the relied-upon view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).

وہ شخص پہلے ہی مر چکا ہے۔ اور کہنے والا خود بھی یہ بات جانتا ہے تو وہ حانث ہو جائے گا اور بیوی مطلقہ ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس نے اپنی قسم کو اس مردہ کی ایسی زندگی پر قائم کیا ہے جس کو اللہ تعالی اس مردہ میں پیدا کرے اور اس کا تصور بھی کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ اس لئے قسم منعقد ہو جائے گی اور اسی وقت حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ عادتا اس سے عاجزی ظاہر ہے۔ف۔ معلوم ہونا چاہئے کہ جس پیالہ میں پانی نہ ہو اگر اس کی طرف اشارہ کر کے کوئی یہ کہے کہ اس میں جو پانی ہے وہ میں پیوں گا۔ تو امام ابوحنیفہ محمد کے نزدیک قسم منعقد ہی نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ قسم کے وقت اس میں مطلق پانی نہیں ہے۔ اور اگر آئندہ اس میں اللہ تعالی پانی موجود کر دے تو ممکن بھی ہے۔ لیکن یہ وہ پانی نہ ہو گا جس کے بارے میں اس نے قسم کھائی ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ پانی قسم کے بعد پیدا ہوا ہے۔ اور امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ان کے نزدیک یہ شرط نہیں کہ قسم پوری ہونے کا امکان بھی ہو۔ جیسا کہ اکل وشرب کے باب میں گذر چکا ہے۔ اور یہاں آدمی کے قتل کرنے کی قسم کا تصور اس طرح ہو سکتا ہے کہ اگر اللہ تعالیٰ اس کو زندہ کر دے تو یقتل کر سکتا ہے۔ اس لئے قسم منعقد ہوگی۔ لیکن عادت الہی ایسی جاری نہیں ہے۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ عادت کے لحاظ سے یہ شخص اس . کے قتل کرنے سے عاجز ہے۔ حالانکہ قسم کے وقت یہ جانتا تھا کہ وہ آدمی مر چکا ہے۔ لہذا حانث ہو جائے گا۔ وان لــم يـعـلـم لا يـحــث .. الخ ۔ ترجمہ سے مطلب واضح ہے۔
If someone swore 'If I do not kill so-and-so, my wife is divorced' and so-and-so was dead and the oath-taker knew it, he becomes sinful. And whoever says 'If I do not kill so-and-so, my wife is divorced' and so-and-so is dead and he knows it, he becomes sinful because he based his oath on a life that Allah could create in him, which is conceivable, so the oath is contracted. Then he becomes sinful due to customary inability. And if he did not know, he does not become sinful because he based his oath on a life that was in him, which is not conceivable. So it becomes analogous to the case of the cup, with the same difference of opinion, and there is no detailed distinction in that case, and that is the correct view. Translation: ... (Urdu commentary elaborates the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf). Commentary: 'From man qala...' If someone says about another 'If I do not kill him, my wife is divorced' while that person has already died, and the speaker knows it, he becomes sinful and his wife becomes divorced. Because he based his oath on a life that Allah could create in the dead, which is conceivable. So the oath is contracted and he immediately becomes sinful due to customary inability. It should be known that if someone points to a cup without water and says 'I will drink the water in it', according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad the oath is not contracted because there is absolutely no water at the time. If Allah later creates water, it is possible, but that water is not the one he swore about because it was created after the oath. According to Abu Yusuf, he becomes sinful because in his view it is not a condition that fulfillment be possible. As passed in the chapter of eating and drinking. Here, the oath to kill a person can be conceived if Allah revives him, so the oath is contracted. But Allah's habit is not such. So it is clear that he is unable to kill him customarily, even though he knew he was dead. So he becomes sinful. 'And if he did not know, he does not become sinful...' The translation makes the meaning clear.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath conditional on killing a person who is already dead. The ruling distinguishes between knowledge and ignorance of the death. If the oath-taker knows the person is dead, the oath is valid (mun'akid) because it is conceivable for Allah to revive the dead, and he immediately becomes sinful (hanith) because he is customarily unable to fulfill it. If he did not know, according to the relied-upon view he does not become sinful because his oath was based on a life that no longer exists, making fulfillment inconceivable. However, Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, analogizing to the case of a cup without water: if someone swears to drink water from a cup that actually has no water, Abu Yusuf holds the oath is broken even if he knows it's empty, because possibility is not a condition. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the oath is not contracted. The author notes that in this case of killing a dead person, the analogy leads to the same difference, but the correct view is the detailed one (knowledge vs. ignorance).

Key Terms
عقد اليمين (aqd al-yamin): The formation of an oath; it is contracted when the condition is conceivable.
العجز العادي (al-'ajz al-'adi): Customary inability, i.e., impossible under normal circumstances, though divinely possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if a man swears to kill a person who is already dead, and he does not know of the death, is his oath broken?
A) Yes, because his wife becomes divorced immediately.
B) No, because he did not know, so the oath is not contracted.
C) Yes, because he should have known.
D) No, because killing a dead person is impossible absolutely.

Key Takeaway
An oath to kill a dead person is valid if the oath-taker knew the person was dead; if unaware, no hanth occurs.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute: The Cup Analogy and the Role of Possibility
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath to kill a dead person via cup analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on oaths involving impossible acts and evaluate the cup analogy's role in the dispute.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf holds that possibility is not a condition for the oath, so hanth occurs even when the action is impossible; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad disagree.

The previous lesson established the general ruling. Now we delve into the internal Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Yusuf challenges the distinction based on knowledge, arguing via the analogy of a cup without water. This lesson unpacks that analogy, the opposing positions, and the correct (mufta bihi) view.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two positions from the general ruling
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: if unaware, no hanth. Abu Yusuf: hanth even if unaware.
The difference hinges on whether possibility of fulfillment is a condition.
2
Introduce the cup analogy (asl)
The cup case: someone swears to drink water from a cup that is actually empty. Abu Yusuf says hanth occurs even if he knows it's empty; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the oath is not contracted.
The cup case is the original case (asl) for the qiyas.
3
Apply the analogy to the dead person case (far')
The dead person case is analogous: both involve an impossible action at the time of the oath. Abu Yusuf rules hanth in both; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule no hanth if unaware.
The shared illa is the impossibility of the action at the time of the oath.
4
Identify the disagreement on the role of knowledge
Abu Yusuf sees no need for knowledge: the action is impossible regardless, so hanth. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad distinguish: if the oath-taker knows of the impossibility, the oath is contracted (conceivable) but hanth immediate; if unaware, the oath is not contracted.
This reflects a deeper difference in the definition of aqd al-yamin.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The passage states that the detailed view (knowledge vs ignorance) is correct. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Thus, the cup analogy does not overrule the distinction; rather, the same distinction applies to the cup case, per the correct view.

So it becomes analogous to the case of the cup, with the same difference of opinion, and there is no detailed distinction in that case, and that is the correct view. Translation: ... If someone says about another 'If I do not kill him, my wife is divorced' while that person has already died, and the speaker knows it, he becomes sinful and his wife becomes divorced. Because he based his oath on a life that Allah could create in the dead, which is conceivable. So the oath is contracted and he immediately becomes sinful due to customary inability. It should be known that if someone points to a cup without water and says 'I will drink the water in it', according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad the oath is not contracted because there is absolutely no water at the time. If Allah later creates water, it is possible, but that water is not the one he swore about because it was created after the oath. According to Abu Yusuf, he becomes sinful because in his view it is not a condition that fulfillment be possible. As passed in the chapter of eating and drinking. Here, the oath to kill a person can be conceived if Allah revives him, so the oath is contracted. But Allah's habit is not such. So it is clear that he is unable to kill him customarily, even though he knew he was dead. So he becomes sinful. 'And if he did not know, he does not become sinful...' The translation makes the meaning clear.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath conditional on killing a person who is already dead. The ruling distinguishes between knowledge and ignorance of the death. If the oath-taker knows the person is dead, the oath is valid (mun'akid) because it is conceivable for Allah to revive the dead, and he immediately becomes sinful (hanith) because he is customarily unable to fulfill it. If he did not know, according to the relied-upon view he does not become sinful because his oath was based on a life that no longer exists, making fulfillment inconceivable. However, Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, analogizing to the case of a cup without water: if someone swears to drink water from a cup that actually has no water, Abu Yusuf holds the oath is broken even if he knows it's empty, because possibility is not a condition. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the oath is not contracted. The author notes that in this case of killing a dead person, the analogy leads to the same difference, but the correct view is the detailed one (knowledge vs. ignorance).

Key Terms
قضية الكوز (qadiyyat al-kooz): The case of the cup: an analogy used in oaths to compare impossible actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the intra-Hanafi dispute, which imam holds that hanth occurs even if the oath-taker was unaware that the person was dead?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Muhammad al-Shaybani
C) Abu Yusuf
D) All three agree

Key Takeaway
The correct Hanafi view distinguishes based on knowledge; Abu Yusuf's blanket hanth is not mufta bihi.

Swearing to Kill a Dead Person: The Cup Analogy
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath validity with impossible object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on oaths involving non-existent objects and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a new scenario.

Core Idea
An oath on an impossible act can be contracted if theoretical possibility exists, but violation is immediate due to custom.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah examines a nuanced case: a person swears to kill someone he knows is already dead. The ruling hinges on whether the oath can be 'contracted' (mun'aqid) when the object of the oath is currently non-existent. The Hanafi masters differ, and the author applies the view that the oath is valid but violated immediately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Legal Issue
Read the passage: a man swears to kill a person who is already dead, and he knows that fact. The central question is whether the oath is legally contracted (mun'aqid) and what consequences follow.
The author first states the final ruling: he becomes hanith immediately, and if the oath was for divorce, his wife is divorced.
2
Map the Intra-Hanafi Positions
Identify Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's position: the oath is not contracted because the object (the deceased) does not exist at the time of swearing, and any future existence (resurrection) would be a different entity. Then identify Abu Yusuf's position: the oath is contracted because theoretical possibility suffices, but due to custom he is incapable, so hanith at once.
Use the cup analogy: pointing to a cup with no water and swearing to drink its water.
3
Analyze the Dalil (Rational Evidence)
Understand the cup analogy: for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the water that may come later is not the same water referenced in the oath, so the oath is invalid. For Abu Yusuf, possibility of fulfillment is not a condition; the oath is valid even if the object may exist later.
The author applies Abu Yusuf's reasoning to the dead person: resurrection is theoretically possible, so oath is contracted, but customarily impossible, so immediate hanith.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, the oath has no legal effect (no divorce, no kaffarah). Under Abu Yusuf, the oath is contracted and he immediately becomes hanith, triggering the consequence (e.g., divorce or need for kaffarah).
The author sides with Abu Yusuf here.
5
Consider the Ignorance Exception
Note the final clause: 'if he did not know [the person was dead], he does not become hanith.' This shows that knowledge of impossibility affects liability.
This is a key nuance: ignorance of death means no violation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter of oath regarding demanding dirhams. [Page 124] Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary, Volume 6. That person has already died. And the speaker himself knows this, then he becomes hanith (oath-breaker) and his wife becomes divorced. Because he based his oath on such life of that dead person which Allah would create in that dead person, and it can be imagined. Therefore the oath is contracted, and at that moment he becomes hanith. Because customarily he is incapable of it. It should be known that if someone points to a cup that has no water and says, 'I will drink the water that is in it', then according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the oath is not contracted at all. Because at the time of the oath there is absolutely no water in it. And if in the future Allah creates water in it, that is possible. But that will not be the water about which he swore, because that water came into existence after the oath. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, he becomes hanith, because according to him it is not a condition that fulfillment of the oath be possible. As has passed in the chapter of eating and drinking. And here, the oath of killing a person can be imagined such that if Allah resurrects him, he can kill him. Therefore the oath is contracted. But the habit of Allah is not such. From this it is clear that customarily this person is incapable of killing him, even though at the time of the oath he knew that the person had died. Therefore he becomes hanith. And if he did not know, he does not become hanith. Etc. The meaning is clear from the translation.

Commentary
Lesson Type: Intra-Hanafi Dispute. This passage opens with the continuation of a ruling concerning an oath to kill a person who is already dead. The author first states the final ruling: if the swearer knows the person is dead, he immediately becomes hanith (oath-violator) and, if it was an oath of divorce, his wife is divorced. The reasoning is that the oath was linked to a possible future event (Allah's resurrection of the dead), so the oath is contracted, but by custom he is incapable, so he violates it immediately. The passage then uses the analogy of a cup with no water to illustrate the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad on one side, and Imam Abu Yusuf on the other. The former hold that the oath is not contracted at all because there is no water at the time of the oath, and any future water would be a different entity. The latter holds that the oath is contracted and he becomes hanith because possibility of fulfillment is not a condition. The author seems to prefer Abu Yusuf's view in the context of killing a dead person, as the oath is contracted due to theoretical possibility, but custom dictates immediate violation. The phrase 'وإن لم يعلم لا يحنث' clarifies that if the swearer was ignorant of the death, he does not become hanith.

Key Terms
حانث (hanith): A person who violates his oath by failing to fulfill what he swore to do.
منعقد (mun'aqid): Contracted; used to describe an oath that is legally valid and binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He becomes hanith immediately.
C) He is required to wait until the person is resurrected.
D) He must expiate without being hanith.

Key Takeaway
An oath on a theoretically possible but customarily impossible act is contracted, and the swearer becomes hanith immediately if he knows of the impossibility.

Oath Using Exception: Interpreting 'Except One Hundred'
Ruling on an oath conditional on not having more than one hundred dirhams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an oath uses an exception like 'except one hundred dirhams' and explain the customary interpretation.

Core Idea
In an oath, 'except one hundred' means 'not more than one hundred'; having less does not break the oath.

Oaths often use exceptions (istithna) that can be tricky to interpret. The Hanafi school looks at customary usage ('urf) to understand what the speaker intended. This lesson examines a specific case: a man swears that if he has 'except one hundred dirhams', his wife is divorced. What if he only has fifty?

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The condition is 'if I have except one hundred dirhams'. This is a negative condition using istithna (exception).
The phrase means 'if I do not have more than one hundred dirhams' by custom.
2
Check the actual situation
The man owns fifty dirhams, which is less than one hundred.
3
Apply the customary meaning
The intended meaning of the exception is to negate ownership of more than one hundred. Since fifty is less than one hundred, the condition is not fulfilled.
The exception of one hundred includes all its parts, so fifty is also excepted.
4
State the ruling
The oath is not broken (lam yahnith) because the condition was not triggered.
This applies even if he had zero dirhams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ إِنْ كَانَ لِى إِلَّا مِائَةُ دِرْهَم فَامْرَأَتُهُ طَالِقٌ فَلَمْ يَمْلِكُ إِلَّا خَمْسِينَ دِرْهَما لَمْ يَحْنِثُ لِاَنَّ الْمَقْصُوْدَ مِنْهُ عُرْفًا نَفْئُ مَازَادَ عَلَى الْمِانَةِ وَلَانَّ اسْتِثْنَاءَ الْمِائَةِ اِسْتِثْنَاؤُهَا بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهَا وَكَذَالِكَ لَوْقَالَ غَيْرُ مِائَةٍ أَوْسِوى مِائَةٍ لَانَّ كُلَّ ذَالِكَ آدَاةُ الْإِسْتِثْنَاءِ
And whoever says: 'If I have except one hundred dirhams, then my wife is divorced' and he does not own except fifty dirhams, he does not break his oath, because the intended meaning customarily is the negation of what is more than one hundred, and because the exception of one hundred is the exception of all its parts. Similarly, if he said 'other than one hundred' or 'besides one hundred', because all of these are tools of exception.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage explains an oath that uses an exception (istithna). If a person says 'If I have except one hundred dirhams, my wife is divorced,' he means he does not have more than one hundred. So if he actually has only fifty, the condition is not fulfilled, and the oath is not broken. The reason is that the phrase is understood in custom as negating anything above one hundred. Moreover, the exception of one hundred includes all its parts, so fifty is also excepted. The same rule applies if he uses 'other than' or 'besides' because these are all particles of exception.

Key Terms
اسْتِثْنَاء (istithnāʾ): Exception; a grammatical construction that excludes something from a general statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The wife is not divorced because he owns more than one hundred.
B) The wife is divorced because he owns more than one hundred.
C) The wife is divorced because he owns exactly one hundred.
D) The wife is not divorced because he owns less than one hundred.

Key Takeaway
In an oath with an exception like 'except one hundred', custom interprets it as negating more than that amount, so having less does not break the oath.

Intercourse with Thrice-Divorced Wife During ʿIddah: Hadd and the Role of Knowledge vs. Assumption
Ruling with proof on hadd for intercourse with thrice-divorced wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intercourse with a thrice-divorced wife during ʿiddah and explain why hadd is applied or dropped based on the man’s knowledge or assumption.

Core Idea
Knowledge of prohibition triggers hadd; assumption of halal due to lingering effects negates hadd.

This lesson examines a critical distinction in Hanafi criminal law: when does intercourse with a thrice-divorced wife during her ʿiddah incur the hadd punishment? The answer hinges on the man’s state of mind. If he knew the act was haram, hadd is wajib because no shubha remains. But if he assumed it was halal based on remaining marriage effects (like lineage, confinement, or maintenance), his assumption is legally valid and drops the hadd. The same principle extends to analogous cases such as freed umm walad, khulʿ, and divorce for compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two scenarios
Pinpoint the act: intercourse with a thrice-divorced wife during her ʿiddah. Note the two contrasting scenarios: (a) the man says ‘I knew it was haram’ vs. (b) he says ‘I assumed it was halal’.
The ruling depends entirely on the man's claim about his knowledge or assumption.
2
Locate the dalil and its wajh al-dalalah
The proof comes from the Quran (2:230) which categorically states that after three divorces, the woman is not halal until she marries another. This is a qatʿi text. Ijma further confirms the prohibition.
The text calls opposing views mere khilaf (opposition) not valid ikhtilaf.
3
Analyse the role of shubha and residual effects
In the second scenario, the man’s assumption is given legal weight because some effects of the marriage persist: lineage is established, she must stay in confinement, and maintenance is due. This creates a shubha ḥukmiyyah that justifies dropping the hadd.
The key is that the assumption is ‘in its proper place’ – it is not baseless ignorance.
4
Apply the analogy to similar cases
The text extends the ruling to a freed umm walad (slave-mother), a woman divorced by khulʿ, and a woman divorced for compensation. In all these cases, the prohibition is established by ijma, yet some marriage effects remain during ʿiddah, so the same logic applies.
These cases are treated like the thrice-divorced woman due to the combination of absolute prohibition and lingering effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ طَلَّقَ امْرَأَتَهُ ثَلَاثًا ثُمَّ وَطِيَهَا فِي الْعِدَّةِ وَقَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ حُدَّ لِزَوَالِ الْمِلْكِ الْمُحَلَّلِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ فَتَكُونُ الشَّبْهَةُ مُنْتَفِيَةٌ وَقَدْ نَطَقَ الْكِتَابُ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْحِلِّ وَعَلَى ذَلِكَ الْإِجْمَاعُ وَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ قَوْلُ الْمُخَالِفِ فِيهِ لِأَنَّهُ خِلَافٌ لَا اخْتِلَافٌ وَلَوْ قَالَ ظَنَنْتُ أَنَّهَا تَحِلُّ لِي لَا يُحَدُّ لِأَنَّ الظَّنَّ فِي مَوْضِعِهِ لِأَنَّ أَثَرَ الْمِلْكِ قَائِمٌ فِي حَقِّ النَّسَبِ وَالْحَبْسِ وَالنَّفَقَةِ فَاعْتُبِرَ ظَنُّهُ فِي إِسْقَاطِ الْحَدِّ وَأُمُّ الْوَلَدِ إِذَا اعْتَقَهَا مَوْلَاهَا وَالْمُخْتَلَعَةُ وَالْمُطَلَّقَةُ عَلَى مَالٍ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمُطَلَّقَةِ الثَّالِثِ لِثُبُوتِ الْحُرْمَةِ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ وَقِيَامِ بَعْضِ الْآثَارِ فِي الْعِدَّةِ ترجمہ: اگر ایک شخص نے اپنی بیوی کو تین طلاقیں دیں پھر عدت ہی میں اس سے وطی کرلی اور یہ کہا کہ میں یہ جانتا تھا کہ یہ مجھ پر حرام ہے تو اس پر حد جاری کی جائے گی (یعنی اگر وہ محصن ہے تو رجم کیا جائے گا ورنہ سو کوڑے مارے جائیں گے) کیونکہ اس مرد کو اسے حلال کرنے کی ملکیت جو نکاح کی وجہ سے تھی وہ مکمل ختم ہو چکی ہے۔ اس لئے کسی طرح کا شبہ باقی نہیں رہا۔ اور قرآن پاک نے اس کے حلال ہونے کی صراحت کے ساتھ انفی کر دی ہے اور اس بات پر تمام علماء کا اجماع ہو چکا ہے اگر کوئی شخص اس میں اختلاف کرے گا تو اس کی بات کا کسی طرح بھی اعتبار نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس طرح کہنے کو خلاف کہہ سکتے ہیں اور اختلاف نہیں کیا جاسکتا ہے اور اگر وطی کرنے والے نے یہ کہا کہ میرے خیال میں وہ میرے لئے حلال ہے۔ تو اس پر حد نہیں جاری کی جائے گی کیونکہ اس کا وہ گمان اپنے موقع میں ہے (بے موقع نہیں ہے) کیونکہ ملکیت نکاح کا اثر اس پر ابھی تک باقی ہے۔ مثلاً اگر عدت کے اندر اسے بچہ پیدا ہو جائے تو اسی مرد سے اس کا نسب ثابت ہوگا۔ اور اس عدت کے دنوں میں اسے نکلنے کی ممانعت ہے اور شوہر پر اس کا نفقہ بھی واجب ہے۔ اس لئے حد ساقط ہونے میں بھی اس کے خیال و گمان کا اعتبار کیا جائے گا اور اگر ام ولد کو اس کے مولی نے آزاد کر دیا یا بیوی سے خلع کر لیا۔ یا مال کے عوض بیوی کو طلاق دی۔ پھر عدت کے دنوں میں اس سے وطی کرلی تو ان کا حکم بھی تین طلاقیں پانے والی کے برابر ہے۔ کیونکہ اس عورت کا حرام ہو جانا اجماع سے ثابت ہے اور عدت کے دنوں میں ملکیت نکاح باقی رہنے کی کچھ علامتیں باقی رہتی ہیں۔
And whoever divorces his wife three times, then has intercourse with her during the ʿiddah and says 'I knew that she was haram for me', he is to be flogged/styled, because the property that made her halal is completely removed from every aspect, thus shubha is negated. The Book has spoken decisively about the negation of halal, and there is ijma on that. The statement of an opponent is not considered, for it is khilaf (mere opposition) not ikhtilaf (valid disagreement). And if he says 'I assumed that she was halal for me', he is not to be flogged, because his assumption is in its proper place, since the effect of ownership remains regarding lineage, confinement, and maintenance. So his assumption is considered in dropping the hadd. And a slave-mother if her master frees her, and a woman divorced by khul, and a woman divorced for compensation are like the thrice-divorced woman due to the establishment of prohibition by ijma and the existence of some effects during the ʿiddah. Translation: If a man gives his wife three divorces, then has intercourse with her during the ʿiddah and says 'I knew she was haram for me', then hadd is imposed (if he is muhsan, he is to be stoned; otherwise, one hundred lashes). Because the ownership that made her halal due to marriage is completely gone, so no shubha remains. The Quran has explicitly negated its permissibility, and there is consensus of all scholars on this. If someone disagrees, his statement is not considered at all, because such a saying can be called khilaf but not ikhtilaf. And if the one who had intercourse says 'I thought she was halal for me', then hadd is not imposed because his assumption is in its proper place (not out of place), since the effect of marriage ownership still remains. For example, if a child is born during the ʿiddah, lineage is established from this man, she is prohibited from leaving the house, and maintenance is obligatory on the husband. Therefore, his assumption is considered in dropping the hadd. And if an umm walad is freed by her master, or a woman is divorced by khul, or a woman is divorced for compensation, then their ruling is the same as the thrice-divorced woman. Because the prohibition of this woman is established by ijma, and some signs of marriage ownership remain during the ʿiddah.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding intercourse with a thrice-divorced wife during her ʿiddah. The text distinguishes two scenarios: if the man knew intercourse was haram, he incurs the hadd punishment because there is no shubha—the marriage is completely dissolved, and the prohibition is absolute by Quran and ijma. If he assumed it was halal, hadd is not imposed because some effects of the marriage remain (lineage, seclusion, maintenance), creating a valid shubha. Similar rulings apply to freed umm walad, khul divorce, and divorce for compensation, as they also involve a complete prohibition with lingering effects during ʿiddah. The text emphasizes that the assumed permission is reasonable in these cases, so it is treated as shubha.

Key Terms
عدت (ʿiddah): The waiting period after divorce during which the woman cannot remarry and some marriage effects remain.
شبهة حكمية (shubha ḥukmiyyah): A legal doubt arising from a valid evidence or authority, such as the existence of some marriage effects.
خلاف لا اختلاف (khilaf la ikhtilaf): Mere opposition without valid scholarly evidence, which carries no legal weight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lesson, why is hadd not imposed when the man says 'I assumed she was halal for me' after thrice divorce?
A) Because the prohibition is not clearly established in the Quran.
B) Because residual effects of marriage (lineage, confinement, maintenance) create a valid shubha.
C) Because the woman must also testify that she believed it was halal.
D) Because the hadd penalty only applies to non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the application of hadd for intercourse with a thrice-divorced wife depends on the man's knowledge: knowledge of prohibition triggers hadd, but a reasonable assumption of halal based on lingering marriage effects negates it.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
No Ḥadd for Kināyah Divorce Intercourse: Role of Ṣaḥābah Ikhtilāf
Ruling on intercourse after kināyah divorce due to Ṣaḥābah ikhtilāf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not apply ḥadd punishment for intercourse during ʿiddah after a kināyah divorce, and identify the role of ikhtilāf al-ṣaḥābah as a source of shubha.

Core Idea
Disagreement among the Companions creates shubha that averts ḥadd, even if the prohibition is known.

In the previous lesson, we saw that for an explicit (ṣarīḥ) thrice divorce, ḥadd applies if the husband knowingly commits intercourse during ʿiddah. But the same does not hold for kināyah divorce. In this passage, Al-Marghinani explains that the disagreement of the Ṣaḥābah—particularly ʿUmar—on the effect of kināyah expressions creates a legally recognized shubha (doubt) that blocks the ḥadd punishment. This illustrates a key Hanafi principle: valid ikhtilāf among the Companions can negate penal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the exception
What is the general rule for ḥadd when a man commits intercourse with a woman who is prohibited to him? Here, the exception is that no ḥadd is applied due to the disagreement of the Companions.
Contrast with the earlier ruling on explicit thrice divorce.
2
Pinpoint the source of shubha
The shubha arises from the ikhtilāf of the Ṣaḥābah on whether the kināyah expression actually effects a divorce, and if so, how many. The text cites ʿUmar's view that it is one revocable divorce.
This disagreement creates legal doubt.
3
Examine the opposing view
Potential counter-argument: if the man admits he knew intercourse was haram, then his knowledge should eliminate any doubt, so ḥadd should apply.
This is a stronger form of shubha, not based on ignorance.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafis hold that the ikhtilāf itself is an objective source of shubha, regardless of the man's subjective knowledge. The doubt is in the legal status of the divorce, not in the man's awareness.
Thus, the shubha remains.
5
Apply to other kināyah expressions and intentions
The same ruling applies to all kināyah expressions. Even if the husband intended three divorces by the kināyah, the disagreement exists and so the ḥadd is still averted.
The ikhtilāf is about the effect of kināyah itself, not the number intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طلاق کنائی کی عدت میں وطی کرنے سے حد کا حکم وَلَوْ قَالَ لَهَا أَنْتِ خَلِيَّةٌ أَوْ بَرِيَّةٌ أَوْ أَمْرُكِ بِيَدِكِ فَاخْتَارَتْ نَفْسَهَا ثُمَّ وَطِيَهَا فِي الْعِدَّةِ وَقَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ لَمْ يُحَدَّ لِاخْتِلَافِ الصَّحَابَةِ فِيهِ فَمِنْ مَذْهَبِ عُمَرَ أَنَّهَا تَطْلِيقَةٌ رَجْعِيَّةٌ وَكَذَا الْجَوَابُ فِي سَائِرِ الْكِنَايَاتِ وَكَذَا إِذَا نَوَى ثَلَاثًا لِقِيَامِ الْإِخْتِلَافِ مَعَ ذَلِكَ ترجمہ: اور اگر اپنی بیوی سے کہا کہ تم خلیہ ہو یا تم بریہ ہو یا یہ کہ تمہارا اختیار تمہارے ہاتھ میں ہے۔ اس کہنے پر بیوی نے اپنے نفس کو اختیار میں کر لیا تو ان تمام صورتوں میں کنائی طلاق واقع ہو گئی۔ اس کے باوجود اس کے شوہر نے اس کی عدت کے دنوں میں اس سے وطی کر لی اور یہ کہا کہ میں جانتا تھا کہ یہ مجھ پر حرام ہوگئی ہے۔ تو بھی اسے حد نہیں ماری جائے گی۔ کیونکہ صحابہ کرام رضی اللہ عنہم کا اس میں اختلاف ہے چنانچہ حضرت عمر رضی اللہ عنہ کا اس مسئلہ میں یہ مذہب ہے کہ کنایات سے طلاق واقع ہوتی ہے۔ اور دوسری کنایہ طلاقوں میں بھی یہی حکم ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر کنایہ طلاق سے تین طلاقیں ہونے کی نیت کی ہو کیونکہ اس صورت میں بھی اختلاف ہی ہے۔
The ruling on intercourse during ʿiddah after kināyah divorce: And if he said to her 'You are khalliyah' or 'bariyyah' or 'your affair is in your hand' and she chose herself, then he had intercourse with her during the ʿiddah and said 'I knew that she was haram for me', he is not to be flogged due to the disagreement of the Ṣaḥābah in it. From the madhhab of ʿUmar (r.a.) is that it is a single revocable divorce. And similar is the answer for all other kināyah expressions. And likewise if he intended three divorces, because disagreement exists alongside that. Translation: If a man said to his wife 'you are khalliyah' or 'bariyyah' or 'your affair is in your hand', and upon that the wife chose herself, then in all these cases a kināyah divorce occurred. Despite that, if the husband had intercourse with her during the ʿiddah and said 'I knew that she had become haram for me', still he will not be flogged. Because the Ṣaḥābah differed on this. For example, Hadrat ʿUmar's opinion (r.a.) is that with kināyah, one revocable divorce occurs. And the same ruling applies to other kināyah divorces. Similarly, if he intended three divorces by the kināyah, because difference still exists.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage deals with a ruling on intercourse after a kināyah (allusive) divorce during the ʿiddah. Unlike the explicit (ṣarīḥ) divorce, kināyah expressions (like 'you are free' etc.) are open to interpretation. Because the Ṣaḥābah (including ʿUmar) differed on whether it counts as one revocable divorce or more, this disagreement creates a shubha that averts the ḥadd punishment. The same applies if the husband intended three divorces, as the ikhtilāf remains. Therefore, even if the man knew the prohibition, the existence of this valid difference among the Companions is sufficient to negate ḥadd.

Key Terms
طلاق كناية (ṭalāq kināyah): Allusive divorce using words that imply divorce but require intention or context.
اختلاف الصحابة (ikhtilāf al-ṣaḥābah): Difference of opinion among the Companions of the Prophet, considered a source of shubha that averts ḥadd.
شبهة (shubha): Legal doubt that prevents application of ḥadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is ḥadd not applied in the case of kināyah divorce even if the man knew it was haram?
A) Because kināyah divorce is not valid
B) Because the Ṣaḥābah disagreed on its effect, creating shubha
C) Because the woman consented
D) Because the ʿiddah had not ended

Key Takeaway
The ikhtilāf of the Companions creates an objective shubha that averts ḥadd even when the perpetrator knows the act is prohibited.
